بشرط ويقال للبائع اقطعها وسلم المبيع ومن باع ثمرة بدا صلاحها أولا صح
ويقطعها مقتصراً، وإن زاد بكل قليل وكثير لم يدخلا فيهما على عكس الزرع والثمار. وفي المعراج : وقوله بكل كثير وقليل يذكر على وجه المبالغة في إسقاط حق البائع عن المبيع، أما الثمر المجدود والزرع المحصود فيها فلا يدخلان إلا بالتنصيص. وفي الخانية : ولو اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار عليها ثمار وقال في البيع بثمارها فأكل البائع الثمار سقطت حصة الثمار من الثمن. وهل يخير المشتري في أخذ الباقي ؟ ذكر في البيوع أنه يخير إن شاء أخذ الباقي بما بقي من الثمن وإن شاء ترك. وذكر في بعض الكتب أنه لا يخير في قول أبي حنيفة كما لو اشترى شاة بعشرة فولدت عند البائع ولداً قيمته خمسة فأكله البائع قال أبو حنيفة : تلزمه الشاة بخمسة ولا خيار له. والصحيح أنه يخير في مسألة الثمار لأن الثمر صار مبيعاً مقصوداً، فإذا أكل البائع تفرقت الصفقة عليه فيخير اهـ. وفي القنية : اشترى أرضاً مع الزرع فأدرك الزرع في يده ثم تقايلا لا تجوز الإقالة لأن العقد إنما ورد على القصيل دون الحنطة. ولو حصد المشتري الزرع ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة بحصتها من الثمن، ولو اشترى أرضاً فيها أشجار فقطعها ثم تقايلا صحت الإقالة بجميع الثمن ولا شيء للبائع من قيمة الأشجار وتسلم الأشجار إلى المشتري هذا إذا علم البائع بقطع الأشجار، وإذا لم يعلم به وقت الإقالة يخير إن شاء أخذها بجميع الثمن، وإن شاء ترك اهـ. قوله : (ويقال للبائع اقطعها وسلم المبيع) أي في الصورتين والمراد بالمبيع الأرض والشجر. وقيده في الخانية بأن ينقد الثمن إليه لأن ملك المشتري مشغول بملك البائع فكان عليه تفريغه وتسليمه كما إذا كان فيها متاع. قيد بالمبيع لأن المدة إذا انقضت في الإجارة وفي الأرض زرع فإن المستأجر لا يؤمر بقلع زرعه وإنما يبقى بأجر المثل إلى انتهائه لأنها للانتفاع وذلك بالترك دون القلع بخلاف الشراء لأنه ملك الرقبة فلا يراعى فيه إمكان الانتفاع ولأن التسليم وإن وجب عليه فارغة لكن تسليم العوض تسليم للمعوض فافترقا فلا يقاس البيع على الإجارة كما هو مذهب الثلاثة . الاختيار : ولو باع قطناً في فراش فعلى البائع فتقه لأن عليه تسليمه، أما جذاذ الثمرة وقطع الرطبة وقلع الجزر والبصل وأمثاله على المشتري لا البائع لأنه يعمل في ملكه وللعرف اهـ. وفي القنية : اشترى ثمار الكرم والأشجار وهي عليها يتم تسليمها بالتخلية وإن كانت متصلة بملك البائع كالمشاع بخلاف الهبة. ولو باع قطناً في فراش أو حنطة في سنبل وسلم كذلك لم يصح إذ لم يمكنه القبض إلا بالفتق والدق يصح تسليم دار فيها متاع لغير المشتري وأرض
وفي
قوله : (أما الثمر المجدود) يعني ما مر من التفصيل في الألفاظ الثلاثة في المتصل بالأرض والشجر كما في الفتح. وفيه أيضاً. والمجدود بدالين مهملتين ومعجمتين بمعنى أي المقطوع غير أن المهملتين هنا أولى ليناسب المحصود .اهـ. قوله : ( أي ظهر صلاحها قال الرملي : هو تفسير لقوله «بدا». قوله : وصحة البيع على هذا التقدير بناء الخ قال في النهر : حاصله أن الاستدلال بتلك الإشارة لا تم
With a condition, and it is said to the seller: cut them and hand over the sold item, and whoever sells fruit after its ripeness appears or before is valid. And he cuts it being limited, and if he increased with every little and much, they do not enter into them contrary to the crop and fruits. And in al-Mi'raj: And his statement 'with every much and little' is mentioned for exaggeration in dropping the seller's right over the sold item. As for the cut fruit and the harvested crop in it, they do not enter except by specification. And in al-Khaniyah: If he bought land in which there are trees with fruits and he said in the sale 'with their fruits', then the seller ate the fruits, the share of the fruits falls from the price. And is the buyer given the option to take the remainder? It is mentioned in al-Buyu' that he is given the option: if he wishes he takes the remainder with what remains of the price, and if he wishes he leaves. And it is mentioned in some books that he is not given an option according to Abu Hanifa, like if he bought a sheep for ten and it gave birth at the seller's a lamb worth five, and the seller ate it; Abu Hanifa said: the sheep is binding on him for five and he has no option. And the correct is that he is given an option in the matter of fruits because the fruit became a intended sold item, so when the seller ate it, the transaction became divided upon him, so he has an option. End. And in al-Qunyah: If he bought land with the crop and the crop ripened in his hand, then they annulled the sale by mutual consent (iqalah), the iqalah is not permissible because the contract only applied to the standing crop not the grain. And if the buyer harvested the crop then they did iqalah, the iqalah is valid for its share of the price. And if he bought land in which there were trees and he cut them, then they did iqalah, the iqalah is valid for the entire price and nothing is due to the seller for the value of the trees, and the trees are delivered to the buyer; this is if the seller knew about the cutting of the trees. And if he did not know at the time of iqalah, he has an option: if he wishes he takes it for the entire price, and if he wishes he leaves. End. His statement: (And it is said to the seller: cut them and hand over the sold item) i.e. in the two situations, and what is meant by the sold item is the land and the trees. And it was restricted in al-Khaniyah by that he pays the price to him because the buyer's ownership is occupied by the seller's ownership, so it is upon him to vacate and deliver it, as if there were goods in it. It is restricted by the sold item because when the period expires in lease and there is crop in the land, the lessee is not ordered to uproot his crop; rather, it remains with rent to its end because it is for utilization, and that is by leaving, not uprooting, unlike purchase because it is ownership of the corpus, so possibility of utilization is not considered. And because delivery is obligatory upon him empty, but the delivery of the consideration is delivery of the thing given in exchange, so they differ; thus sale is not analogized to lease as is the madhhab of the three. Al-Ikhtiyar: And if he sold cotton in its puff, then upon the seller is to open it because he must deliver it. As for picking the fruit, cutting the fresh grass, uprooting carrots, onions, and the like, it is upon the buyer, not the seller, because he acts in his property and due to custom. End. And in al-Qunyah: If he bought fruits of the vine and trees while they are upon them, delivery is completed by vacating (takhliyah) even if they are connected to the seller's property like joint property, unlike a gift. And if he sold cotton in its puff or wheat in its ear and delivered likewise, it is not valid since it cannot be taken possession except by opening and threshing. Delivery of a house in which there are goods to other than the buyer is valid. And in his statement: (As for the cut fruit) means what passed of the detail in the three wordings regarding what is connected to the land and trees as in al-Fath. And in it also: al-Majdud with two unpointed and two pointed dals means the cut, but the unpointed here is more appropriate to match al-Mahsud. End. His statement: (i.e. its ripeness appears) al-Ramli said: it is an interpretation of his word 'bada'. His statement: 'And the validity of the sale upon this estimation is based on...' he said in al-Nahr: its summary is that the deduction by that indication is not complete...
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the commentary on the sale of land with trees and crops. It addresses the ruling that if the seller includes a condition, the seller is ordered to cut and deliver the sold item (land and trees) to the buyer. The text clarifies that if the buyer added the phrase 'with every little and much,' the fruits and crops are not included, contrary to the earlier discussion on crops and fruits (referring to the three wordings). The commentary from al-Mi'raj explains that the phrase 'every much and little' is hyperbole to drop the seller's rights, but already cut fruit and harvested crop require explicit specification. Several rulings from al-Khaniyah and al-Qunyah are cited: if the seller eats the fruit after the sale, the fruit's share is deducted from the price, and according to the correct view, the buyer has an option to either accept the remainder with the reduced price or cancel the transaction. This is analogized to a sheep that gave birth and the seller ate the lamb; Abu Hanifa said no option in that case, but the correct view in the fruit case is that the buyer has an option because the fruit was a separate intended item. Other rulings cover iqalah (mutual rescission): if the crop ripens after purchase, iqalah is not valid because the contract was on the standing crop; if harvested, iqalah is valid for its share. If trees were cut before iqalah, it is valid for the full price if the seller knew; otherwise, the seller has an option. The text then distinguishes between sale and lease: in sale, the seller must deliver the land empty, so he must remove his property, while in lease, the lessee may leave the crop until harvest with rent. Custom rules that picking fruit and uprooting vegetables are on the buyer, not the seller. Finally, delivery of fruits on trees is by takhliyah (vacating), but selling cotton in the puff or wheat in the ear requires actual separation to be valid.

Key Terms
التخلية (al-takhliyah): Vacating the property to allow the buyer to take possession; it is a valid mode of delivery for fruits on trees even if connected to the seller's land.
الإقالة (al-iqalah): Mutual rescission of a contract by agreement of both parties.
المثمر (al-muthmir): The fruit-bearing tree; the fruit is considered a separate intended item if explicitly mentioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the correct Hanafi view, if a seller eats the fruit after selling land with trees (and the fruit was included in the sale), what is the buyer's right?
A) The buyer must accept the land with a reduced price and has no other option.
B) The buyer may either take the land with a reduced price or cancel the sale.
C) The sale becomes void automatically.
D) The buyer can force the seller to replace the fruit.

Key Takeaway
In the sale of land with trees, the seller must deliver the land empty; if the seller eats the fruit, the buyer has an option; and iqalah is only valid when the subject matter has not substantially changed.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Seller's Duty to Cut and Deliver Sold Produce
Ruling on seller's duty to cut produce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the seller must cut and deliver produce when selling land or trees, and distinguish the rule from lease based on ownership of corpus (raqabah) versus use (manfa'ah).

Core Idea
Sale transfers ownership of the corpus; the seller must deliver it empty, unlike lease.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the default rule that when land or trees are sold, the seller must cut and remove his produce to deliver the sold property to the buyer. The commentary contrasts this with lease, where the lessee is not compelled to remove crops because the purpose is use, not ownership. This distinction is the basis for the Hanafi position against the three other imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Find the sentence 'ويقال للبائع اقطعها وسلم المبيع'. Understand that the seller is ordered to cut and deliver the sold property (land/trees) free of his produce.
The order is default: the seller must vacate.
2
Differentiate sale from lease
Read the passage comparing sale and lease. Note that in lease, the lessee may leave his crop and pay rent; in sale, the seller must remove his property because ownership of the corpus (raqabah) requires delivery free of encumbrances.
This distinction is the key Hanafi proof.
3
Recognize the inter-madhhab dispute
The text states that the three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) analogize sale to lease, concluding the seller should not be forced to cut. Identify their reasoning and the Hanafi response.
The dispute is over the nature of sale vs lease.
4
Note exceptions based on custom
Review the examples: picking fruit, uprooting onions, etc., are on the buyer; opening cotton bolls is on the seller. Understand that custom ('urf) and the nature of the sold item determine who cuts.
Custom can shift the default obligation.

فتقه لأن عليه تسليمه، أما جذاذ الثمرة وقطع الرطبة وقلع الجزر والبصل وأمثاله على المشتري لا البائع لأنه يعمل في ملكه وللعرف اهـ. وفي القنية : اشترى ثمار الكرم والأشجار وهي عليها يتم تسليمها بالتخلية بحكم الشراء لا بحكم الهبة اهـ. قوله : (أما الثمر المجدود) يعني ما مر من التفصيل في الألفاظ الثلاثة في المتصل بالأرض والشجر كما في الفتح. وفيه أيضاً. والمجدود بدالين مهملتين ومعجمتين بمعنى أي المقطوع غير أن المهملتين هنا أولى ليناسب المحصود .اهـ. وفي القنية : صحيح فيها أشجار لغيره بحكم الشراء لا بحكم الهبة اهـ. وفيها : وإن اشترى الزرع في الأرض فاحترق أخذها بحصتها إن شاء اهـ. وفي الولوالجية : رجل باع من آخر شجراً وعليه ثمر قد أدرك أو لم يدرك جاز وعلى البائع قطع الثمر من ساعته لأن المشتري ملك الشجر فيجبر البائع على تسليمه فارغاً، وكذلك إذا أوصى بنخل لرجل وعليه بسر أجبر الورثة على قطع البسر وهو المختار من الرواية رجل باع عنباً جزافاً فعلى المشتري قطعه، وكذلك كل شيء باعه جزافاً مثل الثوم في الأرض والجزر والبصل إذا خلي بينه وبين المشتري لأن القطع لو وجب على البائع إنما يجب إذا وجب عليه الكيل أو الوزن، ولم يجب عليه الكيل والوزن لأنه لم يبع مكايلة ولا موازنة وسيأتي تمامه آخر الباب .
With the condition that it is said to the seller: cut it and deliver the sold item. And whoever sells fruit whose ripeness has appeared or not, it is valid. And he cuts it, limiting himself (to that). And if he increases a little or much, they do not enter into them, contrary to crops and fruits. In Al-Mi'raj: His saying 'with every much and little' is mentioned for exaggeration in dropping the seller's right over the sold item. As for the cut fruit and the harvested crop, they do not enter except by specification. In Al-Khaniyya: If someone bought land with trees having fruit and said in the sale 'with its fruits', and the seller ate the fruits, the share of the fruits from the price drops. Does the buyer have the option to take the remainder? It is mentioned in Al-Buyu' that he has the option: if he wishes, he takes the remainder with what remains of the price, and if he wishes, he leaves. It is mentioned in some books that he does not have the option in the view of Abu Hanifa, as if he bought a sheep for ten and she gave birth at the seller's with a lamb worth five and the seller ate it, Abu Hanifa said: the sheep is binding on him for five and he has no option. The correct view is that he has the option in the matter of fruits because the fruit became a intended sold item; so when the seller ate, the transaction became split for him, so he has the option. In Al-Qunyah: He bought land with crop and the crop ripened in his hand, then they agreed to rescind; the rescission is not valid because the contract was only on the green stem, not the wheat. If the buyer harvested the crop then they rescinded, the rescission is valid with its share of the price. If he bought land with trees and cut them then they rescinded, the rescission is valid with the entire price and nothing is due to the seller for the value of the trees, and the trees are delivered to the buyer — this if the seller knew of the cutting; if he did not know at the time of rescission, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes them for the entire price, and if he wishes, he leaves. His saying: (It is said to the seller: cut it and deliver the sold item) i.e. in both situations, and what is meant by the sold item is the land and trees. It is restricted in Al-Khaniyya to payment of the price to him because the buyer's ownership is occupied by the seller's ownership, so he must vacate and deliver it, just as if it contained goods. It is restricted by 'the sold item' because when the period ends in lease and there is crop in the land, the lessee is not ordered to uproot his crop; rather it remains for the equivalent rent until its end, because it is for utilization and that is by leaving, not uprooting, contrary to purchase because it is ownership of the corpus, so possibility of utilization is not considered, and because delivery is obligatory on him empty, but delivery of the compensation is delivery of the compensated thing, so they differ; therefore sale is not analogized to lease, as is the madhhab of the three (other imams). The choice: If he sold cotton in its bolls, the seller must open it because he must deliver it. As for picking fruit, cutting fresh dates, uprooting carrots, onions and the like, it is on the buyer, not the seller, because he acts in his own property and due to custom. In Al-Qunyah: He bought fruits of grapevines and trees while they are on them; delivery is completed by empty space (takhliah) even if attached to the seller's property like shared property, contrary to gift. If he sold cotton in bolls or wheat in ears and delivered likewise, it is not valid since he cannot take possession except by opening and threshing; it is valid to deliver a house containing goods to someone other than the buyer, and land containing trees belonging to someone else by virtue of purchase, not by virtue of gift. His saying: (As for the cut fruit) means what preceded of the detail in the three phrases concerning what is attached to land and trees, as in Al-Fath. And in it also: 'Al-majdud' with two dal's or two dhad's meaning cut, but the two dal's are preferable here to match 'al-mahsud'. In Al-Qunyah: Correct: land with trees belonging to another by reason of purchase, not gift. And in it: If he bought the crop in the land and it burned, he takes (the land) with its share if he wishes. In Al-Walwaljiyya: A man sold to another trees with ripe or unripe fruit; it is valid, and the seller must cut the fruit immediately because the buyer owns the trees, so the seller is compelled to deliver it empty. Similarly, if he bequeathed palm trees to a man with unripe dates on them, the heirs are compelled to cut the unripe dates — this is the chosen from the narration. A man sold grapes by guess; the buyer must cut them, and likewise everything sold by guess like garlic in the ground, carrots and onions — when he gives free access to the buyer, because if cutting were obligatory on the seller, it would be obligatory only if measuring or weighing were obligatory on him, and measuring and weighing are not obligatory because he did not sell by measure or weight, and its completion will come at the end of the chapter.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a composite ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering the seller's duty to vacate the sold property. The main ruling states that when land or trees are sold with produce, the seller is ordered to cut and deliver the property free of his own property. The text then addresses various scenarios: if the seller eats the fruit, the buyer may have a choice to rescind or keep the rest at a reduced price. An intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Hanifa held no choice in a similar case of a sheep eating its young, but the correct (mufta bihi) position is that the buyer does have a choice here because the fruit is a primary sold item. The commentary distinguishes sale from lease: in a lease, the lessee is not compelled to remove crops because the purpose is use, but in sale, ownership of the corpus requires delivery free of encumbrances. The 'three imams' (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) analogize sale to lease, but the Hanafi position rejects this analogy. The passage also details when cutting is on the seller (e.g., opening cotton bolls) versus on the buyer (picking fruit, uprooting vegetables) based on custom and ownership. Finally, it discusses rescission (iqalah) after cutting or harvesting, and the delivery of fruit by takhliah (empty space). The authority weight is from later fatwa works (Al-Khaniyya, Al-Qunyah, Al-Walwaljiyya).

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): Corpus or substance of a property; ownership of the thing itself.
منفعة (manfa'ah): Usufruct or right to use a property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the primary reason the seller must cut and deliver produce when selling land?
A) Because the produce is part of the land.
B) Because the buyer owns the corpus and the seller must deliver it free.
C) Because the buyer cannot use the land with produce.
D) Because the seller is more able to cut.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the seller must cut and deliver his produce when selling land or trees because sale transfers ownership of the corpus, unlike lease.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Buyer's Option When Seller Consumes Fruit and Rules of Iqalah
Ruling on buyer's option and iqalah rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the buyer's option when the seller consumes fruit after sale, and apply the rules of iqalah (rescission) when the buyer has altered the sold item.

Core Idea
The buyer may rescind or keep at reduced price if seller consumes fruit, because fruit is a primary intended item.

This part of the passage addresses two scenarios: first, when the seller eats the fruit after the sale, the buyer may have a choice to rescind or keep the remainder at a reduced price, resolving an intra-Hanafi dispute. Second, rules of iqalah (mutual rescission) are detailed: if the buyer has already harvested or cut, the iqalah is valid with adjustments. These rulings illustrate how the contract is affected when the sold item has been partly consumed or altered.

Guided Path
1
Understand the buyer's option scenario
Read the case: buyer purchases land with fruit, seller eats the fruit. Identify the two views: Abu Hanifa says no option (analogy to sheep), but the correct view (sahih) gives the buyer an option.
The key is that fruit is a primary (maqsud) item in the sale.
2
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Note Abu Hanifa's analogy: buyer of sheep with lamb, seller eats lamb, buyer must take sheep at reduced price without option. The correct view distinguishes because fruit is a primary intended item, while lamb is secondary.
The sheep analogy is used to show the dispute.
3
Apply the rules of iqalah after alteration
Read the iqalah examples: if buyer harvests crop then rescinds, iqalah valid with share of price. If buyer cuts trees then rescinds, iqalah valid with full price if seller knew; if seller didn't know, he has an option.
The key is whether the sold item has been changed and whether the seller consented.
4
Identify the delivery method for fruit on trees
The text says fruit on trees is delivered by takhliah (empty space), even if attached to seller's property, unlike gift.
Takhliah suffices for sale but not for gift.

With the condition that it is said to the seller: cut it and deliver the sold item. And whoever sells fruit whose ripeness has appeared or not, it is valid. And he cuts it, limiting himself (to that). And if he increases a little or much, they do not enter into them, contrary to crops and fruits. In Al-Mi'raj: His saying 'with every much and little' is mentioned for exaggeration in dropping the seller's right over the sold item. As for the cut fruit and the harvested crop, they do not enter except by specification. In Al-Khaniyya: If someone bought land with trees having fruit and said in the sale 'with its fruits', and the seller ate the fruits, the share of the fruits from the price drops. Does the buyer have the option to take the remainder? It is mentioned in Al-Buyu' that he has the option: if he wishes, he takes the remainder with what remains of the price, and if he wishes, he leaves. It is mentioned in some books that he does not have the option in the view of Abu Hanifa, as if he bought a sheep for ten and she gave birth at the seller's with a lamb worth five and the seller ate it, Abu Hanifa said: the sheep is binding on him for five and he has no option. The correct view is that he has the option in the matter of fruits because the fruit became a intended sold item; so when the seller ate, the transaction became split for him, so he has the option. In Al-Qunyah: He bought land with crop and the crop ripened in his hand, then they agreed to rescind; the rescission is not valid because the contract was only on the green stem, not the wheat. If the buyer harvested the crop then they rescinded, the rescission is valid with its share of the price. If he bought land with trees and cut them then they rescinded, the rescission is valid with the entire price and nothing is due to the seller for the value of the trees, and the trees are delivered to the buyer — this if the seller knew of the cutting; if he did not know at the time of rescission, he has the option: if he wishes, he takes them for the entire price, and if he wishes, he leaves. His saying: (It is said to the seller: cut it and deliver the sold item) i.e. in both situations, and what is meant by the sold item is the land and trees. It is restricted in Al-Khaniyya to payment of the price to him because the buyer's ownership is occupied by the seller's ownership, so he must vacate and deliver it, just as if it contained goods. It is restricted by 'the sold item' because when the period ends in lease and there is crop in the land, the lessee is not ordered to uproot his crop; rather it remains for the equivalent rent until its end, because it is for utilization and that is by leaving, not uprooting, contrary to purchase because it is ownership of the corpus, so possibility of utilization is not considered, and because delivery is obligatory on him empty, but delivery of the compensation is delivery of the compensated thing, so they differ; therefore sale is not analogized to lease, as is the madhhab of the three (other imams). The choice: If he sold cotton in its bolls, the seller must open it because he must deliver it. As for picking fruit, cutting fresh dates, uprooting carrots, onions and the like, it is on the buyer, not the seller, because he acts in his own property and due to custom. In Al-Qunyah: He bought fruits of grapevines and trees while they are on them; delivery is completed by empty space (takhliah) even if attached to the seller's property like shared property, contrary to gift. If he sold cotton in bolls or wheat in ears and delivered likewise, it is not valid since he cannot take possession except by opening and threshing; it is valid to deliver a house containing goods to someone other than the buyer, and land containing trees belonging to someone else by virtue of purchase, not by virtue of gift. His saying: (As for the cut fruit) means what preceded of the detail in the three phrases concerning what is attached to land and trees, as in Al-Fath. And in it also: 'Al-majdud' with two dal's or two dhad's meaning cut, but the two dal's are preferable here to match 'al-mahsud'. In Al-Qunyah: Correct: land with trees belonging to another by reason of purchase, not gift. And in it: If he bought the crop in the land and it burned, he takes (the land) with its share if he wishes. In Al-Walwaljiyya: A man sold to another trees with ripe or unripe fruit; it is valid, and the seller must cut the fruit immediately because the buyer owns the trees, so the seller is compelled to deliver it empty. Similarly, if he bequeathed palm trees to a man with unripe dates on them, the heirs are compelled to cut the unripe dates — this is the chosen from the narration. A man sold grapes by guess; the buyer must cut them, and likewise everything sold by guess like garlic in the ground, carrots and onions — when he gives free access to the buyer, because if cutting were obligatory on the seller, it would be obligatory only if measuring or weighing were obligatory on him, and measuring and weighing are not obligatory because he did not sell by measure or weight, and its completion will come at the end of the chapter.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is a composite ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq, covering the seller's duty to vacate the sold property. The main ruling states that when land or trees are sold with produce, the seller is ordered to cut and deliver the property free of his own property. The text then addresses various scenarios: if the seller eats the fruit, the buyer may have a choice to rescind or keep the rest at a reduced price. An intra-Hanafi dispute is noted: Abu Hanifa held no choice in a similar case of a sheep eating its young, but the correct (mufta bihi) position is that the buyer does have a choice here because the fruit is a primary sold item. The commentary distinguishes sale from lease: in a lease, the lessee is not compelled to remove crops because the purpose is use, but in sale, ownership of the corpus requires delivery free of encumbrances. The 'three imams' (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) analogize sale to lease, but the Hanafi position rejects this analogy. The passage also details when cutting is on the seller (e.g., opening cotton bolls) versus on the buyer (picking fruit, uprooting vegetables) based on custom and ownership. Finally, it discusses rescission (iqalah) after cutting or harvesting, and the delivery of fruit by takhliah (empty space). The authority weight is from later fatwa works (Al-Khaniyya, Al-Qunyah, Al-Walwaljiyya).

Key Terms
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement.
مبيع مقصود (mabi' maqsud): Primary intended object of sale; something that is a main purpose of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The buyer has no option and must pay the full price.
B) The buyer has the option to rescind or keep the land at a reduced price.
C) The sale is void.
D) The seller must replace the fruit.

Key Takeaway
The buyer has the option to rescind or keep the remainder at a reduced price when the seller consumes fruit after sale, because fruit is a primary sold item; iqalah is valid after alteration with price adjustments.

Validity of Selling Fruit Before and After Ripening
Ruling on selling fruit regardless of ripeness with inter-madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits selling fruit before ripening (badu al-salah) and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school permits selling fruit before ripening because it is valuable property; the Prophetic prohibition is interpreted as applying only before the fruit appears.

You may have heard that selling fruit before it is ripe is prohibited. This lesson examines a key inter-madhhab dispute: while the majority of schools base their prohibition on a clear hadith, the Hanafi school adopts a different interpretation, arguing that the prohibition applies only before the fruit appears, not after appearance and before ripeness. We will trace the Hanafi evidence from Imam Muhammad's reasoning in zakat and the legal stratagem (hilah) used to avoid potential issues.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
What does the Hanafi school rule regarding selling fruit before the appearance of ripeness (badu al-salah)?
The text states it is valid absolutely because it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Which schools disagree and what is their ruling?
The three imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) prohibit selling fruit until ripeness appears.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
What evidence do they cite?
They rely on the hadith in the two Sahihs from Anas that the Prophet forbade selling fruits until ripeness appears.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
How do Hanafis counter this hadith?
Imam al-Halwani interprets the hadith as referring to selling fruit before it appears (zuhur), not after appearance and before ripening. Also, Imam Muhammad's analogy from zakat shows the sale is valid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence of this difference in rulings?
Hanafis allow the sale of fruit before ripening; the other three schools prohibit it until ripeness is apparent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ومن باع ثمرة بدا صلاحها أو لا صح ) أي ظهر صلاحها وإنما صح مطلقاً لأنه مال متقوم إما لكونه منتفعاً به في الحال أو في المآل . وقيل : لا يجوز قبل بدو الصلاح والأول أصح . وقوله ثمرة» أي ظاهرة قيدنا به لأن بيعها قبل الظهور لا اتفاقاً، يصح وقبل بدو الصلاح والأول أصح. وقوله «ثمرة» أي ظاهرة قيدنا به لأن بيعها قبل الظهور لا يصح اتفاقاً، وقبل بدو الصلاح بشرط القطع في المنتفع به اتفاقاً، وقبل بدو الصلاح بعد الظهور بشرط الترك غير صحيح اتفاقاً ، وبعد بدو الصلاح صحيح اتفاقاً، وبعدما تناهت صحيح اتفاقاً إذا أطلق. وأما بشرط الترك ففيه اختلاف سيأتي فصار محل الخلاف البيع بعد الظهور قبل بدو الصلاح مطلقاً أي لا بشرط القطع ولا بشرط الترك فعند الأئمة الثلاثة لا يجوز، وعندنا يجوز ولكن اختلفوا فيما إذا كان غیر منتفع به الآن أكلاً وعلفاً للدواب؛ فقيل بعدم الجواز ونسبه قاضيخان لعامة مشايخنا، والصحيح الجواز كما قدمنا وقد أشار إليه محمد في كتاب الزكاة فإنه قال : لو باع الثمار في أول ما تطلع وتركها بإذن البائع أدرك فالعشر على المشتري، فلو لم يكن جائزاً لم يوجب فيه على المشتري العشر، وصحة البيع على هذا التقدير بناء على التعويل على إذن البائع على ما ذكرنا من قريب وإلا فلا انتفاع به مطلقاً فلا يجوز بيعه والحيلة في جوازه باتفاق المشايخ أن يبيع الكمثرى أول ما يخرج من أوراق الشجر فيجوز فيها تبعاً للأوراق كأنه ورق كله وإن كان بحيث ينتفع به ولو علفاً للدواب فالبيع جائز باتفاق أهل المذهب إذا باع بشرط القطع أو ويجب قطعه على المشتري. واستدل أصحابنا بما استدل به محمد سابقاً لأنه شامل لما قبل بدو الصلاح والأئمة الثلاثة كما في الصحيحين عن أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه أنه نهى عن بيع الثمار حتى يبدو صلاحها، وعن بيع النخل حتى تزهو . قال : تحمار أو تصفار. وأجاب عنه الإمام الحلواني كما في الخانية أنه محمول على ما قبل الظهور وغيره على مطلقاً حتى بعمومه لأن المدعي عام وهي في خاص لكن قد علم من دلالة الاتفاق على جواز بيع المهر والجحش جواز الثمار التي لا ينتفع بها الآن فذكر محمد الترك بإذن البائع في التصوير إنما هو لوجوب العشر لا بيع
His saying: (And whoever sold fruit whose ripeness has appeared or not, it is valid) i.e., its ripeness has appeared. And it is valid absolutely because it is valuable property, either because it is beneficial immediately or in the future. It is said: it is not permissible before the appearance of ripeness, and the first is more correct. His saying 'fruit' i.e., visible; we restrict by it because selling it before appearance is not valid by agreement. And before the appearance of ripeness with the condition of cutting in something beneficial, by agreement, and before the appearance of ripeness after appearance with the condition of leaving is not valid by agreement, and after the appearance of ripeness it is valid by agreement, and after it has fully matured it is valid by agreement when unqualified. As for with the condition of leaving, there is a difference that will come. So the locus of dispute is the sale after appearance before the appearance of ripeness absolutely, i.e., neither with the condition of cutting nor with the condition of leaving. According to the three imams, it is not permissible; according to us, it is permissible. But they differed regarding when it is not currently beneficial for eating or fodder for animals: some said it is not permissible, and Qadi Khan attributed that to the generality of our shaykhs; the correct view is permissibility, as we have presented. Muhammad indicated it in the Book of Zakat, for he said: If he sold fruits at their first appearance and left them with the seller's permission, and they ripened, then 'ushr is on the buyer. If it were not permissible, he would not have obligated 'ushr on the buyer. The validity of the sale on this basis is built on reliance on the seller's permission as we mentioned recently; otherwise, there is no benefit in it absolutely, so its sale is not permissible. The legal trick (hilah) for its permissibility by agreement of the shaykhs is that he sells the pear at its first emergence from the tree leaves, and it is permissible as a follow-up to the leaves, as if it were all leaves. And if it is such that it is beneficial even as fodder for animals, then the sale is permissible by agreement of the madhhab if he sells with the condition of cutting, or cutting becomes obligatory on the buyer. Our scholars argued with what Muhammad argued previously, because it includes before the appearance of ripeness. The three imams (argue) as in the two Sahihs from Anas that he prohibited selling fruit until its ripeness appears, and selling palm trees until they become red or yellow. Imam al-Halwani answered as in Al-Khaniyya: that it is interpreted as referring to before appearance, and others (interpret) absolutely, but the one claiming (hadith is general) while it is specific, but it is known from the indication of agreement on the permissibility of selling the dowry and the donkey foal the permissibility of fruits that are not beneficial now. So Muhammad's mention of leaving with the seller's permission in the illustration is only for the obligation of 'ushr, not for the sale.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on selling fruit before ripening. The Hanafi madhhab holds that selling fruit is valid even before ripening (badu al-salah) because it is valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) with either present or future benefit. The three other imams (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) prohibit it based on the hadith in the two Sahihs where the Prophet (ﷺ) forbade selling fruits until their ripeness appears and selling date palms until they turn red or yellow. The Hanafi response, detailed by Imam al-Halwani, is that this hadith refers to selling fruit before it appears (i.e., before the fruit is visible), not after appearance and before ripening. The Hanafis also rely on a statement of Imam Muhammad that if someone sells fruit at first appearance and leaves it with the seller's permission, and it ripens, the 'ushr (tithe) is on the buyer — indicating the sale is valid. An intra-Hanafi difference exists regarding fruit not currently beneficial: the correct position is that it is still permissible. The text also provides a legal trick (hilah): selling pears when they first emerge as a follow-up to the leaves, making the sale permissible by consensus. The authority weight: the Hanafi position here is from the zahir al-riwayah as indicated by Imam Muhammad's statement in the Kitab al-Zakat.

Key Terms
بدو الصلاح (badu al-salah): The appearance of ripeness in fruit; a stage where the fruit begins to show signs of being ready.
الحيلة (hilah): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible outcome within the framework of Shari'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling on selling fruit before its ripeness appears (badu al-salah)?
A) It is prohibited based on a hadith in the Sahihain.
B) It is valid only if the fruit is currently beneficial for eating.
C) It is valid absolutely because it is valuable property.
D) It is valid only if the seller cuts the fruit immediately.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab permits selling fruit before it ripens because it is valuable property, interpreting the hadith prohibition as applying only before the fruit appears on the tree.

Sale of Fruit Before and After Ripening: Conditions and Ikhtilaf
Explains the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit with or without ripeness and the dispute with other schools

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling fruit before ripening, identify the conditions that render the sale valid or invalid, and evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute with its proofs and responses.

Core Idea
Hanafis permit selling unripe fruit unconditionally; the prophetic prohibition applies only with a condition of leaving.

The sale of fruit before it ripens is a classic inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi school holds that once the fruit has appeared, it is valuable property and can be sold without any stipulation, while the three other schools require ripening unless immediate cutting is agreed. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Kanz al-Daqā’iq, the conditions that frame the dispute, and the Hanafi response to the hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its context
Identify the ruling: sale of fruit after appearance but before ripening is valid (sahih) unconditionally. Note the five consensus cases that frame the dispute.
The dispute is only about the absolute sale (lā bi-sharṭ al-qaṭʿ wa lā bi-sharṭ al-tark).
2
Identify the opposing view and its proof
The three Imams (Malik, Shafi‘i, Ahmad) prohibit such a sale. Their primary evidence is the hadith in the Ṣaḥīḥayn from Anas forbidding sale until ripeness appears.
The hadith is cited and its apparent generality (ʿāmm) is claimed.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis interpret the hadith as restricted to sales with a condition of leaving (bi-sharṭ al-tark), not to absolute sales. They argue that the prohibition is explained by the Prophet’s own reasoning (ʿilla) about risk of loss, which only applies when the seller retains possession.
This is a case of takhṣīṣ al-ʿāmm by qarīna. Also, consensus on the validity of sale with condition of cutting shows the hadith cannot be general.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
When the fruit has no current benefit (e.g., not even for fodder), some Hanafis (as reported by Qāḍī Khān) deem it invalid. The correct (ṣaḥīḥ) view is permissibility, supported by Muḥammad’s ruling in Kitāb al-Zakāt.
The stratagem (ḥīla) of selling fruit as a follower (tābiʿ) of leaves is mentioned for the unbeneficial case.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamara al-khilāf)
In practice, Hanafis allow the sale of unripe fruit on the tree without any stipulation, while other schools require either ripening or an immediate-cutting condition. This affects agricultural contracts and market transactions.
The definition of ‘ripening’ (badū al-ṣalāḥ) also differs: Hanafis: safety from blight; Shafi‘is: incipient sweetness.

He said: (Whoever sells fruit whether its ripeness has appeared or not, it is valid) i.e. its ripeness has appeared, and it is valid absolutely because it is valuable property either being beneficial immediately or potentially. It was said: it is not permissible before ripening appears, and the first is more correct. And his saying 'fruit' i.e. apparent, we stipulate that because its sale before appearance is not valid unanimously, and before ripening with the condition of cutting in what is beneficial is valid unanimously, and before ripening after appearance with the condition of leaving is not valid unanimously, and after ripening is valid unanimously, and after it has fully ripened is valid unanimously if unconditioned. As for with the condition of leaving, there is disagreement to come. So the place of dispute is the sale after appearance before ripening absolutely, i.e. without condition of cutting nor condition of leaving. According to the three Imams it is not permissible, and according to us it is permissible, but they differed about when it is not beneficial now for eating or fodder for animals: some said impermissibility, Qadi Khan attributed it to most of our scholars, and the correct is permissibility as we have mentioned. Muhammad indicated it in the Book of Zakat. He said: If he sold fruits at their first appearance and left them with the seller's permission and they ripened, then the tithe is on the buyer. If it were not permissible, he would not have imposed the tithe on the buyer. The validity of the sale on this basis is based on reliance on the seller's permission as we mentioned shortly; otherwise there is no benefit at all, so its sale is not permissible. The legal stratagem for its permissibility according to the consensus of the scholars is that he sells the pear at its first emergence from the leaves, then it is permissible as a follow-up to the leaves as if it is all leaves. And if it is such that it is beneficial even as fodder for animals, the sale is permissible according to the consensus of the school if he sells with the condition of cutting, and it is obligatory for the buyer to cut it. Our scholars argued with what Muhammad argued previously because it includes by its generality what is before ripening. And the three Imams [argue] as in the two Sahihs from Anas (may Allah be pleased with him) that he forbade selling fruits until their ripeness appears, and forbade selling palm trees until they turn red or yellow. Imam al-Halwani answered as in al-Khaniyya that it is interpreted as before appearance, and other than that as absolute even by its generality because the claimant is general and it is specific. But it is known from the evidence of consensus on the permissibility of selling a foal and a donkey the permissibility of fruits that are not beneficial now. So Muhammad mentioned leaving with the seller's permission in the illustration only for the obligation of the tithe, not for the sale. In the case where it is with the condition of leaving, they abandoned its apparent meaning and permitted the sale before ripening with the condition of cutting, which is a clear contradiction to its explicit wording. We have agreed that its apparent meaning is abandoned, and that is not permissible without reason. According to them, his (peace be upon him) explanation is: "Do you see if Allah prevents the fruit, then by what right would any of you take his brother's wealth?" This implies that the meaning is that he forbade selling it ripe before ripening because the custom is that people sell fruits before cutting, so he forbade this sale before the mentioned quality exists. So the subject of the prohibition is the fruit before ripening with the condition of leaving until ripening appears, and the sale with the condition of cutting is not imagined to involve that, so it is not covered by the prohibition. Since its subject is its sale with the condition of leaving until it ripens, we have fulfilled the requirement of this prohibition, for we have said this sale is corrupt. So this leaves its sale absolutely as not covered by the prohibition in any way, up to the end of what was investigated in Fath al-Qadir. And he interpreted in al-Mi'raj as a forward sale (salam), and the appearance of ripeness according to us is safety from affliction and spoilage, and according to al-Shafi'i is the appearance of ripeness and sweetness.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute about the sale of fruit before or after ripening. The commentator explains the ruling from Kanz al-Daqaiq: selling fruit, whether ripeness has appeared or not, is valid. He clarifies that this applies when the fruit is already visible (appeared) but not yet ripe. The validity is because it is valuable property. He notes a minority view that it is invalid before ripening appears, but the correct Hanafi position is validity. The commentator then distinguishes several cases: sale before appearance is invalid by consensus; sale before ripening with condition of cutting is valid by consensus (since the buyer will consume immediately); sale before ripening with condition of leaving (i.e., letting the fruit stay on the tree) is corrupt by consensus; sale after ripening is valid by consensus; sale after full ripeness is valid if unconditional. The disputed case is sale after appearance but before ripening, without any condition (i.e., neither cutting nor leaving). The three Imams (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) say it is invalid, while the Hanafi school says it is valid. However, within the Hanafi school there is disagreement about the case where the fruit has no current benefit at all (like unripe fruit that cannot be used even as animal fodder). Qadi Khan reports that most Hanafi scholars say it is invalid, but the correct view according to the commentator is validity. This is supported by a narration from Muhammad al-Shaybani in the Book of Zakat: if someone sells dates at their first appearance and leaves them with the seller's permission, and they ripen, the zakah is on the buyer. If the sale were invalid, Muhammad would not have imposed zakah. Thus, the permissibility is based on reliance on the seller's permission to leave the fruit. If there is no such permission, then the fruit has no benefit, so the sale is invalid. The commentator also mentions a legal stratagem (hila) for selling such fruit: sell it along with its leaves as part of the tree, so it becomes a follow-up to the leaves, and the whole is considered leaves. Then the fruit is sold as part of the tree, which is permissible. If the fruit is beneficial even as fodder, the sale is permissible by consensus if the buyer cuts it immediately. The Hanafi proof for permissibility is the generality of the Quranic verse about trade, as Muhammad argued. The opposing school relies on the hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim from Anas: the Prophet (peace be upon him) forbade selling fruits until they ripen. Imam al-Halwani answers that this hadith is interpreted as referring to sale before appearance, or to sale with the condition of leaving. The Hanafis argue that the hadith's explicit wording (with condition of leaving) is contradicted by the consensus that sale with condition of cutting is valid. Therefore, the prohibition is specifically for sales where fruit is left to ripen, which the Hanafis also consider corrupt. Sale without any condition is not covered by the prohibition. Al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir provides a detailed analysis. Finally, the commentator clarifies that 'appearance of ripeness' (badu al-salah) in the Hanafi school means safety from blight and spoilage, while al-Shafi'i defines it as incipient redness or sweetness.

Key Terms
بدو الصلاح (badu al-salah): The appearance of ripeness; according to Hanafis, it means the fruit becomes safe from blight and spoilage, not necessarily fully ripe.
ثمرة (thamara): Fruit that is visible (apparent) on the tree; the sale before its appearance is invalid by consensus.
جزاف (juzaf): Sale by estimate without weighing or measuring. In this passage, it refers to selling agricultural produce like grapes by estimate, where the buyer must cut it immediately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, the sale of fruit after it appears but before ripening is:
A) Invalid in all cases
B) Valid if sold without any condition (mutlaqan)
C) Valid only if the buyer cuts it immediately
D) Invalid unless the seller gives permission to leave

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling unripe fruit unconditionally, interpreting the prophetic prohibition as limited to sales with a condition of leaving.

General Ruling on New Fruit Before and After Delivery
Ruling statement on new fruit timing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on new fruit appearing before delivery (sale invalid) and after delivery (shared ownership), and apply the rule to similar crops like eggplant.

Core Idea
New fruit before delivery invalidates sale; after delivery, buyer and seller share the new growth.

When you buy produce like fruit, what happens if the tree grows more fruit before you take delivery? The Hanafi school gives two different rulings depending on timing. This lesson explains the general rule and the rationale behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the timing
Determine whether the new growth appears before or after the buyer takes possession (qabd).
Possession is key; it can be physical or constructive.
2
Apply the rule
If before delivery, the sale is invalid (fasid) due to impossibility of distinguishing what was sold from what is new. If after delivery, the sale remains valid but the new fruit is shared by buyer and seller proportionally.
The buyer's word about quantity is accepted with oath.
3
Recognize the extension
The same rule applies to crops like eggplant and watermelon where parts emerge after delivery.
The legal cause (illa) is the same: inability to separate ownership.

ولو اشتراها مطلقاً فاثمرت ثمراً آخر قبل القبض فسد البيع لتعذر التمييز. ولو أثمرت بعده اشتركا للاختلاط والقول قول المشتري مع يمينه في مقداره لأنه في يده، وكذا في بيع الباذنجان والبطيخ إذا حدث بعد القبض خروج بعضها اشتركا، وكان الحلواني يفتي بجوازه في الكل وزعم أنه مروي عن أصحابنا، وهكذا حكى عن الإمام الفضلي وكان يقول : الموجود وقت العقد أصل وما يحدث تبع له، نقله شمس الأئمة عنه ولم يقيده عنه بكون الموجود وقت العقد يكون أكثر بل قال عنه اجعل الموجود أصلاً في العقد وما يب بعد ذلك تبعاً. وقال : استحسن فيه لتعامل الناس فإنهم تعاملوا بيع ثمار الكرم بهذه الصفة ولهم في ذلك عادة ظاهرة وفي نزع الناس عن عاداتهم حرج. وقد رأيت في هذا رواية عن محمد وهو في بيع الورد على الأشجار فإن الورد متلاحق، ثم جوز المبيع في الكل بهذا الطريق وهو قول مالك. والمخلص هذه اللوازم الصعبة أن يشتري أصول الباذنجان والبطيخ والرطبة ليكون ما يحدث على من ملكه، وفي الزرع والحشيش يشتري الموجود ببعض الثمن ويستأجر الأرض مدة معلومة يعلم غاية الإدراك وانقضاء الغرض فيها بباقي الثمن، وفي ثمار الأشجار يشتري الموجود ويحل له البائع ما يوجد، فإن خاف أن يرجع يفعل كما قال الفقيه أبو الليث في الإذن في ترك الثمر على الشجر على أنه متى رجع عن الإذن كان مأذوناً في الترك بإذن جديد فيحل له على مثل . لجواز البيع. قوله : ( ولو أثمرت بعده اشتركا للاختلاط قال في النهر : فإن قلت قد مر أن الترك إن كان بإذن البائع يطيب له الفضل وإلا تصدق بالفضل فمتى يشتركان؟ قلت: معنى الأول أن الزيادة إنما وقعت في ذات المبيع كما مرّ، ومعنى الثانية أن العين الزائدة لم يقع عليها بيع وإنما حدثت بعده وقد خفي هذا على بعض طلبة الدرس إلى أن بينته له بذلك والله تعالى الموفق . قوله : (بباقي الثمن) متعلق بقوله ويستأجر». قوله : (وفي ثمار الأشجار يشتري الموجود ويحل له البائع ما يوجد الخ)
And if he bought it unconditionally and it bore another fruit before delivery, the sale is invalid due to impossibility of distinction. And if it bore after delivery, they share due to mixing; the word of the buyer with his oath regarding its amount is accepted because it is in his hand. Similarly in the sale of eggplant and watermelon, if after delivery some of them emerge, they share. Al-Halwani used to give fatwa of permissibility in all and claimed it is narrated from our scholars. Likewise it is narrated from Imam al-Fadli, who used to say: The existing at the time of contract is the origin and what occurs is follow-up to it. Shams al-A'immah reported from him without restricting it to the existing being more, but he said: Make the existing the origin in the contract and what comes after that as follow-up. He said: I approved it for people's dealings, for they deal in selling grapes in this manner and they have a manifest custom in it, and forcing people to abandon their customs is hardship. I have seen a narration from Muhammad regarding the sale of roses on trees, for roses are successive. Then he permitted the sale in all by this method, and it is the view of Malik. The escape from these difficult consequences is that he buys the roots of eggplant, watermelon, and fodder so that what occurs belongs to him who owns them. In crops and grass, he buys the existing with part of the price and rents the land for a known period until he knows the end of ripening and the completion of the purpose in it with the remaining price. In fruits of trees, he buys the existing and the seller permits him what occurs. If he fears that he may withdraw, he acts as al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said regarding permission to leave fruit on the tree: whenever he withdraws the permission, he is permitted to leave with a new permission, so it becomes permissible to him like that. For the permissibility of sale. He said: (And if it bore after delivery they share due to mixing) He said in al-Nahr: If you say: It has passed that if the leaving is with the seller's permission, the excess becomes lawful for him; otherwise he gives charity with the excess. So when do they share? I say: The meaning of the first is that the increase occurred in the essence of the sold item as passed, and the meaning of the second is that the additional item was not included in the sale and only occurred after it. This was hidden from some students until I explained it to them. And Allah is the Granter of success. He said: (with the remaining price) is connected to 'he rents'. He said: (And in fruits of trees, he buys the existing and the seller permits him what occurs etc.)
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage deals with new growth after the sale of agricultural produce. The first ruling: if someone buys fruit (trees or produce) unconditionally and the tree bears new fruit before delivery, the sale is invalid because it is impossible to distinguish which fruit was sold and which is new. If the new fruit appears after delivery, the buyer and seller share the new fruit in proportion to their ownership, causing mixing. The buyer's statement about the amount is accepted with his oath because the fruit is in his possession. This rule also applies to crops like eggplant and watermelon where parts emerge after delivery (e.g., new fruits growing). However, al-Halwani and al-Fadli gave a fatwa based on istihsan: they treat the existing fruit as the basis and any subsequent growth as a follow-up (taba'), so the sale remains valid and the new fruit belongs to the buyer. This is supported by the custom of people selling grapes in this manner. Shams al-A'immma al-Halwani reports a narration from Muhammad regarding roses, which grow successively, permitting the sale to include future blooms. This resembles the Maliki view. To avoid these complex issues, the commentator provides legal stratagems (hilal): 1) For eggplant, watermelon, and fodder, one should buy the roots (usul) so that whatever grows becomes owned by the buyer from the start. 2) For crops and grass, buy the existing produce with part of the price and rent the land for a known period until the crop is fully ripe, paying the remaining price as rent. 3) For tree fruits, buy the existing fruit and have the seller give permission for future fruit. If the seller might revoke permission, use a mechanism from Abu al-Layth: the permission is given on condition that even if revoked, a new permission is automatically granted, thus ensuring lawfulness. The commentator from al-Nahr clarifies a potential confusion: if the seller gives permission to leave the fruit, the buyer profits from any increase, otherwise he must give charity. So when do they share? The increase in the essence of the sold item (like the fruit growing larger) belongs to the buyer; if a new fruit appears (a separate entity), it was not sold, so it is shared. This distinction is important for fatwa.

Key Terms
اشتراك (ishtirak): Shared ownership between buyer and seller when new fruit appears after delivery, due to mixing of the original sold item with new growth.
تمييز (tamyiz): Distinction; the ability to separate what was sold from what was not. Its impossibility invalidates the sale before delivery.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the general rule in the passage, if a tree bears new fruit after the buyer has taken delivery, what happens?
A) The sale becomes invalid
B) The buyer and seller share the new fruit
C) The new fruit belongs to the seller alone
D) The new fruit belongs to the buyer alone

Key Takeaway
The timing of new growth relative to delivery determines ownership: before delivery invalidates, after delivery creates shared ownership.

The Principle of Istihsan: Customary Sales of Produce
Istihsan-based exception for customary produce sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi istihsan principle that allows sales of produce with subsequent growth based on custom, and identify its application to grapes, roses, and similar cases.

Core Idea
Custom permits treating existing produce as the basis and future growth as follow-up, making the sale valid.

The general rule of sharing new fruit after delivery can be inconvenient in practice, especially when people commonly sell grapes while future bunches are expected. Hanafi scholars al-Halwani and al-Fadli introduced an exception via istihsan: if there is a strong custom, the sale is valid and the future growth belongs to the buyer. This lesson explores that principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
The general rule creates hardship when people habitually sell produce that grows sequentially.
For example, grapes on vines that will produce multiple bunches over time.
2
State the istihsan solution
Al-Halwani and al-Fadli treat the existing fruit as the basis (asl) and future growth as follow-up (taba'), so the sale is valid and the buyer gets everything.
This is based on custom (urf) and a narration from Muhammad.
3
Verify the evidence
The custom of the people is 'manifest' (zahira), and forcing them to abandon it would cause hardship (haraj).
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi source that prioritizes custom over strict analogy.
4
Recognize the scope
The istihsan applies to all similar cases (grapes, roses, etc.) and aligns with the Maliki view.
Thus, mukhtalaf fih but accepted in Hanafi fatwa.

ولو اشتراها مطلقاً فاثمرت ثمراً آخر قبل القبض فسد البيع لتعذر التمييز. ولو أثمرت بعده اشتركا للاختلاط والقول قول المشتري مع يمينه في مقداره لأنه في يده، وكذا في بيع الباذنجان والبطيخ إذا حدث بعد القبض خروج بعضها اشتركا، وكان الحلواني يفتي بجوازه في الكل وزعم أنه مروي عن أصحابنا، وهكذا حكى عن الإمام الفضلي وكان يقول : الموجود وقت العقد أصل وما يحدث تبع له، نقله شمس الأئمة عنه ولم يقيده عنه بكون الموجود وقت العقد يكون أكثر بل قال عنه اجعل الموجود أصلاً في العقد وما يب بعد ذلك تبعاً. وقال : استحسن فيه لتعامل الناس فإنهم تعاملوا بيع ثمار الكرم بهذه الصفة ولهم في ذلك عادة ظاهرة وفي نزع الناس عن عاداتهم حرج. وقد رأيت في هذا رواية عن محمد وهو في بيع الورد على الأشجار فإن الورد متلاحق، ثم جوز المبيع في الكل بهذا الطريق وهو قول مالك. والمخلص هذه اللوازم الصعبة أن يشتري أصول الباذنجان والبطيخ والرطبة ليكون ما يحدث على من ملكه، وفي الزرع والحشيش يشتري الموجود ببعض الثمن ويستأجر الأرض مدة معلومة يعلم غاية الإدراك وانقضاء الغرض فيها بباقي الثمن، وفي ثمار الأشجار يشتري الموجود ويحل له البائع ما يوجد، فإن خاف أن يرجع يفعل كما قال الفقيه أبو الليث في الإذن في ترك الثمر على الشجر على أنه متى رجع عن الإذن كان مأذوناً في الترك بإذن جديد فيحل له على مثل . لجواز البيع. قوله : ( ولو أثمرت بعده اشتركا للاختلاط قال في النهر : فإن قلت قد مر أن الترك إن كان بإذن البائع يطيب له الفضل وإلا تصدق بالفضل فمتى يشتركان؟ قلت: معنى الأول أن الزيادة إنما وقعت في ذات المبيع كما مرّ، ومعنى الثانية أن العين الزائدة لم يقع عليها بيع وإنما حدثت بعده وقد خفي هذا على بعض طلبة الدرس إلى أن بينته له بذلك والله تعالى الموفق . قوله : (بباقي الثمن) متعلق بقوله ويستأجر». قوله : (وفي ثمار الأشجار يشتري الموجود ويحل له البائع ما يوجد الخ)
And if he bought it unconditionally and it bore another fruit before delivery, the sale is invalid due to impossibility of distinction. And if it bore after delivery, they share due to mixing; the word of the buyer with his oath regarding its amount is accepted because it is in his hand. Similarly in the sale of eggplant and watermelon, if after delivery some of them emerge, they share. Al-Halwani used to give fatwa of permissibility in all and claimed it is narrated from our scholars. Likewise it is narrated from Imam al-Fadli, who used to say: The existing at the time of contract is the origin and what occurs is follow-up to it. Shams al-A'immah reported from him without restricting it to the existing being more, but he said: Make the existing the origin in the contract and what comes after that as follow-up. He said: I approved it for people's dealings, for they deal in selling grapes in this manner and they have a manifest custom in it, and forcing people to abandon their customs is hardship. I have seen a narration from Muhammad regarding the sale of roses on trees, for roses are successive. Then he permitted the sale in all by this method, and it is the view of Malik. The escape from these difficult consequences is that he buys the roots of eggplant, watermelon, and fodder so that what occurs belongs to him who owns them. In crops and grass, he buys the existing with part of the price and rents the land for a known period until he knows the end of ripening and the completion of the purpose in it with the remaining price. In fruits of trees, he buys the existing and the seller permits him what occurs. If he fears that he may withdraw, he acts as al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said regarding permission to leave fruit on the tree: whenever he withdraws the permission, he is permitted to leave with a new permission, so it becomes permissible to him like that. For the permissibility of sale. He said: (And if it bore after delivery they share due to mixing) He said in al-Nahr: If you say: It has passed that if the leaving is with the seller's permission, the excess becomes lawful for him; otherwise he gives charity with the excess. So when do they share? I say: The meaning of the first is that the increase occurred in the essence of the sold item as passed, and the meaning of the second is that the additional item was not included in the sale and only occurred after it. This was hidden from some students until I explained it to them. And Allah is the Granter of success. He said: (with the remaining price) is connected to 'he rents'. He said: (And in fruits of trees, he buys the existing and the seller permits him what occurs etc.)
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage deals with new growth after the sale of agricultural produce. The first ruling: if someone buys fruit (trees or produce) unconditionally and the tree bears new fruit before delivery, the sale is invalid because it is impossible to distinguish which fruit was sold and which is new. If the new fruit appears after delivery, the buyer and seller share the new fruit in proportion to their ownership, causing mixing. The buyer's statement about the amount is accepted with his oath because the fruit is in his possession. This rule also applies to crops like eggplant and watermelon where parts emerge after delivery (e.g., new fruits growing). However, al-Halwani and al-Fadli gave a fatwa based on istihsan: they treat the existing fruit as the basis and any subsequent growth as a follow-up (taba'), so the sale remains valid and the new fruit belongs to the buyer. This is supported by the custom of people selling grapes in this manner. Shams al-A'immma al-Halwani reports a narration from Muhammad regarding roses, which grow successively, permitting the sale to include future blooms. This resembles the Maliki view. To avoid these complex issues, the commentator provides legal stratagems (hilal): 1) For eggplant, watermelon, and fodder, one should buy the roots (usul) so that whatever grows becomes owned by the buyer from the start. 2) For crops and grass, buy the existing produce with part of the price and rent the land for a known period until the crop is fully ripe, paying the remaining price as rent. 3) For tree fruits, buy the existing fruit and have the seller give permission for future fruit. If the seller might revoke permission, use a mechanism from Abu al-Layth: the permission is given on condition that even if revoked, a new permission is automatically granted, thus ensuring lawfulness. The commentator from al-Nahr clarifies a potential confusion: if the seller gives permission to leave the fruit, the buyer profits from any increase, otherwise he must give charity. So when do they share? The increase in the essence of the sold item (like the fruit growing larger) belongs to the buyer; if a new fruit appears (a separate entity), it was not sold, so it is shared. This distinction is important for fatwa.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference that overrides strict analogy in favor of custom or necessity; here, it allows treating new growth as follow-up to the original sale.
الموجود وقت العقد أصل وما يحدث تبع (al-mawjud waqt al-'aqd asl wa ma yahdath taba'): A principle that the existing item at the time of contract is the basis (asl) and any subsequent growth is follow-up (taba'), used to validate the sale of produce over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What was the primary basis for al-Halwani's fatwa permitting the sale of produce with subsequent growth?
A) A verse from the Quran
B) A hadith from the Prophet
C) The custom of the people and a narration from Muhammad
D) Consensus of the Companions

Key Takeaway
Custom-based istihsan allows sale of produce with future growth by treating the existing as the basis and future as follow-up, overriding the general rule of sharing.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
10 min
Legal Stratagems (Hiyal) for Selling Growing Produce
Practical legal stratagems for selling growing produce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid Hanafi legal stratagem (hila) to sell produce with ongoing growth, such as by buying the roots or using a combination of sale and lease.

Core Idea
Legal stratagems circumvent the general ruling by structuring transactions differently, ensuring validity while achieving the economic outcome.

Even with the istihsan exception, some situations may still be problematic. The classical scholars provided three concrete strategies (hiyal) to avoid the pitfalls of shared ownership or invalid sales. This lesson walks through each stratagem for different types of produce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem crop
Different crops require different stratagems. Root-crops (eggplant, watermelon, fodder) need one method; annual crops (wheat, grass) need another; tree fruits need a third.
The stratagem must match the nature of the produce.
2
Understand the rationale
Each stratagem avoids the issue of future growth by either making the growth belong to the buyer from the start (buying roots) or separating the sale of existing produce from the use of land (rent).
They are based on existing valid contracts, not on fictitious transactions.
3
Apply the stratagem
For root-crops: buy the roots (usul) so future growth automatically belongs to buyer. For annual crops: buy existing produce with part price and rent land for the remainder. For tree fruits: buy existing fruit and get permission (idhn) from seller for future fruit.
The permission stratagem can be made irrevocable via a mechanism from Abu al-Layth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حيلة شرعية (hila shar'iyya): A legal stratagem that uses a combination of valid contracts to achieve a permissible result that would otherwise be problematic under strict fiqh rules.
إذن (idhn): Permission; in this context, the seller's permission for the buyer to take future fruit, which can be structured to automatically renew.

Sale of Fruit on Trees: Post-Contract Growth and Delivery by Takhliya
Rulings on sale of fruit on trees from multiple Hanafi sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that post-contract growth belongs to the seller, explain delivery by takhliya for fruit on trees, and determine the buyer's rights when the seller cuts the fruit.

Core Idea
Post-contract growth belongs to seller; delivery is by vacating; seller's cutting without damage is binding, with damage gives option.

The sale of fruit on trees involves unique considerations about ownership of growth after the contract and the method of delivery. This lesson clarifies that any new growth belongs to the seller, delivery is achieved by takhliya (vacating), and the seller's cutting of the fruit may affect the buyer's rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the sale of fruit on tree is valid with specific conditions. The act is the sale of fruit that is currently on the tree, not future fruit.
Future fruit is considered non-existent (ma'dum) and its sale is generally impermissible per al-Sarakhsi.
2
Locate the key principle on post-contract growth
Any fruit that grows after the contract belongs to the seller, not the buyer, regardless of whether the buyer allowed the fruit to remain on the tree.
This is based on the principle that one cannot sell what does not exist at the time of contract.
3
Understand the mode of delivery
Delivery (qabd) of fruit on trees is accomplished by takhliya—the seller vacating the property so the buyer can take possession, without physical handover.
This is specific to Hanafi fiqh; Shafi'i school requires actual physical possession.
4
Analyze the effect of seller's cutting
If the seller cuts the fruit in its proper season without causing any deficiency, the buyer has no option and must accept. If the cutting causes damage or deficiency, the buyer gets a price reduction and has the option to rescind the sale.
The cutting is considered a form of delivery if done properly.
5
Note exceptions and conditions
If the seller stipulates that he will cut the fruit, the sale is void. If the fruit perishes before possession, the price is reduced proportionally and the buyer has the option to cancel.
These rules protect the buyer from loss due to damage before possession.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا فرق في كون الخارج بعد العقد للبائع بين أن يكون الترك بإذن البائع أو بغير إذنه، والأصح ما ذهب إليه السرخسي من عدم الجواز في المعدوم وهو ظاهر المذهب؛ كذا في المعراج وفي الخانية: ويقدم بيع الأشجار ويؤخر الإجارة فإن قدم الإجارة لا يجوز لأن الأرض تكون مشغولة بأشجار الأجر قبل البيع فلا تصح الإجارة، وينبغي أن يشتري الأشجار بعد أصولها لهذا ولو باع أشجار البطيخ وأعار الأرض يجوز أيضاً إلا أن الإعارة لا تكون لازمة ويجوز له أن يرجع بعدها اهـ. وفي الولوالجية: لو اشترى التمر على رؤس النخيل فجذه على المشتري، وكذا لو اشترى الجزر فقلعه على المشتري اهـ. وتسليم الثمار على رؤوس الأشجار بالتخلية كما في البدائع وفي الحاوي: لو شرط قطع الثمرة على البائع فسد البيع .اهـ. وفي البدائع : إذا سـ الشجر صار بيعاً مقصوداً، سمى الثمر مع فلو هلك الثمر قبل القبض مطلقاً تسقط حصته من الثمن كالشجر وخير المشتري. ولو جذه البائع وهو قائم فإن جذه في حينه ولم ينقص فلا خيار ويقبضهما، ولو قبضهما بعد جذاذ البائع فوجد بأحدهما عيباً رد المعيب خاصة لأنه قبضهما متفرقين بخلاف ما إذا جذه المشتري بعد القبض ليس له أن يرد المعيب وحده لاجتماعهما عند البيع والقبض، وإن نقصه جذاذ البائع سقط عن المشتري حصة النقصان وله الخيار اهـ.
There is no difference in the post-contract growth belonging to the seller whether the leaving [on the tree] was with the seller's permission or without it. The most correct is what al-Sarakhsi held regarding the impermissibility [of sale] in the non-existent [thing], and that is the apparent [position] of the madhhab; as in al-Mi'raj. And in al-Khaniyya: The sale of trees is given precedence and the lease is postponed; if the lease is given precedence, it is not permissible because the land becomes occupied by the lease-holder's trees before the sale, so the lease is not valid. It is appropriate that he buy the trees after their roots for this [reason]. If he sold watermelon plants and lent the land [to the buyer], it is also permissible, except that the loan is not binding and he may revoke it thereafter. End quote. In al-Walwalijiyya: If he bought dates on the heads of palm trees, then [the seller] cuts them, it is upon the buyer [to take them]; likewise if he bought carrots and [the seller] pulled them out, it is upon the buyer. End quote. Delivery of fruits on treetops is by vacating (takhliya) as in al-Bada'i'. In al-Hawi: If he stipulates cutting of the fruit upon the seller, the sale is void. End quote. In al-Bada'i': If the tree is... [unclear: becomes a dedicated sale?], named the fruit along with it. If the fruit perishes before possession absolutely, its portion of the price falls [from the price] like the tree, and the buyer has the option. If the seller cuts it while it is standing, if he cuts it in its season and does not diminish [it], there is no option and he takes possession of both. If he takes possession after the seller's cutting and finds a defect in one of them, he returns the defective one only because he took possession of them separately, unlike if the buyer cuts after possession — he may not return the defective one alone because they were combined at the time of sale and possession. If the seller's cutting diminishes it, the portion of the deficiency falls from the buyer [i.e., price reduction] and he has the option. End quote.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings from various Hanafi sources on the sale of fruits on trees. The primary ruling is that any growth that appears after the contract of sale belongs to the seller, whether or not the buyer was given permission to leave it on the tree. Al-Sarakhsi's view that sale of a non-existent object (ma'dum) is impermissible is considered the most correct, and this is the apparent position of the Hanafi madhhab. The passage then addresses the order of transactions: if someone intends to both sell trees and lease land, the sale should precede the lease; otherwise, if the lease is done first, the land is already occupied by the lessee's trees, making the sale invalid. It is recommended to buy trees separately from their roots. Similarly, selling watermelon plants and lending the land is permissible, but the loan is not binding — the lender can revoke it. Next, the ruling on cutting (jadhdh) is discussed: if the seller cuts the fruit after sale, the buyer is obligated to take it. Delivery of fruit on trees is effected simply by vacating (takhliya), i.e., allowing the buyer access. If the seller stipulates that he will cut the fruit, the sale becomes void because it resembles an invalid condition. The passage further explains that if the fruit perishes before the buyer takes possession, regardless of how, the price is reduced proportionally, and the buyer has the option to cancel the sale. If the seller cuts the fruit in its proper season without causing any diminution in value or quality, the buyer has no option and must accept both fruit and tree. If the buyer finds a defect in one after taking possession, he may return only the defective item because the two were taken possession of at different times. However, if the buyer himself cuts the fruit after taking possession, he cannot return one alone because they were combined at sale and possession. If the seller's cutting causes the fruit to be deficient (e.g., unripe or damaged), the buyer gets a reduction in price and also has the option to rescind the sale.

Key Terms
التخلية (al-takhliya): Delivery by vacating the property so that the buyer can take possession, without physical handover.
الجذاذ (al-jadhdh): The act of cutting or picking fruit from the tree.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option of inspection — the buyer's right to cancel the sale upon seeing the item purchased sight unseen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller cuts fruit from the tree in its proper season without causing any deficiency, what is the buyer's right?
A) He has the option to reject the fruit.
B) He must accept the fruit and has no option.
C) He may demand a price reduction.
D) He may return only the fruit and keep the tree.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, post-contract growth on sold fruit belongs to the seller, delivery is by takhliya, and the seller's cutting of the fruit is binding if undamaged but gives the buyer options if damaged.
