Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
Validity of Sale for Underground Vegetables and Option of Inspection
Ruling on validity and khiyar al-ru'yah for hidden vegetables

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for a valid sale of underground vegetables and explain the establishment of khiyar al-ru'yah.

Core Idea
A sale of hidden crops is valid only if growth is known; buyer then has option to reject after seeing.

When buying vegetables that grow underground, the buyer cannot see the actual product at the time of contract. Hanafi fiqh provides a careful balance: the sale is valid if the growth is observable externally, but the buyer retains the right to cancel after seeing the actual produce. This lesson examines the conditions for validity and the scope of the option.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key contract elements
The buyer purchases underground vegetables unseen. Are the object and price specified?
Object is mughayyab (hidden) but growth may be known.
2
Determine if the object is known or unknown
If the vegetables have not yet sprouted or their growth is uncertain, the sale is invalid due to gharar. If they have sprouted and growth is known, the sale is valid.
Known growth means the seller can indicate the approximate size and quantity.
3
Apply the khiyar al-ru'yah rule
Once the sale is valid, the buyer has the option of inspection; he can cancel after seeing the actual produce.
This is based on the principle that an unseen item cannot be fully consented to.
4
Recognize the intra-madhhab disagreement
Abu Hanifa holds that the option continues until the buyer sees all the produce. His two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that seeing any part suffices; the fatwa is on their view.
This affects how the option is exercised in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية: رجل اشترى الثمار على رؤوس الأشجار فرأى من كل شجرة بعضها يثبت له خيار الرؤية حتى لو رضي بعده يلزمه، وإن باع ما هو مغيب في الأرض كالجزر والبصل وأصول الزعفران والثوم والشلجم والفجل إن باع بعدما ألقى في الأرض قبل النبات أو نبت إلا أنه غير معلوم لا يجوز البيع، فإن باع بعدما نبت نباتاً معلوماً يعلم وجوده تحت الأرض يجوز البيع ويكون مشترياً شيئاً لم يره عند أبي حنيفة، ثم لا يبطل خياره ما لم ير الكل ويرضى به، وعلى قول صاحبيه لا يتوقف خيار الرؤية على رؤية الكل وعليه الفتوى فإن كان مما يكال أو يوزن بعد القطع كالجزر والثوم والبصل، فإذا قلع البائع شيئاً من ذلك أو قلع المشتري بإذن البائع ينظر؛ إن كان المقلوع يباع بعد القطع بالكيل أو الوزن يثبت خيار الرؤية حتى لو رضي به يلزمه الكل، وإن رد بطل البيع. وإن كان المشتري قلعه بغير إذن البائع، فإن كان المقلوع شيئاً له قيمة لزمه الكل لأنه قبل القلع كان ينمو وبعد القلع لا ينمو والعيب الحادث عند المشتري يمنع الرد بخيار الرؤية، وإن كان المقلوع شيئاً يسيراً لا قيمة له لا يعتبر والقلع وعدمه سواء، وإن كان المغيب يباع بعد القلع عدداً كالفجل قطع البائع بعضه أو قلع المشتري بإذن البائع لا يلزمه ما لم ير الكل العدديات المتفاوتة بمنزلة الثياب والعبيد ونحو ذلك . وإن قلع المشتري بغير إذن البائع لزمه الكل إلا أن يكون ذلك شيئاً يسيراً، وإن اختصم البائع والمشتري قبل القلع فقال المشتري أخاف إن قلعته لا يصلح لي فيلزمني، وقال البائع أخاف إن قلعته لا ترضى به وترده فتتضرر بذلك يتطوع إنسان بالقلع وإلا يفسخ القاضي العقد بينهما .اهـ.
And in al-Khaniyya: A man bought fruits on the tops of trees and saw from each tree some of them — he has the option of inspection; so if he is satisfied after [seeing], it is binding on him. If he sold what is hidden in the ground, like carrots, onions, saffron roots, garlic, turnip, and radish: if he sold after sowing in the ground before [they] grow, or after they grow but [the growth] is unknown, the sale is not permissible. If he sold after the growth is known and recognizable such that one knows their existence under the ground, the sale is permissible and he is buying something he has not seen according to Abu Hanifa. Then his option does not lapse until he sees all and is satisfied with it. According to his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the option of inspection does not depend on seeing all, and the fatwa is upon that. If it is something that is sold by measure or weight after cutting, like carrots, garlic, and onions: if the seller pulls out some of it, or the buyer pulls out with the seller's permission, consideration is given: if the pulled-out [part] is sold after cutting by measure or weight, the option of inspection is established; so if he is satisfied with it, the whole is binding on him; if he rejects, the sale is void. If the buyer pulls it out without the seller's permission: if the pulled-out thing has value, the whole is binding on him because before pulling it was growing and after pulling it no longer grows, and the defect occurring at the buyer's hands prevents rejection by option of inspection. If the pulled-out thing is insignificant with no value, it is not considered and pulling or not pulling is the same. If the hidden [vegetable] is sold after cutting by count, like radish: if the seller cuts some of it or the buyer pulls with the seller's permission, it does not become binding until he sees all — counted items varying in quality are like garments and slaves and the like. If the buyer pulls without the seller's permission, the whole is binding on him unless it is something insignificant. If the seller and buyer dispute before pulling: the buyer says, 'I fear if I pull it, it will not be suitable for me and it will become binding,' and the seller says, 'I fear if you pull it, you will not be satisfied and you will reject it, causing me loss,' then someone volunteers to pull; otherwise the judge rescinds the contract between them. End quote.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This extensive passage from al-Khaniyya covers the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) for root vegetables that are hidden underground. The scenario begins with fruit on trees: if a buyer sees only part of the fruit from each tree, he has the option of inspection for the whole; if he is satisfied after seeing, the sale is binding. Then it addresses the sale of underground vegetables like carrots, onions, etc. If the seller sells them before they sprout or when their growth is unknown, the sale is invalid. But if they have grown visibly and their condition is known externally (though still underground), the sale is valid, and according to Abu Hanifa, the buyer's option of inspection continues until he sees all the produce and is satisfied. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the option does not require seeing all; the entire lot is considered one unit, and the fatwa follows their view. The passage further distinguishes between items sold by weight/measure and those sold by count. For weight/measure items (like carrots), if the seller or buyer (with permission) pulls up some of them, the option of inspection is established for that part; if the buyer is satisfied, the whole becomes binding; if he rejects, the entire sale is void. If the buyer pulls without permission and the pulled part has value, the whole becomes binding because the buyer is deemed to have accepted via his action, and the change (stopping growth) prevents return. Insignificant amounts do not affect the sale. For count-based items (like radish, where each piece varies), pulling some with permission does not make the whole binding until the buyer sees all; it is analogous to garments and slaves, where each unit is distinct. If the buyer pulls without permission, again the whole becomes binding unless the amount is trivial. Finally, the passage provides a dispute resolution: if before pulling the buyer fears the produce will not suit him and the seller fears the buyer will reject it, a neutral third party can pull out a sample; otherwise, the judge cancels the contract to avoid unfair loss.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The buyer's right to cancel the sale after seeing the purchased item if it was unseen at contract.
المغيب في الأرض (al-mughayyab fi al-ard): Things hidden in the ground, like root vegetables, whose exact condition is not visible.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a sale of underground vegetables valid?
A) Before the vegetables sprout, if the buyer knows the quantity.
B) After the vegetables sprout and their growth is known externally.
C) Only after the vegetables are fully grown and visible.
D) Only if the buyer has seen a sample before contract.

Key Takeaway
A sale of underground vegetables is valid only if the growth is known; thereafter, the buyer retains the option of inspection, which lapses according to the Sahibayn's view.

6 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Seeing All vs Seeing Some for Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on khiyar al-ru'yah scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding khiyar al-ru'yah for underground vegetables and identify which view is relied upon for fatwa.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa requires seeing all to lapse the option; the Sahibayn do not, and this is the operative fatwa.

Within the Hanafi school, there is a well-known disagreement on the scope of khiyar al-ru'yah for items that are not fully visible. Abu Hanifa insisted that the buyer must see every unit to be bound, while his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, ruled that seeing a part suffices. This lesson delves into this ikhtilaf and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
State the positions
Abu Hanifa: option continues until the buyer sees the entire lot. Sahibayn: option does not depend on seeing all; satisfaction after seeing any part binds the sale.
The dispute is about the extent of the object that triggers the lapse of the option.
2
Identify the evidence for each side
Abu Hanifa relies on the literal meaning of khiyar: you cannot truly accept what you have not fully seen. The Sahibayn consider the common practice: seeing a representative sample is customary.
The passage does not provide explicit proofs but implies through fatwa.
3
Analyze the tarjih
The text says 'وعليه الفتوى' (upon it is the fatwa), indicating that the Sahibayn's view is the relied-upon position.
This tarjih is based on necessity and ease in transactions.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, the buyer can inspect every single carrot; under the Sahibayn, inspecting a few suffices. The practical effect: time and efficiency in sale execution.
The mufta bihi view saves time and prevents disputes.

Source Text

What is the mufta bihi view regarding khiyar al-ru'yah for underground vegetables?
A) The buyer must see every unit for the option to lapse.
B) The buyer must see the entire lot, but can rely on a sample.

D) The option lapses only when the seller confirms the quality.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

10 min
Effects of Cutting by Seller or Buyer on Khiyar al-Ru'yah
Rulings on cutting by parties and dispute resolution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether the sale becomes binding when the buyer or seller pulls some of the underground produce, and explain the rules for weight/measure versus count items.

Core Idea
Pulling with permission preserves the option; pulling without permission (if valuable) makes the sale binding; insignificant amounts are ignored.

Once the buyer has the option of inspection, any action that removes the produce from the ground can affect this right. The Hanafi school distinguishes between permission and non-permission, and also between items sold by weight/measure and those sold by count. This lesson examines these detailed rules and the special dispute resolution mechanism.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between pulling with and without permission
If the seller pulls or the buyer pulls with the seller's permission, the option remains; the buyer can still reject. If the buyer pulls without permission, and the pulled part has value, the whole sale becomes binding.
Unauthorized pulling is treated as acceptance by action.
2
Consider the type of sale: weight/measure vs count
For weight/measure items (like carrots), pulling some with permission establishes the option for that part; satisfaction binds the whole, rejection voids the whole. For count items (like radish, each varying), pulling some with permission does not bind the whole until the buyer sees all.
Count items are treated like garments: each unit is distinct.
3
Apply the rule of insignificant amounts
If the pulled part has no value (very small), it does not affect the sale; pulling is disregarded.
This prevents trivial actions from changing the ruling.
4
Analyze the dispute resolution mechanism
If before pulling, the buyer fears the produce will not suit him and the seller fears rejection, a neutral third party can pull a sample. If no one volunteers, the judge cancels the contract.
This ensures neither party is forced into an unfair position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that the option does not require seeing all; the entire lot is considered one unit, and the fatwa follows their view. The passage further distinguishes between items sold by weight/measure and those sold by count. For weight/measure items (like carrots), if the seller or buyer (with permission) pulls up some of them, the option of inspection is established for that part; if the buyer is satisfied, the whole becomes binding; if he rejects, the entire sale is void. If the buyer pulls without permission and the pulled part has value, the whole becomes binding because the buyer is deemed to have accepted via his action, and the change (stopping growth) prevents return. Insignificant amounts do not affect the sale. For count-based items (like radish, where each piece varies), pulling some with permission does not make the whole binding until the buyer sees all; it is analogous to garments and slaves, where each unit is distinct. If the buyer pulls without permission, again the whole becomes binding unless the amount is trivial. Finally, the passage provides a dispute resolution: if before pulling the buyer fears the produce will not suit him and the seller fears the buyer will reject it, a neutral third party can pull out a sample; otherwise, the judge cancels the contract to avoid unfair loss.

Key Terms
العدديات المتفاوتة (al-adadiyyat al-mutafawita): Items sold by count that vary in value individually, like garments, slaves, radishes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer purchases carrots (sold by weight) and without permission pulls up a large, valuable carrot, what is the effect?
A) The buyer can still reject the entire sale.
B) The buyer must purchase only that carrot.
C) The entire sale becomes binding on the buyer.
D) The sale is void because of the unauthorized action.

Key Takeaway
Unauthorized pulling of a valuable amount of produce makes the entire sale binding, but small insignificant amounts do not affect the option of inspection.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale of Leaves and Continuously Growing Vegetables
Rulings on sale of leaves and continuously growing vegetables.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on sale of leaves by specifying customary cutting point, and distinguish permissible from impermissible vegetable sales.

Core Idea
Sale of continuously growing vegetables is invalid due to uncertainty; custom ('urf) can make leaf sales valid.

In this lesson, we examine several rulings from al-Qunyah and al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya on the sale of leaves and vegetables. The key principle is that the object of sale must be known and deliverable. Leaves that can be cut at a customary point are saleable, but vegetables that grow hour by hour involve excessive uncertainty (gharar) and are invalid, unless there is a custom (al-ta'amul) to the contrary, as with leek.

Guided Path
1
Identify the item being sold
Determine whether the item is leaves (e.g., garlic leaves, mulberry leaves) or a continuously growing vegetable (e.g., fresh greens, cucumber). The ruling differs accordingly.
The passage distinguishes between items that grow from the top (stalks) and those that grow continuously.
2
Check if the cutting place is defined or known by custom
For leaves, if the place of cutting is known by custom ('urf), the sale is valid. For garlic leaves, the time of cutting had passed, so the buyer cannot reclaim the price.
Custom plays a key role in determining validity.
3
Apply the rule for continuously growing vegetables
Vegetables that grow hour by hour (like lettuce, cucumber) are analogous to wool on a living animal; sale is invalid because of uncertainty in quantity.
Exception: leek (kurrath) is valid due to common practice (al-ta'amul).
4
Examine the ruling for stalks (qawa'im al-khilaf)
Stalks that grow from the top are permissible because their growth is limited and the amount is known.
This distinguishes them from continuously growing herbs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : اشترى أوراق الثوم ولم يبين موضع القطع وكان موضع قطعها معلوماً ومضى وقتها ليس للمشتري يسترد الثمن اشترى أوراق التوت ولم يبين موضع القطع لكنه معلوم عرفاً صح، ولو ترك الأغصان فله أن يقطعها في السنة الثانية، ولو تركها مدة ثم أراد قطعها فله ذلك إن لم يضر ذلك بالشجرة، ولو باع أوراق توت لم تقطع قبله بسنة يجوز ، وبسنتين لا يجوز لأنه بسنة يعلم موضع قطعها عرفاً باع أوراق التوت دون ثمر التوت صح. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : اشترى رطبة من البقول أو قثاء وشيئاً ينمو ساعة فساعة لا يجوز كبيع الصوف، وبيع قوائم الخلاف يجوز وإن كان ينمو لأن نموها من الأعلى بخلاف الرطبات إلا الكراث للتعامل وما لا تعامل فيه لا يجوز اهـ. وفي المنتقى وبيع الحصرم أو التفاح قبل الإدراك جائز لأنه ينتفع به، والخوخ والكمثرى ونحوها غير جائز ، وإن كان ثمر بعض الأشجار مدركاً دون البعض جاز في المدرك دون غيره تين قد أدرك بعضه دون البعض ؛ إن باع الموجود منه جاز، فإن لم يقبضها المشتري حتى خرج الباقي فسد البيع، وينبغي أن يكون تعريفاً على القول الضعيف المشترط لبدو الصلاح وفيه من سرق ماء فسقى أرضه أو كرمه يطيب له ما خرج كما لو غصب شعيراً أو تبناً وسمن به دابته فيطيب له ما زاد في الدابة فعليه قيمة العلف اهـ قوله : (ويقطعها المشتري تفريغاً لملك البائع) وقدمنا أن أجرة القطع على المشتري وأن تسليم الثمرة بالتخلية قوله : (وإن شرط تركها على النخل (فسد أي البيع لما قدمنا أنه محل النهي عن بيع الثمار قبل بدو صلاحها، ولأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وهو شغل ملك الغير أو لأنه صفقة في صفقة لأنه إجارة في بيع إن كان للمنفعة حصة من الثمن أو إعارة في بيع إن لم يكن لها من الثمن. وتعقبهم في النهاية بأنكم قلتم إن كلاً من الإجارة والإعارة غير صحيح فكيف يقال إنه صفقة في صفقة؟ وجوابه أنه صفقة فاسدة في صفقة صحيحة ففسدتا جميعاً،
And in al-Qunyah: He bought leaves of garlic without specifying the place of cutting, but the place of cutting was known and its time had passed — the buyer may not reclaim the price. He bought leaves of mulberry without specifying the place of cutting, but it is known by custom — valid. If he left the branches, he may cut them in the second year. If he left them for a time then wanted to cut them, he may do so if that does not harm the tree. If he sold mulberry leaves that had not been cut for a year before, it is permissible; for two years, not permissible because by one year the place of cutting is known by custom. He sold mulberry leaves without the mulberries — valid. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: He bought a fresh vegetable (rutba) of greens or cucumber or something that grows hour by hour — not permissible, like sale of wool. Sale of stalks of... [khilaf?] is permissible even though they grow because their growth is from the top, unlike fresh vegetables except leek due to custom; what is not customarily dealt with is not permissible. End quote. In al-Muntaqa: Sale of unripe grapes (hisrim) or apples before ripening is permissible because one benefits from them. Peaches, pears and the like are not permissible. If the fruit of some trees is ripe and others not, it is permissible for the ripe and not for the others. For figs that have ripened partially: if he sells what is present, it is permissible; if the buyer does not take possession until the rest appears, the sale becomes invalid. It should be a definition according to the weak opinion that stipulates the beginning of ripeness. And therein: Whoever steals water and irrigates his land or vineyard thereby, what comes out is permissible for him, just as if he usurped barley or straw and fattened his animal, what increases in the animal is permissible for him, and he owes the value of the fodder. End quote. His saying: (And the buyer cuts them to vacate the seller's property.) And we have previously presented that the cutting fee is on the buyer and that delivery of the fruit is by vacating. His saying: (And if he stipulates leaving them on the palm trees, the sale is invalid) because we have previously mentioned that it is the subject of the prohibition of selling fruits before the beginning of ripeness, and because it is a condition not required by the contract, and it is occupying another's property, or because it is a package deal within a package deal: it is a lease within a sale if the benefit has a portion of the price, or a loan within a sale if it has no portion of the price. They objected in al-Nihaya: You (Hanafis) say that both lease and loan are invalid, so how can it be said that it is a package deal within a package deal? The answer is that it is an invalid package deal within a valid package deal, so both become invalid.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage collects several distinct rulings from different sources, all under the topic of sale of agricultural products. First, from al-Qunyah: sale of garlic leaves without specifying where to cut is valid if the customary cutting point is known and the time has passed; the buyer cannot reclaim the price. Similarly, mulberry leaves sold without specifying cutting place but known by custom are valid. If branches are left, the buyer may cut them in the second year, or later if it does not harm the tree. Leaves not cut for one year can be sold; for two years, they cannot because the customary cutting point is lost. Selling mulberry leaves without the fruit is valid. Second, from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: fresh greens that grow constantly (like lettuce, cucumber) cannot be sold because their growth is continuous and uncertain; this is analogous to sale of wool on a living animal, which is not permissible. However, sale of stalks (like those of a tree that regenerate from the top) is permissible because growth is limited. The exception is leek (kurrath) because it is commonly traded. Third, from al-Muntaqa: unripe grapes and apples may be sold because they are usable (e.g., for making verjuice or cooking); but peaches and pears that are not usable before ripening cannot be sold. If some trees have ripe fruit and others not, only the ripe can be sold. For figs that ripen partially, if the seller sells only what is ripe, it is valid; but if the buyer delays possession until the rest ripens, the sale becomes invalid. The passage also includes a note on stolen water: if someone steals water and irrigates his land, the produce is lawful for him, similar to a usurper who feeds an animal — the increase is lawful, but he must pay compensation. Finally, the commentary returns to the ruling that if the seller stipulates that the fruit remain on the tree after sale (e.g., for the seller to keep using the tree), the sale is invalid for two reasons: it is a prohibition of selling fruit before ripening, and it is a condition that occupies another's property without right. Alternatively, it is considered a package deal: either a lease (if the benefit has a portion of the price) or a loan (if not) combined with a sale. Since both lease and loans (for a condition in a sale) are invalid in Hanafi law, the entire sale is void. The objection from al-Nihaya is answered: the package is one invalid (the condition) and one valid (the sale), but they taint each other.

Key Terms
التعامل (al-ta'amul): Common practice or custom in transactions; used here to justify exception for leek.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the sale of cucumber (qiththa') while still growing not permissible?
A) Because cucumber is not beneficial before ripening.
B) Because it grows hour by hour, causing uncertainty.
C) Because cucumber is specifically prohibited by hadith.
D) Because custom prohibits it.

Key Takeaway
Sale of continuously growing vegetables is invalid due to uncertainty, but custom can make an exception, as with leek.

Sale of Unripe Fruits Before Ripeness
Rulings on sale of unripe fruits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fruits that may be sold before full ripeness and those that may not, based on their usability.

Core Idea
Unripe fruits that are still usable (e.g., for cooking) can be sold; those with no benefit before ripening cannot.

This lesson examines the ruling on selling fruits before they are fully ripe. The key criterion is whether the fruit has any beneficial use at the time of sale. Grapes (for verjuice) and apples (for cooking) are permissible, while peaches and pears, which are only enjoyable when ripe, are not. The passage also discusses partial ripeness and the effect of delay in taking possession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the fruit type
Determine whether the fruit is usable before full ripeness (e.g., grapes for verjuice, apples for cooking) or not (e.g., peaches, pears).
Usability is the decisive factor.
2
Apply the rule for unripe fruit
If the fruit is beneficial before ripeness, sale is permissible. Otherwise, it is not.
This differs from the general prohibition of selling fruit before buduw al-salah.
3
Handle partial ripeness
If only some trees are ripe, only those can be sold. For fruits like figs that ripen in stages, selling the ripe part is valid only if the buyer takes possession before the rest ripens.
Delay in possession can invalidate the sale.
4
Note the weak opinion
The passage mentions that the requirement of buduw al-salah is a weak opinion; the relied-upon view is based on usability.
This explains why some unripe fruits are permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بدو الصلاح (buduw al-salah): The beginning of ripeness of fruit, a condition for valid sale of fruit on trees.

Why is selling unripe peaches not permissible according to al-Muntaqa?

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Effect of Stipulating to Leave Fruit on Tree After Sale
Ruling on stipulating to leave fruit on tree invalidates the sale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a condition to leave fruit on the tree invalidates the sale, using the arguments based on prohibition of selling before ripeness, occupying another's property, and package deal (safqa fi safqa).

Core Idea
Stipulating that fruit remain on the seller's tree after sale invalidates the contract because it combines a sale with an invalid condition.

This lesson examines the critical ruling on a common practice: when a buyer agrees to purchase fruit but allows the seller to keep it on the tree for a period. The Hanafi school holds such a stipulation invalidates the sale entirely. We explore the multiple legal arguments: the prohibition of selling fruit before ripeness, the condition being outside the contract, and the combination of sale with lease or loan (safqa fi safqa). The passage also addresses an objection from al-Nihaya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the stipulation
The seller stipulates that the buyer leave the fruit on the tree after purchase, meaning the seller continues to benefit from the tree.
This is the condition in question.
2
Apply the first reason: prohibition of selling before ripeness
If the fruit is not yet ripe, the sale is invalid because it falls under the prohibition of selling fruit before buduw al-salah.
This reason applies only if the fruit is unripe at sale.
3
Apply the second reason: condition not required by the contract
The condition of leaving fruit occupies the seller's property without right; it is not part of the normal sale.
Any extra benefit for the seller without compensation is problematic.
4
Apply the third reason: safqa fi safqa (package deal)
The stipulation effectively adds either a lease (if the benefit has a price) or a loan (if not) to the sale, making it a combination of contracts.
Since both lease and loan are invalid when combined with a sale in this manner, the sale is void.
5
Address the objection from al-Nihaya
The objection is that if lease and loan are individually invalid, how can they be combined with a sale? The response is that it is an invalid package within a valid package, corrupting the whole.
The Hanafi position is that combining an invalid condition with a valid sale invalidates the entire contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي القنية : اشترى أوراق الثوم ولم يبين موضع القطع وكان موضع قطعها معلوماً ومضى وقتها ليس للمشتري يسترد الثمن اشترى أوراق التوت ولم يبين موضع القطع لكنه معلوم عرفاً صح، ولو ترك الأغصان فله أن يقطعها في السنة الثانية، ولو تركها مدة ثم أراد قطعها فله ذلك إن لم يضر ذلك بالشجرة، ولو باع أوراق توت لم تقطع قبله بسنة يجوز ، وبسنتين لا يجوز لأنه بسنة يعلم موضع قطعها عرفاً باع أوراق التوت دون ثمر التوت صح. وفي الفتاوى الظهيرية : اشترى رطبة من البقول أو قثاء وشيئاً ينمو ساعة فساعة لا يجوز كبيع الصوف، وبيع قوائم الخلاف يجوز وإن كان ينمو لأن نموها من الأعلى بخلاف الرطبات إلا الكراث للتعامل وما لا تعامل فيه لا يجوز اهـ. وفي المنتقى وبيع الحصرم أو التفاح قبل الإدراك جائز لأنه ينتفع به، والخوخ والكمثرى ونحوها غير جائز ، وإن كان ثمر بعض الأشجار مدركاً دون البعض جاز في المدرك دون غيره تين قد أدرك بعضه دون البعض ؛ إن باع الموجود منه جاز، فإن لم يقبضها المشتري حتى خرج الباقي فسد البيع، وينبغي أن يكون تعريفاً على القول الضعيف المشترط لبدو الصلاح وفيه من سرق ماء فسقى أرضه أو كرمه يطيب له ما خرج كما لو غصب شعيراً أو تبناً وسمن به دابته فيطيب له ما زاد في الدابة فعليه قيمة العلف اهـ قوله : (ويقطعها المشتري تفريغاً لملك البائع) وقدمنا أن أجرة القطع على المشتري وأن تسليم الثمرة بالتخلية قوله : (وإن شرط تركها على النخل (فسد أي البيع لما قدمنا أنه محل النهي عن بيع الثمار قبل بدو صلاحها، ولأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وهو شغل ملك الغير أو لأنه صفقة في صفقة لأنه إجارة في بيع إن كان للمنفعة حصة من الثمن أو إعارة في بيع إن لم يكن لها من الثمن. وتعقبهم في النهاية بأنكم قلتم إن كلاً من الإجارة والإعارة غير صحيح فكيف يقال إنه صفقة في صفقة؟ وجوابه أنه صفقة فاسدة في صفقة صحيحة ففسدتا جميعاً،
And in al-Qunyah: He bought leaves of garlic without specifying the place of cutting, but the place of cutting was known and its time had passed — the buyer may not reclaim the price. He bought leaves of mulberry without specifying the place of cutting, but it is known by custom — valid. If he left the branches, he may cut them in the second year. If he left them for a time then wanted to cut them, he may do so if that does not harm the tree. If he sold mulberry leaves that had not been cut for a year before, it is permissible; for two years, not permissible because by one year the place of cutting is known by custom. He sold mulberry leaves without the mulberries — valid. In al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: He bought a fresh vegetable (rutba) of greens or cucumber or something that grows hour by hour — not permissible, like sale of wool. Sale of stalks of... [khilaf?] is permissible even though they grow because their growth is from the top, unlike fresh vegetables except leek due to custom; what is not customarily dealt with is not permissible. End quote. In al-Muntaqa: Sale of unripe grapes (hisrim) or apples before ripening is permissible because one benefits from them. Peaches, pears and the like are not permissible. If the fruit of some trees is ripe and others not, it is permissible for the ripe and not for the others. For figs that have ripened partially: if he sells what is present, it is permissible; if the buyer does not take possession until the rest appears, the sale becomes invalid. It should be a definition according to the weak opinion that stipulates the beginning of ripeness. And therein: Whoever steals water and irrigates his land or vineyard thereby, what comes out is permissible for him, just as if he usurped barley or straw and fattened his animal, what increases in the animal is permissible for him, and he owes the value of the fodder. End quote. His saying: (And the buyer cuts them to vacate the seller's property.) And we have previously presented that the cutting fee is on the buyer and that delivery of the fruit is by vacating. His saying: (And if he stipulates leaving them on the palm trees, the sale is invalid) because we have previously mentioned that it is the subject of the prohibition of selling fruits before the beginning of ripeness, and because it is a condition not required by the contract, and it is occupying another's property, or because it is a package deal within a package deal: it is a lease within a sale if the benefit has a portion of the price, or a loan within a sale if it has no portion of the price. They objected in al-Nihaya: You (Hanafis) say that both lease and loan are invalid, so how can it be said that it is a package deal within a package deal? The answer is that it is an invalid package deal within a valid package deal, so both become invalid.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage collects several distinct rulings from different sources, all under the topic of sale of agricultural products. First, from al-Qunyah: sale of garlic leaves without specifying where to cut is valid if the customary cutting point is known and the time has passed; the buyer cannot reclaim the price. Similarly, mulberry leaves sold without specifying cutting place but known by custom are valid. If branches are left, the buyer may cut them in the second year, or later if it does not harm the tree. Leaves not cut for one year can be sold; for two years, they cannot because the customary cutting point is lost. Selling mulberry leaves without the fruit is valid. Second, from al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya: fresh greens that grow constantly (like lettuce, cucumber) cannot be sold because their growth is continuous and uncertain; this is analogous to sale of wool on a living animal, which is not permissible. However, sale of stalks (like those of a tree that regenerate from the top) is permissible because growth is limited. The exception is leek (kurrath) because it is commonly traded. Third, from al-Muntaqa: unripe grapes and apples may be sold because they are usable (e.g., for making verjuice or cooking); but peaches and pears that are not usable before ripening cannot be sold. If some trees have ripe fruit and others not, only the ripe can be sold. For figs that ripen partially, if the seller sells only what is ripe, it is valid; but if the buyer delays possession until the rest ripens, the sale becomes invalid. The passage also includes a note on stolen water: if someone steals water and irrigates his land, the produce is lawful for him, similar to a usurper who feeds an animal — the increase is lawful, but he must pay compensation. Finally, the commentary returns to the ruling that if the seller stipulates that the fruit remain on the tree after sale (e.g., for the seller to keep using the tree), the sale is invalid for two reasons: it is a prohibition of selling fruit before ripening, and it is a condition that occupies another's property without right. Alternatively, it is considered a package deal: either a lease (if the benefit has a portion of the price) or a loan (if not) combined with a sale. Since both lease and loans (for a condition in a sale) are invalid in Hanafi law, the entire sale is void. The objection from al-Nihaya is answered: the package is one invalid (the condition) and one valid (the sale), but they taint each other.

Key Terms
صفقة في صفقة (safqa fi safqa): A package deal combining two contracts in one, often leading to invalid combinations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi ruling on a sale where the buyer stipulates that the seller may leave the fruit on the tree after purchase?
A) The sale is valid because the buyer owns the fruit.
B) The sale is valid if the fruit is ripe.
C) The sale is invalid (fasid).
D) The sale is valid if the seller pays rent.

Key Takeaway
A condition that gives the seller extra benefit after sale, such as leaving fruit on the tree, invalidates the sale due to being a prohibited package deal or an extra condition.

Ruling on Khiyar al-Ru'ya and Buyer Cutting the Item
States ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya and buyer cutting the purchased item

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a buyer cuts a purchased item before inspection under khiyar al-ru'ya, and apply the distinction between valuable and insignificant portions.

Core Idea
Cutting a valuable part of an unseen item without permission forfeits the right to reject under khiyar al-ru'ya.

This lesson examines a specific scenario within khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection). When a buyer cuts a portion of the purchased item before fully inspecting it, the ruling depends on the value of the cut part and the permission of the seller. Understanding this helps clarify when the option is lost and the sale becomes binding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the option involved
Recognize that the buyer has purchased an unseen item and has the option to inspect and either accept or reject it upon seeing it. The act of cutting is a physical alteration of the item.
Khiyar al-ru'ya is established for items sold without being seen.
2
Determine the value of the cut portion
If the cut portion is something of value (e.g., a significant part of a fruit), the buyer is deemed to have accepted the whole because cutting prevents further growth and introduces a defect. If it is insignificant (no value), cutting does not affect the option.
The text uses 'shay'an la qimata lahu' (something with no value).
3
Consider the seller's permission
If the buyer cuts with the seller's permission, the option is not lost; the buyer may still reject after seeing the whole. Without permission, the ruling hinges on value.
This applies also when the seller cuts a portion of items sold by count.
4
Apply the ruling on items sold by weight/measure vs count
For items sold by weight or measure, the entire lot is bound by the option. For items sold by count (e.g., radishes, garments), cutting a part does not bind the buyer until they see the whole, unless the buyer cuts without permission.
The text mentions 'ghayr al-maghayyab yuba' ba'da al-qal' adadan' (hidden things sold after cutting by count).
5
State the exception for insignificant cutting
If the cut portion is trivial and valueless, it is ignored, and the option remains intact as if no cutting occurred.
The rule is based on the principle that cutting a valuable part constitutes a change that prevents return.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدخل تحت الكيل أو الوزن يثبت خيار الرؤية حتى لو رضي به يلزمه الكل، وإن رد بطل البيع. وإن كان المشتري قلعه بغير إذن البائع، فإن كان المقلوع شيئاً له قيمة لزمه الكل لأنه قبل القلع كان ينمو وبعد القلع لا ينمو والعيب الحادث عند المشتري يمنع الرد بخيار الرؤية، وإن كان المقلوع شيئاً يسيراً لا قيمة له لا يعتبر والقلع وعدمه سواء، وإن كان المغيب يباع بعد القلع عدداً كالفجل قطع البائع بعضه أو قلع المشتري بإذن البائع لا يلزمه ما لم ير الكل العدديات المتفاوتة بمنزلة الثياب والعبيد ونحو ذلك . وإن قلع المشتري بغير إذن البائع لزمه الكل إلا أن يكون ذلك شيئاً يسيراً.
That which falls under measurement or weight, the option of inspection is established, such that if he is satisfied with it, the whole is binding on him, and if he rejects it, the sale is void. And if the buyer cuts it without the seller's permission, then if the cut portion is something of value, the whole is binding on him because before cutting it was growing and after cutting it does not grow, and the defect occurring with the buyer prevents rejection by the option of inspection. And if the cut portion is something insignificant with no value, it is not considered, and cutting or not cutting are the same. And if the hidden thing is sold after cutting by count, like radishes, the seller cutting some or the buyer cutting with permission does not bind him until he sees the whole; items differing by count are like garments and slaves and the like. And if the buyer cuts without the seller's permission, the whole is binding on him unless it is something insignificant.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the sharh explaining the effects of khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection) in the Hanafi madhhab. It covers scenarios where the buyer cuts part of the purchased item before full inspection. The key principle: if the buyer cuts a valuable portion without permission, he is deemed to have accepted the whole and loses the right of rejection. However, if the cut portion is worthless, it does not affect the option. The passage also distinguishes between items sold by measurement/weight versus by count, with specific rules for each. The reasoning is that cutting a growing item halts its growth, constituting a change that prevents return.

Key Terms
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'ya): The buyer's option to inspect an unseen item and either accept or reject it upon seeing it.
قلع (qal'): The act of cutting or removing a purchased item (e.g., fruit, root crop) from its place.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-ru'ya, when does cutting an item without permission make the sale binding?
A) When the cut portion is of any size, even insignificant.
B) When the cut portion has value and the item is sold by weight or measure.
C) Only when the seller cuts the item.
D) When the buyer cuts with permission.

Key Takeaway
The buyer's right to reject an unseen item under khiyar al-ru'ya is lost if he cuts a valuable portion without the seller's permission, as this is considered acceptance of the whole.

Condition of Leaving Fruit on Tree – Invalidity and Istihsan Exception
Ruling with proof: invalidity of condition to leave fruit on tree.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stipulating to leave fruit on the tree after sale, explain the proofs for its invalidity, and apply the istihsan exception when the fruit has reached full growth.

Core Idea
A condition to leave fruit on the tree turns the sale into a transaction within a transaction, making it invalid unless custom excuses.

In this passage, the commentator explains why a sale becomes invalid when the buyer stipulates that the fruit remain on the tree for a period. The ruling is grounded in the prohibition of selling fruit before ripeness and the principle of mixing a sale with a lease or loan. Yet, Imam Muhammad introduces a nuanced exception based on custom (urf) when the fruit is fully grown, a view that becomes the relied-upon position in the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The act is a sale of fruit with a condition that the fruit remains on the tree. The default Hanafi ruling is that the sale is fasid (invalid).
Invalidation is due to the condition being foreign to the contract and involving another's property.
2
Examine the proofs cited
The text gives two proofs: (1) analogy to the prohibited sale before ripening, and (2) the condition makes it a 'transaction within a transaction' (safqah fi safqah) – either a lease or a loan combined with a sale.
Both proofs show how the condition corrupts the contract.
3
Analyze the objection and its answer
An objection from al-Nihaya: if the ancillary contract (lease/loan) is itself invalid, how can it be part of a transaction? The answer: the invalid ancillary contracted is combined with a valid sale, corrupting both.
This clarifies that the entire contract becomes fasid.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Muhammad (with Abu Yusuf) holds that if the fruit has reached full growth, the condition is excused by custom and does not invalidate. Abu Hanifa holds it invalidates regardless.
This is an example of istihsan overriding qiyas.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi is the view of Muhammad (and Abu Yusuf), as recorded in al-Asrar, al-Muntqa, and al-Tuhfa.
Thus the operative ruling today: condition invalid unless fruit is fully grown and custom permits.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن شرط تركها على النخل فسد أي البيع لما قدمنا أنه محل النهي عن بيع الثمار قبل بدو صلاحها، ولأنه شرط لا يقتضيه العقد وهو شغل ملك الغير أو لأنه صفقة في صفقة لأنه إجارة في بيع إن كان للمنفعة حصة من الثمن أو إعارة في بيع إن لم يكن لها من الثمن. وتعقبهم في النهاية بأنكم قلتم إن كلاً من الإجارة والإعارة غير صحيح فكيف يقال إنه صفقة في صفقة؟ وجوابه أنه صفقة فاسدة في صفقة صحيحة ففسدتا جميعاً. وكذا لو شرط ترك الزرع على الأرض لما قلنا أطلقه فشمل ما إذا تناهى عظمهما أو لا، وفي الأول خلاف محمد فإنه يقول : استحسن أن لا يفسد بشرط الترك للعادة بخلاف ما إذا لم يتناه لأنه شرط فيه الجزء المعدوم وهو ما يزداد بمعنى في الأرض والشجر. وفي الأسرار : الفتوى على قول محمد وبه أخذ الطحاوي وفي المتنقى : ضم إليه أبا يوسف. وفي التحفة : والصحيح قولهما.
And if he stipulates leaving them on the palm trees, the sale is invalid due to what we previously mentioned that it is the subject of the prohibition on selling fruits before their ripeness becomes apparent, and because it is a condition not required by the contract and it involves occupying the property of another, or because it is a transaction within a transaction: either a lease within a sale if the benefit has a portion of the price, or a loan within a sale if it has no portion of the price. They objected in al-Nihaya: 'You said that both lease and loan are invalid, so how can it be said that it is a transaction within a transaction?' The answer is that it is an invalid transaction within a valid transaction, so both become invalid. Similarly, if he stipulates leaving the crop on the land due to what we said; he made it general to include whether they have reached full growth or not. In the first case, there is a difference of opinion from Muhammad, for he says: I consider it commendable (istihsan) that it should not invalidate due to the condition of leaving based on custom, unlike when they have not reached full growth because it involves a condition on a non-existent part, which is the increase in the land and trees. In al-Asrar: The fatwa is on the saying of Muhammad, and al-Tahawi adopted it, and in al-Muntqa: Abu Yusuf is joined with him. In al-Tuhfa: The correct view is the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, discussing a condition in the sale of fruit or crops that they be left on the trees or land for a period. The Hanafi position is that such a condition invalidates the sale because it resembles a prohibited sale before ripening and because it mixes a sale with a lease or loan, which is a 'transaction within a transaction' (صفقة في صفقة). The objection raised is that if such ancillary contracts are themselves invalid, how can they be part of a transaction? The answer is that the invalid ancillary invalidates the entire contract. The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: Imam Muhammad holds an istihsan view that if the fruit/crop has reached full growth, custom allows the condition and does not invalidate the sale. The preferred fatwa is according to Muhammad, often joined by Abu Yusuf. This shows the school's flexibility based on custom.

Key Terms
شرط ترك (shart tark): A condition in the sale contract that the sold item (fruit/crop) remains on the tree/land for a period.
صفقة في صفقة (safqah fi safqah): A transaction within another transaction; the Hanafi legal term for a contract combining two different types of transactions (sale and lease/loan).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi), when does a condition to leave fruit on the tree after sale NOT invalidate the sale?
A) Always, because custom allows it.
B) Never, because it is a transaction within a transaction.
C) Only if the fruit has reached full growth and custom requires it.
D) Only if the buyer pays extra for the benefit.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school invalidates a sale condition to leave fruit on the tree unless the fruit is fully grown and custom excuses it, according to the mufta bihi of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Legal Stratagem for Renting Trees with Fruit
Explains a legal stratagem for renting trees with fruit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal stratagem (hilah) for renting trees with fruit and distinguish the permissible structure from the impermissible one.

Core Idea
Renting trees with fruit is impermissible; the hilah uses separate ownership and a binding loan.

In Hanafi fiqh, renting a tree that bears fruit is not allowed because fruit before ripening cannot be sold or rented. However, a legal stratagem (hilah) permits a similar outcome by restructuring the transaction. This lesson unpacks that stratagem as explained by classical Hanafi jurists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Prohibited Transaction
Determine why renting a tree with fruit is impermissible: the fruit is not yet ripe and its sale or rental is prohibited under the rules of gharar and sale of non-existent goods.
The passage assumes this prohibition is known from other texts.
2
Describe the Hilah Structure
The stratagem requires writing a binding right (haqq lazim) for the buyer to leave the fruit on the tree until ripening, while the tree and fruit belong to different owners. This transforms the transaction from rent (ijara) to a loan (i'arah).
Key phrase: 'by a binding command that the fruits and trees belong to another'.
3
Check Conditions of the Hilah
Ensure that (1) the fruit and tree have separate owners, (2) the right to leave fruit is binding, and (3) no rent is charged. If these hold, the arrangement is valid.
The Hilah relies on the absence of rent; otherwise it reverts to impermissible ijara.
4
Distinguish from Impermissible Alternatives
If the buyer refuses to grant a loan, the seller has two options: annul the sale or cut the fruit. Renting the tree from the buyer is explicitly forbidden.
Jami' al-Fusulayn: 'if he then rents the tree... that is not permissible'.
5
Apply the Resolution
In practice, the seller (original owner of fruit) can leave the fruit on the tree without paying rent, using the binding right. This avoids the prohibition while achieving the desired result.
The hilah is a legitimate tool in Hanafi law to overcome strict prohibitions when the underlying objective is lawful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو أراد إجارة الأشجار والكروم فالحيلة فيه أن يكتب أن لهذا المشتري حق ترك الثمار على الأشجار في مدة كذا بأمر لازم أن تكون الثمار والأشجار لآخر، وله حق الترك فيها إلى وقت الإدراك، فإذا ذكر هذا حمل على أنه بحق لازم؛ كذا في شرح ظهير الدين المرغيناني اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين : باع شجراً عليه ثمر وكرماً فيه عنب لا يدخل الثمر، فلو استأجر الشجر من المشتري ليترك عليه الثمر لم يجز ولكن يعار إلى الإدراك، فلو أبى المشتري يخير البائع إن شاء أبطل البيع أو قطع الثمر، ولو باع أرضاً بدون الزرع فهو للبائع بأجر مثلها إلى الإدراك اهـ.
If one wishes to rent trees and vineyards, the legal stratagem (hilah) for it is to write that this buyer has the right to leave the fruits on the trees for a certain period by a binding command that the fruits and trees belong to another, and he has the right to leave them there until the time of ripening. So if this is mentioned, it is taken as a binding right. Thus in Sharh Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: He sold a tree with fruit on it, or a vine with grapes, and the fruit is not included; if he then rents the tree from the buyer to leave the fruit on it, that is not permissible, but he may lend it (i'arah) until ripening. If the buyer refuses, the seller has the option: either annul the sale or cut the fruit. And if he sold land without the crop, it belongs to the seller with rent of its like until ripening.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage presents a hilah (legal stratagem) from Hanafi jurists to circumvent the prohibition of renting trees that bear fruit, since the sale of fruit before ripening is prohibited. The solution is to have the trees and fruit owned by different persons, with a binding right to leave the fruit on the trees until ripening, effectively a form of i'arah (loan) rather than ijara (rent). The passage also cites Jami' al-Fusulayn which clarifies that if someone sells a tree with fruit (not included) and then wants to rent the tree to leave the fruit, it is impermissible; instead, it should be a loan (i'arah). If the buyer refuses the loan, the seller can annul the sale or cut the fruit. This shows the school's careful distinction between rent and loan to avoid forbidden transactions.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hilah): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible result while avoiding an explicit prohibition by structuring the transaction differently.
إعارة (i'arah): A gratuitous loan of an object for temporary use without rent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the stratagem, what must be established to allow leaving fruit on a tree after sale?
A) A rental contract between seller and buyer.
B) A binding right to leave the fruit without rent.
C) A partnership in the tree.
D) A sale of the fruit with delayed delivery.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi stratagem permits leaving fruit on a tree after sale by using a binding loan instead of a rental, avoiding the prohibition of renting unripe fruit.

Condition to Leave Fruit on Trees: Muhammad's Istihsan
Intra-Hanafi dispute on fruit condition with istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Imam Muhammad's istihsan overrides qiyas and apply the ruling to scenarios involving permission and rental.

Core Idea
Custom makes valid what qiyas would void: the sale of trees with fruit left to ripen.

The Hanafi school has an internal dispute about selling trees when the buyer stipulates to leave the fruit until maturity. Abu Hanifa rules the sale invalid if the fruit is not yet fully grown, because the condition includes a non-existent part. But Imam Muhammad, joined by Abu Yusuf, uses istihsan (juristic preference) based on the widespread custom of leaving fruit to ripen. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the later reliance on Muhammad's view, and the practical consequences for buyers, sellers, and renters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Abu Hanifa invalidates the sale when the condition involves fruit that is not yet mature (tanaha), because the condition includes a future increase (juz' ma'dum). Imam Muhammad upholds the sale via istihsan, citing custom ('adah).
The dispute arises only when fruit has not completed growth; if mature, all agree the sale is valid.
2
Understand the istihsan justification
Muhammad argues that strict qiyas would cause hardship because people customarily leave fruit to ripen. Istihsan, based on custom, overrides that qiyas. The later Hanafi masters (Al-Asrar, Al-Tuhfah) adopt Muhammad's view as the mufta bihi.
This is a classic example of istihsan bi al-'urf.
3
Analyze the cases after sale without condition
If the buyer buys absolutely (no condition) and leaves fruit: (a) with seller's permission, increase is lawful; (b) without permission, he must give charity for the increase. If fruit was already mature, no charity due because it is only a change of state.
The ruling distinguishes between increase in substance (mutajammid) and mere change of state.
4
Compare rental scenarios
If the buyer rents the palm trees (to ripen fruit) after buying absolutely, the rental is void due to lack of custom/need, so the buyer's retention of fruit is deemed permitted. But if he rents the land (for crops) and leaves the crop, the rental is fasid (invalid) due to ignorance (jahalah), making the increase impure.
Void (batil) vs. fasid matters: batil has no legal effect, so permission remains; fasid has some effect but causes illegality.
5
Note the theological aside
The passage ends with a metaphysical note: growth comes from the sun, moon, and stars by Allah's permission, reminding that natural increase is not solely from human action. This reinforces that charity is not due for change of state.
This is a rare inclusion in fiqh texts, reflecting a holistic worldview.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا لو شرط ترك الزرع على الأرض لما قلنا أطلقه فشمل ما إذا تناهى عظمهما أو لا، وفي الأول خلاف محمد فإنه يقول: استحسن أن لا يفسد بشرط الترك للعادة بخلاف ما إذا لم يتناه لأنه شرط فيه الجزء المعدوم وهو ما يزداد بمعنى في الأرض والشجر. وفي الأسرار: الفتوى على قول محمد وبه أخذ الطحاوي وفي المتنقى: ضم إليه أبا يوسف. وفي التحفة: والصحيح قولهما. وقيد باشتراط الترك لأنه لو اشتراها مطلقاً وتركها، فإن كان بإذن البائع طاب له الفضل، وإن تركها بغير إذنه تصدق بما زاد في ذاته لحصوله بجهة محظورة، وإن تركها بعد ما تناهى لم يتصدق بشيء لأن هذا تغير حالة لا تتحقق زيادة، وإن اشتراها مطلقاً أو بشرط القطع وتركها على النخل وقد استأجر النخيل إلى وقت الإدراك طاب له الفضل لأن الإجارة باطلة لعدم التعارف والحاجة فبقي الإذن معتبراً لأن الباطل لا وجود له فكان إذناً مقصوداً بخلاف ما إذا اشترى الزرع واستأجر الأرض إلى أن يدرك وترك حيث لا يطيب له الفضل لأن الإجارة فاسدة للجهالة، وإذا فسد المتضمن فسد المتضمن فأورثت خبثاً. وقد ذكر أصحابنا هنا أن الشمس تنضحها بإذن الله تعالى وبتقديره ويأخذ اللون من القمر، والطعم من الكواكب، فلم يبق فيه إلا عمل الشمس والقمر والكواكب؛ كذا في المعراج.
And likewise if he stipulates leaving the cultivation on the land, because we said it is absolute, so it includes whether its [the fruit's] growth has completed or not. In the first case (when growth has completed), there is a disagreement of Muhammad: he says he istihsan (prefers) that it does not become invalid due to the condition of leaving because of custom, unlike when it has not completed, because he stipulates a non-existent part, which is what increases in meaning in the land and trees. In Al-Asrar: the fatwa is on Muhammad's saying, and Al-Tahawi took it, and in Al-Muntqa: Abu Yusuf is joined to him. In Al-Tuhfah: the correct is their saying (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is restricted by stipulating leaving, because if he bought it absolutely and left it, if it was with the seller's permission, the increase is lawful for him, and if he left it without his permission, he gives in charity what increased in its essence because it was obtained by a prohibited means. And if he left it after completion, he does not give anything in charity because this is a change of state not involving an increase. And if he bought it absolutely or with the condition of cutting and left it on the palm trees while having rented the palm trees until ripening time, the increase is lawful for him because the rental is void due to lack of custom and need, so the permission remains effective because what is void has no existence, so it was intended permission, unlike if he bought the crop and rented the land until ripening and left it, where the increase is not lawful because the rental is invalid due to ignorance. And when the implicit is invalid, the explicit becomes invalid, so it causes impurity. And our companions have mentioned here that the sun dries them by the permission of Allah and His decree, and they take color from the moon, and taste from the stars; so nothing remains except the work of the sun, moon, and stars; thus in Al-Mi'raj.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on selling trees with fruit when the buyer stipulates that the fruit be left on the trees until ripening. The base ruling (from the matn) is that the sale is valid if the fruit has reached full maturity (tanaha) but invalid if not, because the condition involves a non-existent part that will increase. Imam Muhammad differs: he uses istihsan based on custom ('adah) to uphold the validity even when maturity is incomplete. The fatwa is on Muhammad's view, often with Abu Yusuf. The passage then explains the consequences of leaving fruit without a condition: if with permission, the increase is lawful; if without permission, charity is required; if after maturity, no charity. It also compares renting trees versus land, noting the difference between void and invalid contracts. The metaphysical explanation about sun, moon, and stars is a theological aside to explain natural growth.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Preference of a ruling based on custom or equity over qiyas.
تناهى (tanaha): When the fruit reaches full growth and maturity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does Imam Muhammad consider the sale of trees with fruit valid even if the fruit is not fully mature, contrary to Abu Hanifa?
A) Because the fruit is considered a separate property.
B) Because of istihsan based on custom, as people commonly leave fruit to ripen.
C) Because the condition is void so the sale stands without it.
D) Because the buyer can cut the fruit later.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, istihsan based on custom can override strict qiyas to validate sales that would otherwise be invalid due to conditions involving future increase.

Legal Stratagem for Renting Trees and the Qasil Dispute
Hila and intra-madhhab dispute on crop contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi legal stratagem for renting trees to leave fruit and distinguish Abu Hanifa's and Abu Yusuf's positions on the sale of qasil that becomes grain.

Core Idea
Renting trees is invalid due to uncertainty, but a documented necessary right (haqq lazim) achieves the same effect legally.

The Hanafi school recognizes that direct rental of trees for leaving fruit on them is impermissible because of uncertainty (gharar). However, a legal stratagem (hila) using a documented necessary right allows the same outcome. This passage also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of green fodder (qasil) that becomes grain before possession. Understanding these rulings sharpens your ability to navigate contracts involving crops and trees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibition
Renting trees directly is invalid because the benefit (i.e., the fruit's presence) is uncertain in duration and quantity; this is gharar in Islamic law.
The base ruling is that renting trees is batil (void).
2
Understand the hila
The legal stratagem is to write a contract granting the buyer a necessary right (haqq lazim) to leave fruit on the trees for a specified period, with the condition that the trees and fruit belong to another person. This transforms the arrangement from a rental into a documented right.
The hila is permissible because it uses valid contracts (sale and condition).
3
Differentiate rental vs loan
A loan (i'ara) of the tree for leaving fruit until ripening is permissible, but a rental (ijara) is not. The buyer may request a loan; if the seller refuses, the buyer has options.
Loan is gratuitous and does not involve consideration, avoiding gharar.
4
Examine the qasil case
When fodder (qasil) is bought and before possession it becomes grain, Abu Hanifa voids the sale because the subject matter has changed species. Abu Yusuf holds the sale valid because grain is the natural end state of qasil.
The disagreement hinges on whether the change constitutes a new species (Abu Hanifa) or an inherent development (Abu Yusuf).
5
Apply the analogy
The ruling on fruit sold without the tree is analogous: if the fruit hasn't ripened and the seller refuses a loan, the buyer can either cancel the sale or cut the fruit immediately. The reasoning is that cutting destroys value, but preserving it requires permission.
This analogy extends the same logic from the qasil case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي البخاري عن قتادة وفي المعراج معزياً إلى الفصول: لو أراد إجارة الأشجار والكروم فالحيلة فيه أن يكتب أن لهذا المشتري حق ترك الثمار على الأشجار في مدة كذا بأمر لازم أن تكون الثمار والأشجار لآخر، وله حق الترك فيها إلى وقت الإدراك، فإذا ذكر هذا حمل على أنه بحق لازم؛ كذا في شرح ظهير الدين المرغيناني اهـ. وفي جامع الفصولين: باع شجراً عليه ثمر وكرماً فيه عنب لا يدخل الثمر، فلو استأجر الشجر من المشتري ليترك عليه الثمر لم يجز ولكن يعار إلى الإدراك، فلو أبى المشتري يخير البائع إن شاء أبطل البيع أو قطع الثمر، ولو باع أرضاً بدون الزرع فهو للبائع بأجر مثلها إلى الإدراك اهـ. وفيه أيضاً: شرى قصيلاً فلم يقبضه حتى صار حباً بطل البيع عند أبي حنيفة لا عند أبي يوسف اهـ. وينبغي على قياس هذا أنه لو باع ثمرة بدون الشجرة ولم يدرك ولم يرض البائع بإعارة الشجر أن يتخير المشتري إن شاء أبطل البيع وإن شاء قطعها. ووجهه فيهما أن في القطع إتلاف المال إذ لا ينتفع به. وقوله لو باع أرضاً بدون الزرع فهو للبائع بأجر مثلها» شكل لما قدمنا أنه يجب على البائع قطعه وتسليم الأرض فارغة وليس هذا مذهب الأئمة من أنه يؤخر التسليم إلى الإدراك لأنهم لم يوجبوا أجر المثل فليتأمل.
And in Al-Bukhari from Qatada, and in Al-Mi'raj attributed to Al-Fusul: If one wants to rent out trees and vineyards, the legal stratagem (hila) is to write that this buyer has the right to leave the fruits on the trees for a specified period, with a necessary condition that the fruits and trees belong to another, and he has the right to leave them until ripening time. If this is mentioned, it is interpreted as a necessary right; thus in Sharh Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he sold a tree with fruit and a vine with grapes, the fruit does not enter [the sale]. If he then rents the tree from the buyer to leave the fruit on it, it is not permissible, but it may be given as a loan (i'ara) until ripening. If the buyer refuses, the seller has the option to either cancel the sale or cut the fruit. And if he sold land without the crop, the crop belongs to the seller with a rent equal to its value until ripening. And in it also: If he bought fodder (qasil) and did not take possession until it became grain, the sale is void according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to Abu Yusuf. And by analogy to this, if he sold fruit without the tree and it has not ripened, and the seller does not agree to lend the tree, the buyer may choose to either cancel the sale or cut the fruit. The reasoning in both is that cutting destroys wealth since no benefit is obtained. And his statement: 'If he sold land without the crop, it belongs to the seller with a rent equal to its value' is problematic given our earlier point that the seller must cut the crop and deliver the land empty; this is not the school's position that delivery is delayed until ripening, because they did not require a rent equal to value. So reflect on this.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) for renting trees to leave fruit, using a necessary right documented in writing. It then provides rulings from Jami' al-Fusulayn: selling trees with fruit without the fruit included, renting the tree to leave fruit is invalid but a loan (i'ara) is permissible. If the buyer refuses, the seller can cancel or cut. Then a case of buying fodder that becomes grain before possession: Abu Hanifa says the sale is void due to change in species; Abu Yusuf says it's valid. An analogy is drawn for fruit sold without the tree if it hasn't ripened. The passage ends with a critical note about a seeming inconsistency: when selling land without crops, the crop is left with a rent, contradicting the rule that the seller must clear the land; the commentator suggests this needs reflection.

Key Terms
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem to achieve a permissible outcome while avoiding prohibition.
قصيل (qasil): Green fodder; young grain before it hardens.
إعارة (i'ara): Loan of a usufruct without compensation; gratuitous loan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He can only cancel the sale.
B) He can only cut the fruit.
C) He can either cancel the sale or cut the fruit.
D) He can force the seller to lend the tree.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits legal stratagems to achieve permissible ends within valid contracts, and intra-Hanafi disagreements highlight the role of usul in deriving rulings.

Excluding Known Pounds from Fruit on Trees (Istithna')
Ruling on excepting a known quantity of fruit from sale, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on excluding known pounds from fruit on trees and identify the reason for the disagreement between the zahir al-riwayah and Hasan's narration.

Core Idea
Excluding a known weight of fruit from a tree is valid if the remainder is beneficial and the exclusion is specific, not a common share.

This lesson examines a specific sale: buying all fruit on a tree except a known number of pounds. The Hanafi school's relied-upon view permits this, but a minority narration from Abu Hanifa forbids it. Understanding the dispute teaches us about the limits of tolerable ignorance (jahalah) in contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling and its justification
The author states that if a buyer excludes known pounds from the fruit on trees, both the sale and the exclusion are valid. The justification is analogized to selling a known quantity from a heap of food, which is valid.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Locate the opposing narration and its qiyas
Hasan ibn Ziyad narrates from Abu Hanifa that the exception is invalid. This is argued by qiyas to the case of selling a food heap at a rate per qafiz, which Abu Hanifa invalidated due to ignorance of the sold quantity at contract time.
The objection is that the total fruit weight is unknown, leading to potential dispute.
3
Analyze the response: how the majority reconcile the analogy
The response points out that in the heap sale, the price was unknown due to the rate; here the price is known. Also, the ignorance of the total fruit weight does not lead to dispute because the excluded amount is known and the remainder is deliverable.
Thus, the jahalah is tolerable, unlike the heap case.
4
Note the conditions for validity
The exclusion must be of a specific part (e.g., known pounds) and not a common share (like quarter or third). The fruit must also be beneficial at the time; if it is still unripe or susceptible to damage, the ruling may differ. Additionally, the remainder after exclusion must be more than the excluded amount.
If only one pound exists, it would be an exclusion of all from all, which is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His saying: (And if he excludes from it known pounds, it is valid) meaning the sale and the exclusion are valid, because what is permitted to be the subject of a contract alone is permitted to be excluded from it, and the sale of a qafiz from a heap is permissible, so similarly its exclusion, unlike excluding the pregnancy from a pregnant slave girl or a sheep, or the limbs of an animal, for it is not permissible, as if he sold this sheep except its tail or this slave except his hand. This is understood from the apparent narration. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is not permissible, and it is more consistent with the Imam's analogy in the issue of selling a food heap at a rate of each qafiz for a dirham, for he invalidated the sale due to ignorance of the sold quantity at the time of contract, and this applies to excluding known pounds from what is on trees, even if it does not lead to dispute. The conclusion is that every ignorance that leads to dispute is invalidating, but it does not follow that what does not lead to it is valid; rather, for validity without dispute, the sold item must be within the limits of the Sharia. Don't you see that the two parties may agree on a condition not required by the contract, and on a sale with an unknown term like the arrival of the pilgrim and the like, and that is not considered corrective? Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Mi'raj: It is said that the narration of al-Hasan and al-Tahawi is interpreted as when the fruit is not beneficial, because it may be struck by an affliction and there is only the excluded amount, so harm occurs. The point of disagreement is when he excludes a specific part; if he excludes a portion like a quarter or a third, it is valid by agreement; thus in al-Bada'i. Therefore the author said 'known pounds.' And he restricted it with 'from it' meaning from the fruit on the heads of palm trees, because if it were cut and he excluded pounds from it, it is valid by agreement. And he restricted it with 'pounds' because if he excluded one pound, it is valid by agreement because it is excluding a little from much, unlike the possibility that it is not except that amount, making it an exclusion of all from all; thus in al-Binaya. 'More consistent with the Imam's analogy' etc. In al-Nahr: It can be answered as we have stated that the invalidity in his view in the heap sale is based on ignorance of the price, while the sold is known by pointing, and in our case the price is known. His saying: (And the point of disagreement is when he excludes a specific part etc.) The reasoning that known pounds are specific is that by 'pound' is meant its weight equivalent of the fruit, not the piece that is the instrument of weight; what is placed on the scale and measured by pounds is a specific thing, not a common share of the entire fruit, unlike a quarter or third, as understood from his earlier statement: 'And the sale of ten cubits of a house without shares becomes invalid.' His saying: (Because it is excluding a little from much) implies that if it is known that the fruit amounts to a large amount exceeding three pounds or ten, etc., leaving the remainder more than the excluded, then it is valid, but reflect. In al-Fath, there is indication that it is not. His saying: 'valid?'
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on excepting (istithna') a known weight (pounds) of fruit from the sale of fruit on trees. The base ruling is that such an exception is valid, analogized to selling a specific quantity from a heap. However, there is a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad that Abu Hanifa considered it invalid, based on the principle of ignorance (jahalah) that can lead to dispute, similar to his invalidation of selling a heap at a rate per unit. The commentator reconciles: the invalidity is only if the fruit is not yet beneficial; if beneficial, it's valid. Also, if the exception is of a specific part (like a quarter), it's valid by consensus. The passage clarifies that 'known pounds' means specific weight, not a common share. The restriction to pounds is to ensure the exception is of a little from much; if only one pound is possible, it might be all from all. The text ends with a critical reflection on the conditions for validity.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithna'): Excluding a specific part of the sold item from the sale.
جهالة (jahalah): Ignorance of a necessary element that can lead to dispute.
أرطال معلومة (artāl ma'lūma): Known pounds (specific weight of fruit).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon view in the Hanafi school, when is it valid to exclude known pounds from fruit on trees in a sale?
A) Always, regardless of the condition of the fruit.
B) Only if the fruit is beneficial and the exclusion is specific.
C) Only if the fruit has already been cut and collected.
D) Only if the excluded amount is less than a tenth of the total.

Key Takeaway
Excluding a known weight of fruit from a tree is valid if the remainder is beneficial and the exclusion is specific; tolerable jahalah does not invalidate the contract.

Excepting Known Pounds from Fruit on Trees in a Sale
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on excepting a known weight of fruit from a sale of fruit on trees, identify the condition for validity, and distinguish the majority position from the minority intra-Hanafi view.

Core Idea
A known weight of fruit may be excepted from a tree sale if it is determinate and less than the total; contrary narrations are outweighed.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical issue in fruit sales: can a seller reserve a specific weight (e.g., 10 ratls) for himself while selling the rest? The author presents the Hanafi ruling with its proof, discusses an intra-madhhab disagreement, and clarifies the conditions for a valid exception. Mastering this rule prevents disputes over the exact quantity delivered.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is a sale of fruit on trees with an exception of known pounds. The majority Hanafi ruling is that the sale and the exception are valid (sahih).
Note that the exception of a part that cannot be sold independently (like a limb) is invalid.
2
Understand the dalil (proof)
The proof is qiyas (analogy) to selling a specific measure (qafiz) from a heap of grain, which is permissible. The principle: what can be sold independently can be excepted.
Other invalid exceptions (fetus, limb) are distinguished because they cannot be sold independently.
3
Recognize the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad narrated from Abu Hanifa that the exception is not valid, analogizing to the case of selling a heap of food with an unknown price. The majority responds that the analogy is weak because the price is known and the fruit is specified by indication.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school; the mufta bihi is the majority view.
4
Apply the conditions for validity
The exception must be mu'ayyan (determinate, e.g., known pounds), the fruit must be on the tree (not cut), and the excepted amount must be less than the total fruit. If the excepted amount equals or exceeds the total, the exception becomes invalid as 'exception of the whole from the whole'.
The passage stresses that the sale must be within the limits of Sharia; mere agreement does not validate an otherwise defective contract.

Source Text
