And if he excepts from it known pounds, it is valid, meaning the sale and the exception, because what is permissible to conclude the contract upon individually is permissible to except from it. Selling a qafiz from a heap is permissible, so too its exception, unlike the exception of a fetus from a pregnant slave girl or a sheep, or limbs of an animal, for it is not permissible, such as if he sells this sheep except its tail, or this slave except his hand. This is understood from the apparent narration. Al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifa that it is not permissible, and it is more analogous to the Imam's position in the issue of selling a heap of food, each qafiz for a dirham, for he invalidated the sale due to ignorance of the amount of the sold item at the time of contract, and this applies to excepting known pounds from what is on trees, even if it does not lead to dispute. The conclusion is that every ignorance that leads to dispute invalidates; it does not necessarily follow that what does not lead to dispute is valid with it; rather, for validity without leading to dispute, the sold item must be within the limits of the Sharia. Do you not see that the two contracting parties may agree on a condition not required by the contract, and on a sale with an unknown term like the arrival of the pilgrim and the like, and that is not considered a valid factor. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and in al-Mi'raj: It is said that the narration of al-Hasan and al-Tahawi is interpreted as when the fruit is not beneficial because it may be struck by a calamity, and there is only the amount excepted, so harm may affect it. End. The place of disagreement is when he excepts a specific thing; if he excepts a part like a quarter or a third, it is valid by agreement. Thus in al-Bada'i'. Hence the author said 'known pounds'. And he restricted it with 'from it', meaning from the fruit on the heads of the palm trees, because if it were cut and he excepted pounds from it, it would be valid by agreement. And he restricted it with 'pounds' because if he excepted one pound, it would be valid by agreement because it is exception of a little from much, unlike [the plural] 'pounds' because that amount may be all that exists, making it an exception of the whole from the whole. Thus in al-Binaya. ... [Rest of translation omitted for brevity]
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling of excepting a known quantity of fruit from a sale of fruit on trees. The author begins by stating the standard Hanafi position: it is valid to except a known number of pounds (ratls) from the fruit on the trees. The proof given is a general principle: anything that can be sold independently can be excepted. For example, selling a specific measure from a heap is valid, so excepting it from a sale is also valid. The exception of a fetus or a limb is invalid because those cannot be sold independently. The author then presents a contrary narration from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad from Abu Hanifa, which considers such an exception invalid. This opinion is analogized to Abu Hanifa's famous ruling that selling a heap of food with an unknown price is invalid due to ignorance of the quantity at the time of contract. However, the majority holds that if the exception does not lead to dispute, it is valid, provided the sale itself is properly defined. The disagreement is specifically when the exception is a specific weight (like pounds) as opposed to a fraction (like one-quarter), which is valid by consensus. The passage also clarifies that the exception must be from fruit still on the trees; if the fruit were already cut, exception is valid by agreement. The author concludes with a reflection (ta'ammul) on the condition that the remaining fruit must be more than the excepted amount.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā’): The act of excluding a specific part of the sold item from the sale, rendering it retained by the seller.
أرطال (arṭāl): Plural of ratl, a unit of weight. Here it means a known number of weight units of fruit.
صبرة (ṣubrah): A heap or pile of grain or other commodity; its sale by measured quantities is subject to specific rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, is it valid to except a known number of pounds (e.g., 10 ratls) from the fruit on a tree when selling the fruit?
A) Yes, because the exception is of a determinate part.
B) No, because it creates ignorance of the total quantity.
C) Yes, but only if the fruit is already cut.
D) No, because the fruit cannot be sold independently.

Key Takeaway
A known weight exception from a sale of fruit on trees is valid in the Hanafi school provided the excepted part is determinate and the remaining fruit is clearly more; the minority opinion based on jahala is outweighed.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Sale with Exception of a Quantified Part: Phrasing and Its Legal Effect
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the rulings of sale with exception using 'illa' and 'ala an' according to the Hanafi school, and state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the validity and price distribution.

Core Idea
Using 'illa' excludes the part from the sale; using 'ala an' retains it — the price adjusts but may invalidate if uncertainty arises.

When selling a heap (ṣubrah) of grain, the seller may wish to exclude a portion for himself. The Arabic phrasing used — 'illā' (except) vs 'alā an' (on condition that) — determines the legal effect. This lesson examines the Hanafi rulings on such sales, highlighting the intra-school differences among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrasing
Distinguish between istithnā' (exception) with 'illā and ishtirāṭ (condition) with 'alā an. The former excludes the part from the transaction; the latter retains it as a condition.
The key is whether the excepted part is removed from the sale or kept as a retained portion.
2
Apply the rule to the heap sale
For 'illā: seller gets full price for the remainder. For 'alā an: seller gets a proportionate price (e.g., nine-tenths of price for nine-tenths of heap).
In the text: selling a heap for 100 except its tenth gives full 100 for 9/10; 'on condition that its tenth is mine' gives 90 for 9/10.
3
Analyze the sheep case (Abu Yusuf)
If seller says 'I sell you 100 sheep for 100 on condition that this sheep is mine', the sale is invalid due to price ambiguity. If he uses 'except this sheep', it is valid and the buyer gets 99 sheep for 100.
Abu Yusuf emphasizes that 'ala an' with an unspecified single sheep causes jahāla (ignorance) of the price per sheep.
4
Analyze the slave case (Muhammad)
If seller says 'I sell you this slave for 1000 except his half for 500', Muhammad rules it valid for a total of 1500: half sold for 1000, the other half for 500. But if he uses 'ala an' with a separate price, it becomes invalid due to combining two transactions.
Muhammad allows the exception if the excepted part is also sold separately for a specified price.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: whether the sale is valid (ṣaḥīḥ) or corrupt (fāsid), and how the price is allocated. Using 'illa' almost always yields a valid sale; using 'ala an' may be valid only if the excepted part is quantified and the price is clear.
The author notes further discussion in the chapter on corrupt sales.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
روي عن محمد أنه بجميع من هذا وسيأتي في البيع الفاسد الإيراد على القاعدة المذكورة في استثناء الحمل وهو أن الإيصاء بالخدمة منفردة جائز واستثناؤها لا وكذلك الغلة ونذكر جوابه وهي قاعدة مطردة منعكسة كما في البناية. ولو باع صبرة بمائة إلا عشرها فله تسعة أعشارها بجميع الثمن، ولو قال على أن عشرها لي فله تسعة أعشارها بتسعة أعشار الثمن خلافاً لما الثمن فيها. وعن أبي يوسف : لو قال أبيعك هذه المائة شاة بمائة على أن هذه لي أو ولي هذه فسد، ولو قال إلا هذه كان ما بقي بمائة ولو قال ولي نصفها كان النصف بخمسين. ولو قال بعتك هذا العبد بألف إلا نصفه بخمسمائة، عن محمد جاز في كله بألف وخمسمائة لأن المعنى باع نصفه بألف لأنه الباقي بعد الاستثناء فالنصف المستثنى عين بيعه بخمسمائة. ولو قال على أن لي نصفه بثلاثمائة أو مائة دينار فسد لإدخال صفقة في صفقة؛ كذا في فتح القدير من البيع الفاسد وسيأتي تمامه في البيع الفاسد إن شاء الله تعالى.
It is narrated from Muhammad that... and it will come in the corrupt sale the objection upon the mentioned rule concerning the exception of the fetus, which is that bequest of service alone is valid and its exception is not, and likewise the produce, and we will mention its answer, and it is a consistent and converse rule as in al-Binaya. And if he sold a heap for a hundred except its tenth, then he gets nine-tenths of it for all the price. And if he said on condition that its tenth is mine, then he gets nine-tenths of it for nine-tenths of the price, contrary to what... concerning the price. And from Abu Yusuf: if he said I sell you these one hundred sheep for one hundred on condition that this is mine or this is for me, it is invalid; and if he said except this, then what remains is for one hundred. And if he said and for me half of it, then half is for fifty. And if he said I sold you this slave for one thousand except his half for five hundred, from Muhammad it is permissible in all for one thousand and five hundred because the meaning is that he sold half for one thousand because it is the remainder after the exception, so the excepted half is the very thing sold for five hundred. And if he said on condition that I have half of it for three hundred or one hundred dinars, it is invalid due to combining two transactions in one; as in Fath al-Qadir from the corrupt sale, and its completion will come in the corrupt sale if Allah wills.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning sale with an exception of a quantified part. The author begins by referencing a rule reported from Imam Muhammad: whatever can be the subject of a contract alone, its exception is valid; but the exception of service of a slave or produce is not valid because they cannot be sold alone. He then gives examples. If someone sells a heap of grain for 100 dirhams except one-tenth, the seller gets nine-tenths of the heap for the entire 100 dirhams — meaning the excepted tenth is excluded entirely and the buyer gets all the heap except that tenth for the full price. But if he says 'on condition that its tenth is mine' (retaining ownership of the tenth), then the seller gets nine-tenths of the heap for nine-tenths of the price, because the tenth is not sold. Abu Yusuf explains: if someone says 'I sell you these 100 sheep for 100 on condition that this sheep is mine' (i.e., he excludes a specific sheep), the sale is invalid because the price is for all but he retains one, making the price ambiguous. If he says 'except this sheep', the sale of the rest is valid for 100 dirhams. Similarly, if he says 'and half is mine', the half is for 50 dirhams. Muhammad gives a case: selling a slave for 1000 except his half for 500 — this is valid for a total of 1500, because the slave's half is sold for 1000 and the excepted half is sold separately for 500. But if he says 'on condition that I have half for 300 or 100 dinars', it is invalid because it combines two different transactions into one, creating uncertainty. The author notes this will be discussed further in the chapter on corrupt sales.

Key Terms
استثناء (istithnā'): Exception; excluding a part of the sold item from the sale.
صبرة (ṣubrah): A heap or pile of grain or similar commodity sold by estimation.
على أن (ʿalā an): On condition that; used to stipulate a condition in the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone sells a heap of wheat for 100 dirhams except one-tenth, what does the seller receive?
A) Nine-tenths of the heap and 90 dirhams
B) Nine-tenths of the heap and 100 dirhams
C) The entire heap and 100 dirhams
D) One-tenth of the heap and 10 dirhams

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the phrasing used to exclude a part from a sale determines whether the sale is valid and how the price is distributed: 'illā' (except) excludes the part and gives the seller full price for the rest; 'alā an' retains the part and usually gives a proportionate price, but can invalidate if it creates uncertainty.

Joint Purchase and Division of Parts Between Partners
Rulings on two persons buying an item and dividing parts among themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for dividing a jointly purchased item and determine the validity of the division based on the independent saleability of parts.

Core Idea
A division of a jointly purchased item is valid only if each part can be sold separately; the subsidiary follows the original.

When two people jointly purchase an item and later wish to divide it among themselves, Islamic commercial law provides clear guidelines. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the division is only valid if the parts can be sold independently. This lesson explores that principle through classical examples of a sword, ring, house, and camel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The validity of dividing a jointly purchased item hinges on whether each part is independently saleable (qabil li al-bayʿ).
This is the key condition derived from the examples.
2
Apply to items with separable parts
For items like a sword (hilt and blade), ring (gem and band), and house (land and building), each part can be sold individually, so the division is valid.
These parts have independent market value.
3
Apply when one part is original (asl) and the other subsidiary (taba)
In the camel example where one gets body and the other gets head, skin, and legs, the body is the original part, and the subsidiary parts belong to its owner.
The subsidiary follows the original because it cannot be sold independently.
4
Apply when neither part is original and neither is independently saleable
In the camel example where one gets head, skin, legs and the other gets meat, none of these can be sold alone, so the partners share equally half-half.
Without an original part, the default is equal co-ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجلان اشتريا سيفاً وتواضعا على أن يكون الحلية لأحدهما وللآخر النصل كان السيف المحلى بينهما والخاتم مع الفص كذلك. ولو اشتريا داراً على أن لأحدهما الأرض وللآخر البناء جاز كذلك. ولو اشتريا بعيراً وتواضعا على أن يكون لأحدهما رأسه وجلده وقوائمه وللآخر بدنه تواضعا في ذلك ولم يذكر البائع شيئاً فالكل لصاحب البدن لأن البدن أصل وغيره بمنزلة التبع. ولو تواضعا على أن لأحدهما رأسه وجلده وقوائمه وللآخر لحمه فهو بينهما نصفان لأن كل واحد من ذلك لا يحتمل الإفراد بالبيع وأحدهما ليس بأصل فكان الكل بينهما.
Two men bought a sword and agreed that the hilt would be for one and the blade for the other; the ornamented sword is between them. And likewise the ring with its gem. And if they bought a house and agreed that the land is for one and the building for the other, it is permissible likewise. And if they bought a camel and agreed that the head, skin and legs are for one and the body for the other, and they agreed on that without mentioning anything to the seller, then all is for the owner of the body because the body is the original and the rest are subsidiary. And if they agreed that the head, skin and legs are for one and the meat for the other, then it is between them half-half because each of those cannot be sold individually and neither is original, so all is between them.
Source pages p. 508

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of dividing a jointly purchased item among the partners. The key principle is whether each part can be sold individually (i.e., is independently valuable and tradeable). For a sword, the hilt and blade are considered separate parts that can be sold individually, so the division is valid. Similarly, land and building are separate. For a camel, the body is the main part; head, skin, and legs are subsidiary, so the owner of the body gets everything because the subsidiary parts follow the original. However, if the division is between non-original parts (head, skin, legs vs. meat), none of these can be sold alone, so they remain co-owners half and half. This illustrates the rule: if one part is the 'asl' (origin) and the other 'taba' (subsidiary), the subsidiary goes with the origin; if neither is original, they share equally.

Key Terms
تواضعا (tawāḍaʿā): They mutually agreed (after purchase) on division of the item.
أصل (aṣl): Original; main part that determines the subsidiary.
تبع (tabaʿ): Subsidiary; part that follows the main part.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Two partners buy a camel and agree that one gets the head, skin, and legs, and the other gets the body. What is the ruling?
A) The division is valid; each gets their part
B) The body owner gets everything; head, skin, legs follow
C) They remain co-owners half-half
D) The sale is invalid because it was not specified at purchase

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the validity of dividing a jointly purchased item depends on whether each part can be sold independently; if one part is original, the subsidiary follows, otherwise equal shares.

Dispute on Selling Food with Exception of Quantity
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale with exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on selling food with an exception of unspecified quantity and explain the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A sale with exception of an unspecified quantity is invalid per Abu Hanifa but valid with buyer's option per Abu Yusuf.

This passage from al-Tatarkhaniyya records an intra-Hanafi dispute between the two great imams: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. The case involves a sale of food with an exception of ten qafizes (a measure) – but the exact ten are not specified at the time of contract. Abu Hanifa rules the sale invalid due to uncertainty (gharar), while Abu Yusuf validates it, giving the buyer the option to cancel after the quantity is separated. Understanding this dispute illuminates the Hanafi approach to gharar and khiyar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
What exactly is being sold? A quantity of food with an exception of ten qafizes. The dispute hinges on whether the exception is sufficiently certain.
The excepted quantity is unspecified at the time of contract.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and basis
Abu Hanifa says the sale is fasid (invalid) because the excepted quantity is unknown, leading to gharar (uncertainty) in the subject matter.
Gharar renders a sale invalid in Hanafi fiqh.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and basis
Abu Yusuf says the sale is valid but the buyer has khiyar (option) to void the sale after the ten qafizes are separated, because the separation makes the excepted quantity known.
Khiyar al-tayyin or khiyar al-ru'ya.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, the sale is void from the start; under Abu Yusuf, it is valid subject to the buyer's option.
This affects whether the seller can enforce the contract.
5
Reflect on the mufta bihi
The passage does not specify which view is relied upon for fatwa. Later Hanafi works may adopt Abu Yusuf's view for ease of transactions.
Intra-madhhab disputes often require tarjih.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية : لو قال أبيعك هذا الطعام بألف درهم إلا عشرة أقفزة منها فالبيع فاسد في قول أبي حنيفة، وفي قول أبي يوسف البيع جائز، وللمشتري الخيار إذا عزل منه العشرة أقفزة، ولو باع بمائة إلا ديناراً كان البيع بتسعة وتسعين اشترى أمة وفي بطنها ولد لغير البائع بالوصية لرجل فأجاز صاحب الولد بيع الجارية جاز ولا شيء له من الثمن وإن لم يجز لم يجز لأن الجنين بمنزلة أجزاء الجارية تتمة منها : لو باع نصف عبد مشترك جاز وانصرف إلى نصيبه ولو أقر بنصفه انصرف إلى النصفين اهـ .
And in al-Tatarkhaniyya: if he said I sell you this food for one thousand dirhams except ten qafizes from it, then the sale is invalid in the saying of Abu Hanifa, and in the saying of Abu Yusuf the sale is valid, and the buyer has the option when he separates the ten qafizes. And if he sold for one hundred except a dinar, the sale is for ninety-nine. [If] he bought a slave-girl and in her womb is a child belonging to someone else by bequest to a man, and the owner of the child authorized the sale of the slave-girl, it is valid and he has nothing of the price; and if he did not authorize, it is not valid because the fetus is like parts of the slave-girl. Completion from it: if he sold half of a jointly owned slave, it is valid and refers to his share; and if he acknowledged half of it, it refers to the two halves. End.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage records an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf regarding the sale of food with an exception of a specific quantity (ten qafizes). Abu Hanifa deems the sale invalid because the excepted quantity is not specified, causing gharar. Abu Yusuf, however, allows the sale and gives the buyer a choice (option) to void the sale after the quantity is separated. This is followed by a separate ruling: if someone sells a slave-girl who is pregnant with a child belonging to another person (by bequest), and the child's owner authorizes the sale, the sale is valid and the owner gets no share of the price. If he does not authorize, the sale is invalid because the fetus is considered part of the slave-girl. The passage ends with an additional ruling: selling half of a jointly owned slave is valid and refers to the seller's share; if he acknowledges half, it refers equally to both halves.

Key Terms
قفز (qafīz): A measure of capacity for grain.
خيار (khiyār): Option; the right to cancel a contract.
جنين (janīn): Fetus; unborn child.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on selling food with an exception of ten unspecified qafizes?
A) Valid with buyer's option
B) Invalid because of uncertainty
C) Valid without option
D) Valid if seller specifies the ten qafizes at the time of contract

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale with an exception of an unspecified quantity is invalid per Abu Hanifa due to gharar, but valid per Abu Yusuf with the buyer's option to cancel after separation.

Principles of Exception: Independent Subject and Measurability
Summary of four principles governing exceptions in sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first two Hanafi principles on exception in sale and apply them to determine if an exception is valid and whether it can be stipulated for the seller.

Core Idea
An exception is valid only if the excepted item can be sold alone; if measurable, the seller may retain it per the second principle.

The Hanafi school has distilled key rules for sales exceptions. The first two principles address fundamental conditions: the excepted part must be capable of independent sale, and if it is measurable, the seller may retain it. This lesson unpacks these rules using examples from classical fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the first principle: validity of exception requires independent saleability.
The first principle states that if an item can be the subject of a contract alone, then its exception from a sale is valid, even if it was originally included as a subsidiary (e.g., building or tree). If it cannot be sold alone (like a part of a thing that is not divisible), the exception is invalid.
2
Identify the second principle: stipulating exception for the seller is limited to measurable items.
The second principle distinguishes between muqaddarat (measurable by weight, measure, or number) and qimiyyat (non-measurable, unique). For muqaddarat, the seller can stipulate to keep a part. For qimiyyat, such a stipulation is invalid.
3
Apply to examples.
Test: land and building? Building can be sold alone, so exception valid. A sword's hilt? Cannot be sold alone, so exception invalid. A specific cow? It is qimiyy (non-measurable), so seller cannot stipulate to keep it.
4
Connect to subsequent principles.
These foundations set the stage for principles 3 and 4.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم اعلم أن حاصل ما نقلناه هذه في المسألة يدور على أربع قواعد الأولى ما صح إيراد العقد عليه بانفراده صح استثناؤه سواء دخل في المبيع تبعاً كالبناء والشجر أو لا وما لا فلا. الثانية ما صح استثناؤه صح اشتراطه للبائع إذا كان من المقدرات، وإن كان من القيميات فلا. الثالثة ما صح إيراد العقد عليه اتفاقهما بعد العقد على أن يكون البعض لهذا والبعض لهذا كالبناء الأرض بانفراده صح مع وما لا فلا كالسيف والحلية الرابعة إذا استثنى ما يصح فإن ذكر للمستثنى ثمناً لم يكن للإخراج وكان الثمن الأول والثاني كبعتك هذا العبد بألف إلا نصفه بخمسمائة وإلا كان للإخراج من المبيع ولا يسقط من الثمن شيء، وإن كان شرطاً في المقدرات سقط ما قابله. وقدمنا عن الظهيرية أنه لو باع سفل داره على أن يكون له حق قرار العلو عليه فإنه يجوز لأنه مبيع أدخل عليه استثناء صحته كاشتراط الخيار اهـ .
Then know that the sum of what we have transmitted in this issue revolves around four principles: First: Whatever can be the subject of a contract alone, its exception is valid, whether it enters the sale as a subsidiary like building and tree or not, and whatever cannot is not. Second: Whatever is valid to except, it is valid to stipulate for the seller if it is of the measurable (quantified) type, but if it is of the non-measurable type, then not. Third: Whatever can be the subject of a contract alone, it is valid for the two (partners) to agree after the contract that some of it is for one and some for the other, like building and land each alone, while what cannot is not, like sword and hilt. Fourth: If he excepts what is valid, then if he mentions a price for the excepted thing, it is not for exclusion and there are two prices: the first and second, as in 'I sold you this slave for one thousand except his half for five hundred'; otherwise, it is for exclusion from the sold item and nothing falls from the price. And if it was a condition in the measurable, its counterpart falls. And we have presented from al-Zahiriyya that if he sold the lower floor of his house on condition that he has the right of stability of the upper floor upon it, it is valid because it is a sale to which exception is entered; its validity is like stipulating the option. End.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is a summary of four key principles (qawa'id) that govern exceptions in sales according to the Hanafi school. The first principle: The validity of an exception depends on whether the excepted part can independently be the subject of a contract. For example, a building or tree can be sold separately, so their exception is valid. The second principle: If the excepted part is measurable (e.g., by weight or volume), it can be validly stipulated for the seller; if it is a non-measurable item (like a specific animal), it cannot. The third principle: For partners, the validity of dividing a jointly purchased item after the contract depends on whether each part can be sold alone; land and building can, but a sword's hilt and blade cannot each be sold independently (though earlier we saw the sword case differently—this might refer to the parts not being individually saleable on their own). The fourth principle: If the excepted part is given a separate price, it is a separate sale and not an exclusion; the seller receives the sum of both prices. If no price is mentioned for the excepted part, it is an exclusion and the seller receives the full price for the remainder. For measurable items, if a condition is made, the corresponding amount of the price is reduced. The author also references al-Zahiriyya that selling the lower floor with the right of the upper floor's stability is valid, analogous to stipulating an option.

Key Terms
قاعدة (qāʿida): Legal principle; a general rule governing multiple cases.
مقدرات (muqaddarāt): Measurable items; quantified by weight, measure, or number.
قيميات (qīmiyyāt): Non-measurable items; valued by estimation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first principle, when is an exception valid?
A) Always
B) Only if the excepted item can be sold alone
C) Only if it is measurable
D) Only if it is a building or tree

Key Takeaway
An exception in sale is valid only if the excepted item can be sold alone; if stipulated for the seller, it must also be measurable.

Principles of Exception: Partner Divisions and Price Effects
Summary of four principles governing exceptions in sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the third and fourth principles on exception in sale, including the effect of mentioning a price and the rule for measurable items.

Core Idea
If partners agree to divide a jointly bought item, each part must be independently saleable; if a price is named for the excepted part, it becomes a separate sale, not an exception.

The third and fourth principles handle two specific scenarios: division of a purchase between partners and the effect of specifying a price for the excepted part. These rules prevent uncertainty and ensure clarity in transactions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third principle: division after contract requires each part to be independently saleable.
Partners who jointly buy an item can later divide it only if each part can be sold alone. Example: land and building can be divided; a sword and its hilt cannot because neither part is independently saleable.
2
Apply the third principle: identify whether division is valid.
Test cases: a plot of land with a tree? Both can be sold alone, so valid. A cup and its handle? Neither alone? The cup might be, but handle not, so invalid.
3
Understand the fourth principle: price for excepted part changes the nature.
If the seller says 'I sell you this slave for one thousand except his half for five hundred,' then it is actually two sales, not an exception. The prices add up (total 1500). If no price is mentioned for the excepted part, it is a true exception and no reduction in price.
4
Apply the fourth principle to measurable items: if a condition is made, the corresponding price falls.
For muqaddarat, if the exception is conditional, the price is reduced accordingly.
5
Note the al-Zahiriyya example: selling lower floor with right of upper floor is valid as an exception like an option.
This shows that an exception can be structured as a separate condition, analogous to an option.

Sale of Grain in Its Husk and Similar Items
Ruling on selling grain in its husk and related prohibitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling grain in its husk and identify when such a transaction becomes prohibited due to riba.

Core Idea
Selling grain in its husk is valid, but exchanging it for the same type in husk is prohibited due to potential riba.

The Hanafi school permits selling grain while still in its husk or pod, considering it valuable property from which benefit is derived. However, caution is needed when the same type of commodity is exchanged, because the uncertainty in measure can lead to riba. This lesson explains the distinction and the rulings, including examples and a key exception for items like date pits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
The author states that selling wheat in its ear or beans in their pod is valid (sahih) because they are valuable and benefit is derived.
2
Note the examples provided
Items like barley, rice, sesame, walnuts, almonds, and pistachios in their shells are also valid to sell.
3
Recognize the prohibition
It is not permissible to sell such grain for the same type if both are in husks, because of possible riba (usury) due to uncertainty in measurement.
4
Understand the distinction for cotton seeds and date pits
Abu Yusuf distinguishes that pits are considered essentially non-existent and worthless in custom, so the same prohibition does not apply to selling them in their coverings.
This is by analogy based on custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كبيع بر في سنبله وباقلاً في قشره) أي صحيح لأنه مال متقوم منتفع به فيجوز بيعه في قشره كالشعير وفي البناية ومن أكل الفولية يشهد بذلك وكذا الأرز والسمسم والجوز واللوز والفستق، ولا يجوز بيعه بمثله من سنبل الحنطة لاحتمال الربا كما في فتح القدير . وقدمنا أنه لا يجوز بيع قصيل البر بحنطة والقصيل الشعير يجز أخضر لعلف الدواب؛ كذا في المصباح. وأورد المطالبة بالفرق بين ما إذا باع حب قطن في قطن بعينه أو نوى تمر في تمر بعينه أي باع ما في هذا القطن من الحب أو ما في هذا التمر من النوى فإنه لا يجوز مع أنه أيضاً في غلافه. وأشار أبو يوسف إلى الفرق بأن النوى هنالك عدماً هالكاً معتبر في العرف على أن يكون له حق المرور منه قياساً على ما سبق قريباً وهو ظاهر ولم أره.
His saying: (like selling wheat in its ear and beans in their pod) i.e., valid, because it is valuable property from which benefit is derived, so it is permissible to sell it in its husk like barley. And in al-Binaya: and whoever eats fava beans testifies to that; likewise rice, sesame, walnuts, almonds, pistachios. And it is not permissible to sell it for the same type from the ear of wheat due to the possibility of riba, as in Fath al-Qadir. And we have presented that it is not permissible to sell cut green wheat for wheat, and cut green barley is cut green for animal fodder, as in al-Misbah. And he raised the demand for distinction between when he sells cotton seeds in their cotton in kind or date pits in their dates in kind, i.e., he sells what is in this cotton of seeds or what is in this date of pits, for it is not permissible although it is also in its covering. And Abu Yusuf indicated the distinction that the pit there is considered non-existent and destroyed in custom, on the condition that he has the right of passage from it by analogy to what came recently, and it is apparent and I have not seen it.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage discusses the validity of selling grain while still in its husk or pod. For example, selling wheat in the ear (sunnbul) or beans in the pod is valid because they are valuable and benefit is derived from them. The author mentions that items like rice, sesame, walnuts, almonds, and pistachios in their shells are also valid to sell. However, it is not permissible to sell such grain for the same type of grain that is also in its husk, because of the potential of riba (usury) due to uncertainty in measurement. The author also references a prohibition against selling cut green wheat for wheat (since the green wheat is not fully mature). The final part discusses a point raised: selling cotton seeds in their cotton in kind, or date pits in the dates in kind, is not permissible even though they are in their natural coverings. Abu Yusuf distinguishes that the pit is considered essentially non-existent and useless, unlike the husk of wheat which is beneficial. This distinction is based on custom, and the author says it is apparent but he has not seen it elsewhere.

Key Terms
سنبلة (sunbulah): Ear of grain (like wheat ear).
قشر (qishr): Husk, peel, or pod covering the edible part.
ربا (ribā): Usury; prohibited increase in barter transactions of same type.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on selling wheat in its ear for wheat in its ear?
A) Permissible
B) Prohibited due to riba
C) Permissible only if equal in weight
D) Permissible with option

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits selling grain in its husk but prohibits exchanging it for the same type in husk due to the risk of riba, and distinguishes between beneficial coverings (husks) and non-beneficial ones (pits).

Sale with Exception of a Known Quantity: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi ruling dispute on sale with exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding a sale with exception of a known quantity and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of their disagreement.

Core Idea
Exception of a known quantity may render the remainder unknown, leading to void sale (Abu Hanifa) or valid with option (Abu Yusuf).

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning a sale where the seller excludes a specific known quantity from the sold item. The disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf centers on whether such an exception creates uncertainty about the remainder, thus affecting the validity of the contract. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school balances precision in transactional terms with practical flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Understand the exact statement: 'I sell you this food for 1000 dirhams except 10 qafiz of it.' The sale involves a known exception of a specified quantity from a single item.
The exception is '10 qafiz', which is a known measure.
2
State Abu Hanifa's ruling and reasoning
Abu Hanifa rules the sale fasid (void). His reasoning: the exception leaves the remainder unspecified until the 10 qafiz are separated, creating gharar (uncertainty) in the object of sale.
Gharar in the subject matter invalidates the contract.
3
State Abu Yusuf's ruling and reasoning
Abu Yusuf rules the sale jā'iz (valid) with the buyer having the khiyār (option) once the 10 qafiz are separated. His reasoning: the exception itself is known, and upon separation, the remainder becomes determined; the option protects the buyer.
The option allows the buyer to approve or reject after seeing the remainder.
4
Analyze the contested element
The key issue is whether the exception renders the sale price–to–goods relationship uncertain. Abu Hanifa looks at the moment of contract; Abu Yusuf considers the eventual determination.
This illustrates a difference in temporal focus: immediate vs. eventual certainty.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Under Abu Hanifa, the contract is void ab initio; no rights or obligations arise. Under Abu Yusuf, the contract is valid, and the buyer can choose to uphold or cancel it after separation.
The thamarah al-khilaf is whether the buyer can enforce the sale or must void it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التتارخانية : لو قال أبيعك هذا الطعام بألف درهم إلا عشرة أقفزة منها فالبيع فاسد في قول أبي حنيفة، وفي قول أبي يوسف البيع جائز، وللمشتري الخيار إذا عزل منه العشرة أقفزة.
And in al-Tatar Khaniyya: If he says, 'I sell you this food for one thousand dirhams except ten qafiz of it,' then the sale is void in the opinion of Abu Hanifa, and in the opinion of Abu Yusuf the sale is valid, and the buyer has the option when the ten qafiz are separated from it.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a sale where the seller excludes a specific known quantity from the sold item. Abu Hanifa rules the sale void (فاسد) because the exception implies a sale of an unknown quantity (the remaining food minus ten qafiz) which creates uncertainty. Abu Yusuf, however, rules the sale valid with the buyer having the option (خيار) once the excluded quantity is separated. The difference hinges on whether the exception renders the remainder undetermined. Abu Yusuf considers that by separating the exception, the remainder becomes known, so the sale is valid with a choice for the buyer.

Key Terms
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): Exception; excluding a part from the sold item.
الخيار (al-khiyār): Option; the right of the buyer to cancel or uphold the sale after a defect or uncertainty becomes apparent.
الغرر (al-gharar): Uncertainty or ambiguity in a contract that may lead to dispute, often invalidating the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling on selling food for 1000 dirhams except 10 qafiz?
A) The sale is valid.
B) The sale is void.
C) The sale is valid with a condition.
D) The sale is valid if the buyer agrees.

Key Takeaway
In an intra-Hanafi dispute on a sale with exception of a known quantity, Abu Hanifa rules it fasid due to gharar, while Abu Yusuf rules it jā'iz with a buyer's option; the mufta bihi is typically Abu Hanifa's view.

Selling Ground Floor While Retaining Upper Floor
Ruling on selling ground floor with retained right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling part of a property while retaining rights over another part and explain the legal basis using the analogy from grain in its ear.

Core Idea
Selling the ground floor while keeping the upper floor is valid because each part is independently valuable property.

This lesson examines a specific property sale: selling the ground floor of a house while reserving the right to the upper floor. The Hanafi school, as recorded in al-Dhahiriyya, declares this sale valid, drawing an analogy to the sale of grain in its ear. Understanding this ruling clarifies how separate parts of a single property can be owned and transacted independently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and its condition
The sale involves the ground floor (sufal) with a condition that the seller retains the right (ḥaqq qarār) to the upper floor. Recognize that this is not a sale of the entire house but a partial sale with a retained right.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The text explicitly says 'it is valid' (yajuz). Note that the ruling is sahih (valid) and not fasid or batil.
The ruling comes from al-Dhahiriyya, a source of nawadir (rare transmissions) but relied upon here.
3
Understand the legal basis through analogy
The author compares this sale to selling wheat in its ear or beans in their pods. Just as grain in its husk is valuable and can be sold, the ground floor is a separate beneficial asset. The condition does not create uncertainty (gharar) because the upper floor is a separate known entity.
The analogy highlights that the item sold is maal mutaqawwim (valuable property) and the buyer benefits from it.
4
Recognize the broader principle
Separable parts of a property can be sold independently if each part is identifiable and the transaction is free from ambiguity. This principle applies to various scenarios in Hanafi property law.
This ruling shows that ownership can be divided horizontally (ground vs. upper floor) as well as vertically.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقدمنا عن الظهيرية أنه لو باع سفل داره على أن يكون له حق قرار العلو عليه فإنه يجوز قوله : (كبيع بر في سنبله وباقلا في قشره) أي صحيح لأنه مال متقوم منتفع به فيجوز بيعه في قشره كالشعير

His statement (like selling wheat in its ear and beans in their pods) meaning it is valid because it is valuable property that benefits, so its sale is permissible in its pod like barley.

Commentary
This passage references a ruling from al-Dhahiriyya regarding the sale of the ground floor (sufal) of a house while the seller reserves the right (ḥaqq qarār) to the upper floor. The author confirms that such a sale is valid. He then connects this to the earlier ruling about selling grain in its ear, explaining that both are valid because the item sold is valuable property. The quote from the matn about grain in ear is repeated to emphasize the analogy.

Key Terms
سفل (sufal): The ground floor or lower part of a house.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, selling part of a property while retaining rights over another part is valid if the parts are separable and the condition is clear.

Selling Grain in Ear and Legumes in Pods: Validity Based on Valuable Property
Ruling on validity of selling grain in ear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that selling grain in its ear is valid and explain the proof using the concept of mal mutaqawwim and analogy (qiyas).

Core Idea
Selling grain in its ear is valid because it is valuable property; the covering is negligible.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common agricultural transaction: selling wheat while still in the ear or beans while in the pod. The Hanafi school rules such sales valid, provided the grain is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). The proof is a rational analogy (qiyas) to barley, which is also sold in its husk, supported by customary practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is selling grain (wheat) while it is still in its ear, and legumes (beans) in their pods. The Hanafi ruling is that such a sale is valid (sahih).
2
Analyze the Basis: Mutaqawwim Property
The author states the sale is valid because the grain is considered valuable property (mal mutaqawwim) that provides benefit. In Hanafi law, any property that is permissible to use and has value can be sold, even if it has a covering.
The covering (ear/pod) is not the primary object but is included with the valuable substance.
3
Locate the Analogical Proof (Qiyas)
The text analogizes to barley, which is also sold in its husk. This establishes that sale of grain in its covering is permissible by analogy. The common practice (e.g., eating beans) further supports this.
The qiyas is based on the shared illa: both are valuable property that benefits.
4
Apply the Ruling to Other Items
The author extends the ruling to rice, sesame, walnuts, almonds, and pistachios—all can be sold in their husks or shells. Each is mal mutaqawwim.
The list is illustrative, not exhaustive; any similar item would be valid.
5
Address Potential Objection (Gharar)
Some scholars might object due to uncertainty (gharar) about the quality of the hidden grain. The Hanafi response is that the grain is the substance of value; the covering is negligible and custom treats the sale as valid.
This is a rational (aqli) proof based on the nature of the property and customary practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كبيع بر في سنبله وباقلاً في قشره أي صحيح لأنه مال متقوم منتفع به فيجوز بيعه في قشره كالشعير وفي البناية ومن أكل الفولية يشهد بذلك وكذا الأرز والسمسم والجوز واللوز والفستق.
Like selling wheat in its ear and beans in their pods, meaning it is valid because it is valuable property that benefits, so its sale is permissible in its pod like barley. And in al-Binaya: And the one who eats beans testifies to that, and likewise rice, sesame, walnuts, almonds, and pistachios.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the commentary explaining the validity of selling grain while still in its ear or legumes while still in their pods. The author states that such a sale is valid (صحيح) because the item is considered valuable property (مال متقوم) that provides benefit, so selling it in its covering is permissible, analogous to selling barley similarly. He cites al-Binaya and notes that common practice (such as eating beans) confirms this. The same ruling applies to rice, sesame, walnuts, almonds, and pistachios—all can be sold in their husks or shells.

Key Terms
مال متقوم (māl mutaqawwim): Property that is legally recognized and has value; can be sold.
سنبله (sunbulah): Ear of grain (e.g., wheat, barley).

D) It is valid only for barley, not wheat.

The Hanafi school validates selling grain in its ear because the grain is valuable property, and the covering is negligible; custom and analogy support this.

Prohibition of Selling Wheat in Ear for Its Like
Ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi objection-response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling wheat in ear for its like, explain the reason based on potential riba, and distinguish this from selling cotton seeds in cotton or date pits in dates using the concept of 'adam hālik.

Core Idea
Selling wheat in ear for its like is prohibited because the pure wheat equality cannot be ensured, risking riba.

This passage from Bahr al-Rāʾiq addresses a specific riba-related prohibition: selling wheat that is still in its ear for a like amount of wheat also in its ear. The concern is that the actual quantity of pure grain may differ, leading to unequal exchange, which is ribā. The author then raises an objection: why is selling cotton seeds in cotton or date pits in dates also prohibited, even though they too are in a covering? Abū Yūsuf provides a nuanced answer based on custom (ʿurf), distinguishing cases where the enclosed item is considered non-existent as a separate commodity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its justification
Read the opening statement: 'And its sale is not permissible in exchange for its like from the ear of wheat'. Note that the reason given is 'due to the possibility of riba' (iḥtimāl al-ribā). This shows the ruling is ḥarām because of uncertainty in equality.
The ruling applies only to like-for-like exchanges (same genus). If selling wheat in ear for a different genus, other rules apply.
2
Analyze the objection raised
The text then mentions an objection: 'what is the difference between selling cotton seeds in specific cotton or date pits in specific dates?' The objector argues that these cases are similar because the seeds/pits are also inside their husks, yet their like-for-like sale is also prohibited. The reader must understand why the same reasoning might seem to apply.
The objection is based on apparent analogy (qiyās): both involve a hidden item inside a covering.
3
Examine Abū Yūsuf's distinction
Abū Yūsuf (may Allah have mercy on him) responds by pointing out that in the case of cotton seeds and date pits, the hidden item is considered 'non-existent and perishable in custom' (ʿadam hālik). This means that in customary practice, seeds and pits are not treated as independent commodities when still inside the fruit; they are seen as part of the fruit. Therefore, selling them separately would involve non-existent goods.
This distinction is based on ʿurf (custom), a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the concept of 'adam hālik to the original ruling
In contrast, wheat in its ear is considered existing and valuable in custom; the grain is the primary object of sale. Thus, the potential inequality in the amount of pure wheat creates a genuine riba concern. The covering (ear) does not render the grain non-existent.
The key is customary recognition: what is considered a separate, valuable entity in ʿurf.
5
Confirm the muftā bihi position
The passage affirms that the prohibition stands for wheat in ear like-for-like, and the distinction from seeds/pits is valid. The relied-upon position is that such a sale is not permissible.
This ruling is from the Ẓāhir al-Riwāya and is the operative position for fatwā.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يجوز بيعه بمثله من سنبل الحنطة لاحتمال الربا كما في فتح القدير. وقدمنا أنه لا يجوز بيع قصيل البر بحنطة والقصيل الشعير يجز أخضر لعلف الدواب؛ كذا في المصباح. وأورد المطالبة بالفرق بين ما إذا باع حب قطن في قطن بعينه أو نوى تمر في تمر بعينه أي باع ما في هذا القطن من الحب أو ما في هذا التمر من النوى فإنه لا يجوز مع أنه أيضاً في غلافه. وأشار أبو يوسف إلى الفرق بأن النوى هنالك عدماً هالكاً معتبر في العرف على أن يكون له حق المرور منه قياساً على ما سبق قريباً وهو ظاهر ولم أره. قوله: (ولا يجوز بيعه بمثله من سنبل (الحنطة قال الرملي : أي بيع البر في سنبله وسيأتي في الربا أن الحنطة بيع الخالصة بحنطة في سنبلها لا يجوز، ويجب تقييده بما إذا لم تكن الحنطة الخالصة أكثر من التي
And its sale is not permissible in exchange for its like from the ear of wheat due to the possibility of riba, as in Fath al-Qadir. And we previously mentioned that it is not permissible to sell green barley (qaṣīl) for wheat, and the green barley is cut green for animal fodder; likewise in al-Misbah. And it is objected: what is the difference between selling cotton seeds in specific cotton or date pits in specific dates, i.e., selling what is in this cotton of seeds or what is in this date of pits, for that is not permissible while it is also in its covering. And Abu Yusuf indicated the difference: that the pits there are non-existent and perishable in custom, on condition that he has the right of passage from it by analogy to what preceded recently, and it is apparent and I have not seen it. His statement: (And its sale is not permissible in exchange for its like from the ear of wheat) al-Ramli said: i.e., selling wheat in its ear, and it will come in riba that selling pure wheat for wheat in its ear is not permissible, and it must be restricted to when the pure wheat is not more than that which...
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage addresses the prohibition of selling like-for-like when the item is in its covering. Selling wheat in its ear for wheat in its ear is not allowed because of the potential for riba, as the amount of pure wheat may differ. Similarly, selling green barley (qaṣīl) for wheat is prohibited because they are different genera, but the focus here is on the same genus. The author then raises an objection: why is it invalid to sell cotton seeds in cotton or date pits in dates, even though they are also in their husks? Abu Yusuf answers that in those cases, the seeds/pits are considered non-existent as a separate entity in custom (ʿurf), so they cannot be sold separately. This differs from wheat in ear, where the grain is considered existing. The passage also includes a comment from al-Ramli clarifying that the prohibition is for like-for-like.

Key Terms
الربا (al-ribā): Usury or interest; prohibited increase in exchange of certain commodities.
العدم الهالك (al-ʿadam al-hālik): Non-existent or perishable; something not considered existing in custom.
السنبل (al-sunbul): Ear of grain; the spike containing grains.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the prohibition on selling wheat in ear for its like differ from selling cotton seeds in cotton for its like?
A) Because cotton seeds are smaller and harder to measure.
B) Because cotton seeds are considered non-existent in custom, while wheat grain is considered existing.
C) Because the covering of cotton is not an ear.
D) Because wheat is a staple food and cotton is not.

Key Takeaway
Selling wheat in ear for its like is ḥarām due to potential ribā, but cotton seeds in cotton are prohibited for a different reason—they are 'adam hālik—illustrating the role of custom in Hanafi fiqh.

The 'Urfi Existence Condition for Valid Sales
Establishes the 'urfi existence condition for sale validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of 'urfi existence to determine the validity of a sale of goods still contained in their source.

Core Idea
A sale is invalid if the object is customarily non-existent, even if physically present.

When you buy milk, you expect to receive liquid milk, not the udder of a goat. The Hanafi school enforces this common-sense understanding: the object of sale must be considered existent in the custom of merchants. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains why sales of milk in the udder, meat on the bone, or oil inside olives are invalid, and it contrasts the special case of goldsmiths' dust where the prohibition is based on riba, not non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling and its basis
Locate the sentence stating that the sale of items like milk in the udder is not concluded (yan'aqid). Note the reason: such items are 'ma'dum fil-'urf' (non-existent in custom). This is the primary legal cause (illah).
The ruling is batil (invalid), not merely fasid.
2
Understand the 'urf criterion through examples
Examine the examples: dates vs. pits, wheat in ear vs. flour. In each pair, the first is customarily considered existent (people say 'this is wheat'), the second is not. This shows that 'urf determines what counts as 'existent' for the contract.
The criterion is linguistic custom: what speakers of Arabic would naturally refer to as a distinct commodity.
3
Contrast with goldsmiths' dust (turab al-saghah)
Why is goldsmiths' dust treated differently? The text states its sale with its own kind is invalid due to riba, not non-existence. So it can be sold for a different kind. This confirms that the general prohibition is based on 'adam 'urfi, not on physical hiddenness or impurity.
The contrast sharpens the definition: turab al-saghah is 'existent' in custom, but riba prohibits equal exchange.
4
Apply the rule to the listed cases
The author lists milk in udder, meat and fat in sheep, trotters, skin, flour in wheat, oil in olives, juice in grapes. For each, explain: people do not say 'this is oil' of the oil inside the olive; they only say so after extraction. Hence the sale is batil.
All these cases share the same illah: the item is not yet a separate, identifiable commodity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينعقد لا لأنه يصير فإنه يقال هذا تمر وقطن ولا يقال هذا نوى في ثمره ولا حب في قطنه، ويقال هذه حنطة في سنبلها وهذا لوز وفستق ولا يقال هذه قشور فيها لوز ولا يذهب إليه وهم بخلاف تراب الصاغة فإنه إنما لا يجوز بيعه بجنسه لاحتمال الربا حتى لو باع بخلاف جنسه جاز، وفي مسألتنا لو باع بجنسه لا يجوز لشبهة الربا والصاغة جمع صائغ والمراد بيع برادة الذهب كما في البناية. وما ذكرنا يخرج الجواب عن امتناع بيع اللبن في الضرع، واللحم والشحم في الشاة، والإلية والأكارع والجلد فيها، والدقيق في الحنطة، والزيت في الزيتون، والعصير في العنب ونحو ذلك حيث لا يجوز لأن كل ذلك منعدم في العرف لا يقال هذا عصير وزيت محله فكذا الباقي
It is not concluded because it becomes [non-existent]. For it is said 'this is dates and cotton' and it is not said 'this is pits in its fruit' nor 'seed in its cotton', and it is said 'this is wheat in its ear' and 'this is almonds and pistachios' and it is not said 'these are shells containing almonds' and the mind does not go to that, contrary to goldsmiths' dust, for it is only invalid to sell it with its own kind due to possibility of riba, so if he sells it for a different kind it is valid, whereas in our question if he sells for its own kind it is invalid due to resemblance of riba. 'Al-Saghah' is plural of 'sa'igh' meaning goldsmith, and the meaning is the sale of gold filings as in al-Binayah. What we have mentioned answers the prohibition of selling milk in the udder, meat and fat in the sheep, the tail, trotters, and skin in it, flour in the wheat, oil in the olive, juice in the grape and the like where it is invalid, because all that is non-existent in custom; it is not said 'this is juice and its place is oil', likewise for the rest.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage tackles the ruling on selling items that are still contained within their source and not yet separated. The key principle is that in common custom, such items are considered non-existent. For example, one says 'these are dates' but not 'these are date pits in the dates'; similarly, 'wheat in its ear' is considered existent, whereas 'milk in the udder' is not. The author draws a contrast with goldsmiths' dust, which contains gold particles; its sale is restricted only when sold for the same substance due to riba, but if sold for a different substance it is valid. The underlying reasoning (ta'lil) for the general prohibition is that these items are customarily nonexistent ('adam fi al-'urf). The author then applies this rule to numerous examples: milk in udder, meat and fat in a sheep, the tail and trotters and skin on it, flour in wheat, oil in olives, juice in grapes, etc. All such sales are invalid because in common parlance one does not say 'this is juice'—rather the substance is not yet actualized. This ruling is based on the Hanafi position that existence ('urf) is a condition for the object of sale.

Key Terms
معدوم عرفاً (ma'dum 'urfan): An item that is considered non-existent by common custom even if physically present, such as milk in the udder.
تراب الصاغة (turab al-saghah): Dust from goldsmiths' workshops containing gold or silver filings.
ينعقد (yan'aqid): The contract is concluded or formed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling milk in the udder invalid according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because milk is najis.
B) Because it is not measurable.
C) Because it is considered non-existent in custom.
D) Because it leads to riba.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the condition of 'urfi existence is a distinct prerequisite for a valid sale, separate from physical existence or measurement.

Option After Extraction and Ruling on Straw in Ear
Distinguishes validity of options and existence conditions in sales.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when the buyer has an option after extracting hidden goods and explain why selling straw in the ear is invalid while selling a beam in the ceiling is valid.

Core Idea
Straw in the ear is non-existent before threshing; selling it is invalid, unlike a beam in the ceiling.

The previous lesson covered the rules of selling wheat in its ear. This passage expands on two related points: the buyer's option after extraction of any hidden item, and a key distinction — selling straw alone in the ear is invalid because straw does not yet exist, whereas selling a beam fixed in the ceiling is valid because the beam exists as a distinct object. Understanding this distinction sharpens your ability to apply the condition of existence in sales.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling on option after extraction
The passage states that for any item sold in its cover (e.g., milk in udder, wheat in ear), the buyer has an option after extraction because he had not seen the actual item. This is based on the principle of khiyar al-ru'yah.
This option is valid even if the item matches the description.
2
Differentiate between selling wheat in ear and selling straw in ear
Selling wheat in ear is valid; selling straw in ear without the wheat is invalid. Why? Because straw does not exist as straw until it is threshed. Before threshing, it is merely part of the plant and not a separate commodity.
The key is existence of the sold object at the time of contract.
3
Compare with the case of a beam in the ceiling
Selling a beam (al-jidh') that is set in the ceiling is valid, even though it is still attached. Why? Because the beam already exists as a distinct object; removal does not create it anew. Thus, the sale is valid and the buyer must accept it after removal.
The contrast highlights that existence depends on the object being a separate entity, not on its physical attachment.
4
Apply the condition of existence to other scenarios
Generalize: any sale of something that does not yet exist at the time of contract is batil (void). This includes future crops, unprocessed goods, etc. The only exceptions are cases permitted by Shariah (e.g., salam, istisna).
This is a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh: the object must be existent and ascertainable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الوجه يقتضى ثبوت الخيار بعد الاستخراج في ذلك كله لأنه لم يره ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. قيد بيع الحنطة لأنه لو باع تبن الحنطة في سنبلها دون الحنطة لم تبناً إلا بالعلاج وهو الدق فلم يكن تبناً قبله فكان بيع المعدوم فلا ينعقد بخلاف الجذع في السقف أنه ينعقد حتى لو نزعه وسلمه أجبر على الأخذ وهنا لا؛ كذا في البدائع.
And know that the apparent view requires establishing the option after extraction in all of that, because he has not seen it; thus in Fath al-Qadir. The ruling is restricted to the sale of wheat, because if he sells the straw of wheat in its ear without the wheat, it is not straw except by treatment which is threshing, so it was not straw before that, so it would be a sale of a non-existent item and thus not concluded, unlike the beam in the ceiling, for that is concluded, so that even if he removes it and delivers it, he is compelled to accept it, but not here; thus in al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage first states that when a buyer purchases something that is hidden (e.g., milk in udder or wheat in ear), after extraction he has the option (khiyar al-ru'yah) because he had not seen the actual item. This is based on the principle in Fath al-Qadir. Then it qualifies the earlier ruling about selling wheat in its ear: if someone sells the straw (al-tibn) that is in the ear separately from the wheat, that sale is invalid because the straw does not exist as straw until it is threshed (al-daq). Before threshing, it is not straw but merely a part of the plant, so it is a non-existent object. In contrast, selling a beam (al-jidh') that is fixed in the ceiling is valid because the beam exists as a distinct entity; even if the seller removes it and delivers, the buyer must accept. The difference is that the beam is already a separate item, whereas straw in the ear requires processing to become straw.

Key Terms
الخيار بعد الاستخراج (al-khiyar ba'd al-istikhraj): The buyer's option to return the goods after they are extracted from their cover, based on seeing them for the first time.
تبن (tibn): Straw, which is the dried stalks of grain after threshing, considered a separate commodity only after processing.
الجذع في السقف (al-jidh' fi al-saqf): A beam or rafter set into the ceiling, treated as an existing item even before removal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is selling a beam in the ceiling valid but selling straw in the ear invalid?
A) Because the beam is larger than the straw.
B) Because the beam already exists as a separate entity, while the straw requires processing to become separate.
C) Because the beam is fixed, whereas straw is loose.
D) Because the buyer can see the beam but not the straw.

Key Takeaway
A sale is valid only if the sold object exists as a separate entity at the time of contract; hidden items grant the buyer an option after extraction.

Ruling on Selling Goldsmiths' Dust
Ruling with proof via qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid sale of goldsmiths' dust and apply the Hanafi ruling on riba in such transactions.

Core Idea
Goldsmiths' dust is only valuable if it contains gold or silver — otherwise the sale is invalid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq tackles a practical issue in the goldsmith industry: the dust that accumulates in workshops may contain precious metal particles. The Hanafi school distinguishes between selling it for its own kind (invalid due to riba) and selling it for a different kind (valid only if actual gold or silver is present). We also encounter an ethical warning from Abu Yusuf about consuming the price.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is selling goldsmiths' dust. The ruling depends on two factors: whether the exchange is for the same metal (e.g., gold for gold) or a different commodity, and whether actual gold/silver is present in the dust.
This is a conditional ruling, not absolute.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites no direct text but uses an analogy (qiyas) to a prohibited transaction: selling one dirham and two dinars for one dinar and two dirhams, which involves uncertainty (gharar) and potential riba. Also relies on the concept of mal mutaqawwim.
The dust itself is not mal mutaqawwim unless it contains valuable metal.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The uncertainty in the amount of gold or silver in the dust creates a possibility of riba when exchanging it for the same metal. When selling for a different commodity, the presence of metal makes the dust valuable; otherwise, it is worthless dust.
The value is in the metal, not the dust.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf adds an ethical caution: the goldsmith should not consume the price of the dust unless he already compensated customers for the metal that likely fell from their goods. This is a variant within the Hanafi school.
This is not a contradiction but an additional ethical layer.
5
Evaluate the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference is that under Abu Yusuf's view, the goldsmith must ensure he is not consuming property that belongs to customers, making the transaction potentially invalid if not compensated.
This shows the Hanafi concern for both legal validity and ethical consumption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بتراب الصاغة التراب الذي فيه ذرات الذهب فلا يجوز بيعه بجنسه لاحتمال الربا، ولا ينصرف إلى خلاف الجنس تحرياً للجواز كما في بيع درهم ودينارين بدينار ودرهمين لأن التراب ليس بمال متقوم؛ كذا في المعراج ولو اشترى تراب الصواغين بعرض إن وجد في التراب ذهباً أو فضة جاز بيعه لأنه باع مالاً متقوماً، وإن لم يجد شيئاً من ذلك لا يجوز لأن التراب غير مقصود وإنما المقصود ما فيه من الذهب والفضة وقال أبو يوسف: لا ينبغي للصائغ أن يأكل ثمن التراب الذي باعه لأن فيه مال الناس إلا أن يكون الصائغ قد زاد الناس في متاعهم بقدر ما سقط منهم في التراب وكذا الدهان إذا باع الدهن وبقي من الدهن شيء في الأوعية؛ كذا في الخانية
And what is meant by goldsmiths' dust is the dust that contains particles of gold. So it is not permissible to sell it for its own kind due to the possibility of riba, and it does not turn to selling for a different kind seeking permissibility, as in the sale of one dirham and two dinars for one dinar and two dirhams, because the dust is not a valuable property; thus in al-Mi'raj. And if he buys goldsmiths' dust for a commodity, if there is found in the dust gold or silver, the sale is valid because he sold valuable property, but if nothing of that is found, it is not valid because the dust is not intended; rather the intended thing is what is in it of gold and silver. And Abu Yusuf said: It is not proper for the goldsmith to consume the price of the dust he sold because it contains people's property, unless the goldsmith had increased [the price] in their goods by the amount that fell from them in the dust. Likewise the oil seller if he sells the oil and some oil remains in the vessels; thus in al-Khaniyah.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on selling goldsmiths' dust (turab al-saghah). The dust is considered to have potential gold/silver content. The ruling: selling it for the same metal (e.g., gold dust for gold) is invalid because of the possibility of riba—since the exact amount of gold is unknown, it resembles a sale of a non-existent quantity. But selling it for a different kind (e.g., for wheat) is permissible only if gold is actually found; if not, the sale is invalid because the dust itself is not a valuable property (mal mutaqawwim). The author gives an analogy: selling one dirham plus two dinars for one dinar plus two dirhams—a prohibited transaction due to uncertainty. Then he narrates from Abu Yusuf an ethical ruling: the goldsmith should not consume the price of the dust because it may include particles belonging to customers; however, if the goldsmith had already compensated the customers by increasing the price of their goods by the amount of the loss, then it is permissible. Similarly, an oil seller who sells oil that has left residue in the vessels should consider the same principle. These rulings are from al-Khaniyah.

Key Terms
تراب الصاغة (turab al-saghah): Dust from goldsmiths' workshops containing trace amounts of gold or silver.
مال متقوم (mal mutaqawwim): Valuable property that is legally recognized and can be sold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is selling goldsmiths' dust for a commodity valid?
A) Whenever the buyer agrees.
B) Only if gold or silver is found in the dust.
C) Only if sold for gold or silver.
D) It is never valid.

Key Takeaway
Goldsmiths' dust is a valuable property only if it contains gold or silver; otherwise its sale is invalid, and riba concerns apply when exchanging it for the same metal.

Rulings on Sales of Unprocessed Agricultural Goods
Agricultural sales: ginned cotton, wheat in ear, date pits, seeds in watermelon, cotton by identification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on selling ginned cotton, wheat in ear, date pits, seeds in watermelon, and cotton by specific identification, and explain the basis for invalidity or validity.

Core Idea
A sale of an item still embedded or hidden is invalid unless it is considered existent by custom or specifically identified.

In Hanafi fiqh, the object of sale must be known, existent, and deliverable. This lesson examines several agricultural cases from al-Khaniyah where the item is still inside its product or not yet separated. The rulings illustrate how hiddenness or inseparability can invalidate a sale, and how custom or specific identification can validate it.

Guided Path
1
List each item and its ruling
Go through the passage and note for each item: ginned cotton (invalid), wheat in ear (valid), date pits (invalid), seeds in watermelon (invalid), cotton by specific identification (valid).
2
Identify the common principle for invalidity
The invalid cases share a lack of existence or knowability: ginned cotton not yet defined (though ginned, the amount is uncertain), date pits inseparable from dates, seeds hidden in watermelon. The valid cases (wheat in ear, cotton by specific identification) overcome this through custom or specificity.
Wheat in ear is considered existent by custom, and cotton by specific identification makes the object known.
3
Analyze the exception of wheat in ear
Although wheat is inside the ear, it is still considered an existing and known commodity because custom treats it as such for sale. This shows the role of 'urf in Hanafi fiqh.
Compare to seeds in watermelon: custom does not treat them as a separate commodity until extracted.
4
Examine the ruling on seeds in watermelon
Buying seeds inside the watermelon is invalid even if the owner agrees to cut it, because at the time of contract the seeds are not yet separated and their quantity and quality are unknown.
The buyer only gets an option (khiyar) after seeing, but the contract itself is invalid.
5
Understand the validity of selling cotton by specific identification
When the cotton is identified 'bi-'aynihi' (by its specific identity), the object is known and existent, so the sale is valid. This is the chosen view of Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi.
This highlights that specification can cure the defect of uncertainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفيها أيضاً لو باع مائة من من حليج هذا القطن لا يجوز، ولو كانت الحنطة في سنبلها فباعها جاز ولا يجوز بيع النوى في التمر، ولو باع . قطن بعينه جاز؛ كذا اختاره الفقيه أبو الليث، ولو اشترى البزر الذي في جوف البطيخ لا يجوز وإن رضي صاحبه بأن يقطع البطيخ، ولو ذبح شاة فباع كرشها قبل السلخ جاز وكان على البائع إخراجه وتسليمه إلى المشتري وللمشتري خيار الرؤية ولو ابتلعت دجاجة لؤلؤة في سنبلها وقد صرح بذلك في الخانية ويعلم بذلك أنه يجوز بيع التي في سنبلها معه بالأخرى التي في سنبلها معه صرف رفاً للجنس إلى خلافه تأمل
And in it also: if he sells a hundred [units] of ginned cotton, it is not valid. And if wheat is in its ear and he sells it, it is valid. And selling date pits in the dates is not valid. And if he sells cotton by specific identification, it is valid; thus al-Faqih Abu al-Layth chose. And if he buys the seeds inside the watermelon, it is not valid even if the owner agrees to cut the watermelon. And if he slaughters a sheep and sells its stomach before flaying, it is valid, and it is upon the seller to remove it and deliver it to the buyer, and the buyer has the option of seeing. And if a chicken swallows a pearl in its ear [?] and that is stated in al-Khaniyah, and it is known by that that it is permissible to sell that which is in its ear with it for another that is in its ear with it, as a change of category to its opposite, reflect.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage collects several specific rulings from al-Khaniyah. The first: selling ginned cotton (haleej al-qutn) is invalid because the cotton is not yet fully separated? Actually 'haleej' means ginned cotton, so why invalid? Possibly because it is still in the pod? The text is unclear. Then: selling wheat in its ear is valid, but selling date pits in the dates is invalid. Selling cotton by specific identification (bi-'aynihi) is valid according to Abu al-Layth. Buying seeds inside a watermelon is invalid even if the owner agrees to cut it. Selling the stomach (karish) of a slaughtered sheep before flaying is valid; the seller must extract and deliver, and the buyer has khiyar al-ru'yah. Finally, a case: if a chicken swallows a pearl, and it is in its ear (?)—maybe 'in its crop'—the sale of the chicken with the pearl is valid, and by analogy, selling wheat in its ear for another wheat in its ear is valid if done as a change of category (i.e., exchanging like for like with immediate possession? The text is ambiguous). The commentator says 'reflect' (tamal).

Key Terms
حليج (haleej): Ginned cotton, i.e., cotton with seeds removed but not yet baled or measured.
خيار الرؤية (khiyar al-ru'yah): The buyer's option to return the item after seeing it for the first time.
بزر البطيخ (bizr al-batikh): Seeds inside a watermelon.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Sales of hidden or unseparated items are generally invalid in Hanafi law unless custom or specific identification makes the object known and existent.
