Sales of Animal Parts and Exchanges of Hidden Items
Animal part sale and exchange of hidden grains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on selling the stomach of a slaughtered sheep before flaying and explain the case of a chicken swallowing a pearl, including the option of seeing and the rules for exchanging unseen items.

Core Idea
Selling an animal part before full separation is valid if it can be delivered; exchanging like for like while hidden must avoid riba.

This lesson covers two distinct cases from al-Khaniyah: first, selling the stomach of a freshly slaughtered sheep before flaying; second, a complex case involving a chicken that swallowed a pearl and the implications for exchanging wheat still in its ear. The rulings highlight the importance of deliverability, the option of seeing, and the avoidance of riba in exchanges of food items.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the stomach sale case
A sheep is slaughtered but not yet skinned. The stomach is sold. The ruling: valid. Reason: the stomach is a defined, existing part that can be separated. The seller must extract it and deliver. The buyer has khiyar al-ru'yah because the exact state of the stomach is unseen.
The stomach is not najis ritually, but even if it were, sale might be valid if the buyer can use it lawfully (e.g., for feeding animals).
2
Understand khiyar al-ru'yah
Since the buyer did not see the stomach at the time of contract, they have the option to return it after seeing if it is not as expected.
This option applies in all cases where the object is unseen.
3
Examine the chicken and pearl case
A chicken swallows a pearl (or similar item). The text says it is known that selling the chicken with the pearl inside is valid, and by analogy, exchanging wheat in ear for wheat in ear is valid as a change of category (sarf? actually it's a barter of the same genus).
The text is ambiguous; the pearl is in the chicken's crop (not ear). The ruling validates the sale and extends to grain exchanges.
4
Apply the principle to grain exchanges
Exchanging wheat in ear for wheat in ear is permissible if done as a change of category, meaning it is not considered ribawi like-for-like exchange because the ear is a covering that differentiates the two? This avoids riba by making it a sale of a different category.
The commentator says 'sarf rifan li al-jins ila khilafihi' – a change of category to its opposite, meaning the ear itself is considered a separate commodity.
5
Reflect on the underlying fiqh principles
The cases illustrate that hidden items can be sold if they are deliverable and there is no riba. The chicken case shows that the item inside remains part of the chicken for sale purposes.
Tamal: the commentator invites deep reflection.

Selling Chopped Wheat for Wheat: Genus-Based Permissibility
Refines a ruling on genus in wheat sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on selling chopped wheat for wheat and explain why the difference in genus renders the exchange permissible despite both being wheat derivatives.

Core Idea
Chopped wheat and dry wheat are different genera; exchanging them is not riba.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq refines a previous statement that selling chopped wheat for wheat is impermissible. The commentator, Ibn Nujaym, cites Jami' al-Fusulayn to clarify that the exchange is actually permissible due to the lack of genus (jinas). This lesson focuses on how genus classification affects riba rulings and demonstrates the importance of verifying textual transmissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial claim
Note the earlier statement: 'It is not permissible to sell chopped wheat for wheat.' This is the claim being refined.
The claim appears in the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq.
2
Locate the refined ruling
Ibn Nujaym transmits from Jami' al-Fusulayn that buying chopped wheat for wheat is permissible, whether by measure or estimation.
This contradicts the initial claim.
3
Identify the basis for permissibility
The basis is 'adam al-jinas' (lack of genus). Chopped green wheat and dry wheat are considered different genera, so riba rules do not apply.
Hanafi usul: riba only applies within the same genus when exchanged by weight or measure.
4
Evaluate the scribal error suggestion
Ibn Nujaym suggests that the negative particle 'la' in the initial claim might be a scribal addition, implying the original perhaps said 'yajuz' (permissible). Reflect (tamal) indicates caution.
This shows the importance of textual criticism in fiqh.
5
Apply the ruling
Thus, the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is permissibility. Any fatwa should follow Jami' al-Fusulayn and the refined position.
This ruling applies to all exchanges of green and dry wheat, regardless of measure or estimate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وقدمنا أنه لا يجوز بيع قصيل البر بحنطة) قال الرملي قدمه في شرح قوله ويباع الطعام كيلاً وجزافاً» وأقول: قدم عن جامع الفصولين شراء قصيل البر بالبر كيلاً وجزافاً جائز لعدم الجناس ولعل حرف النفي من زيادة الكتاب تأمل
His saying: (And we have preceded that it is not permissible to sell chopped wheat for wheat). Al-Ramli said: He presented it in the commentary on his saying 'And food is sold by measure and by estimation.' And I say: He presented from Jami' al-Fusulayn that buying chopped wheat for wheat by measure and by estimation is permissible due to the lack of genus (jinas), and perhaps the negative particle is an addition from the book, reflect.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This is a comment by the author (likely Ibn Nujaym) on a previous statement that it is not permissible to sell chopped green wheat (qasil al-burr) for wheat (hintah). Al-Ramli notes that this was mentioned earlier in the commentary. Then the author quotes from Jami' al-Fusulayn that buying chopped wheat for wheat, whether by measure or estimation, is permissible because they are not of the same genus (la jinas). The word 'la' (not) in 'la yajuz' might be a scribal error; i.e., it could be that it is actually permissible. The commentator says 'reflect' (tamal) suggesting caution. This highlights the importance of whether chopped wheat and dry wheat are considered the same genus for purposes of riba. The Hanafi position is that they are different species, so exchange is allowed even if by weight, because the prohibition of riba only applies to same genus items traded by weight or measure.

Key Terms
قصيل البر (qasil al-burr): Chopped green wheat or young wheat stalks.
الجناس (al-jinas): Legal genus classification affecting riba rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Chopped green wheat and dry wheat are different genera in Hanafi fiqh, so exchanging them is permissible without riba concerns.

Rulings on Pearl in Chicken and Pearl in Shell
Rulings on two sale cases with hidden items

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rulings on selling an item with an unseen component (chicken vs. oyster shell) and identify the mufta bihi position in the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
A sale is valid when the hidden item is part of the sold entity; invalid when hidden and separate.

This lesson examines two related sale scenarios: a chicken that has swallowed a pearl and a pearl still inside an oyster shell. The Hanafi school treats them differently based on whether the hidden item is considered part of the sold object. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi dispute over the oyster shell case, with the relied-upon position being that the sale is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two distinct transactions: (1) a chicken that has swallowed a pearl is sold, and (2) a pearl inside an oyster shell is sold.
2
Determine the ruling for the chicken case
The sale of the chicken with the pearl inside is valid. If the buyer had seen the chicken (and thus the pearl) before purchase, he has no option to return it unless the pearl has changed. If he had not seen it, he has the option of sight (khiyar al-ru'yah) upon seeing the pearl.
The pearl is considered part of the chicken's body, so the sale is of a known entity.
3
Identify the intra-Hanafi dispute on pearl in shell
Abu Yusuf holds the sale valid with the option of sight for the buyer. Muhammad holds it invalid. The text states that the fatwa is upon Muhammad's view.
The disagreement hinges on whether the pearl is considered part of the shell or a separate hidden item.
4
Analyze the basis of difference
Abu Yusuf treats the shell and pearl as a single unit, so the sale is of a potentially valuable item with a known deficiency (the pearl may or may not be present). Muhammad views the pearl as a separate, unknown item, making the sale uncertain (gharar) and thus invalid.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its application
The mufta bihi is Muhammad's ruling that the sale is invalid. Therefore, selling a pearl inside an unopened oyster is not a valid contract. The buyer cannot acquire the pearl through such a sale.
This principle prevents excessive uncertainty in transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فباع حبة اللؤلؤة التي في بطنها جاز ولا خيار للمشتري إن كان رآها إلا إذا تغيرت، وإن لم يكن المشتري رأى اللؤلؤة فله الخيار إذا رآها ولو اشترى لؤلؤة في صدف قال أبو يوسف: يجوز البيع وله الخيار إذا رأى. وقال محمد لا يجوز وعليه الفتوى
So he sold the pearl that is in its belly, it is valid, and there is no option for the buyer if he had seen it, except if it has changed. And if the buyer did not see the pearl, he has the option when he sees it. And if he buys a pearl in an oyster shell, Abu Yusuf said: the sale is valid and he has the option when he sees. And Muhammad said: it is not valid, and upon that is the fatwa.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage deals with two cases: (1) a chicken that has swallowed a pearl – selling the chicken with the pearl inside is valid. However, if the buyer had already seen the chicken (and thus the pearl), he has no option; if he didn't see it, he has khiyar al-ru'yah. If the pearl changes (e.g., becomes damaged), option applies. (2) A pearl in an oyster shell (sadaf): there is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. Abu Yusuf holds the sale is valid with khiyar, while Muhammad says it is invalid. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Muhammad's ruling that it is invalid. The reason for difference: Abu Yusuf considers the shell and pearl as a single entity, while Muhammad sees the pearl as hidden and unknown, hence it is a sale of a non-existent item (since the pearl is not visible). The fatwa is according to Muhammad.

Key Terms
لؤلؤة في صدف (lu'lu'a fi sadaf): A pearl inside an oyster shell, unseen.
لا خيار (la khiyar): No option for the buyer if he had previously seen the item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Valid, with option for buyer.
B) Invalid.
C) Valid only if the seller guarantees the pearl.
D) Valid, without option.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a sale involving an unseen item depends on whether the item is considered part of the sold object; if it is separate and unknown, the sale is invalid.

Selling Covered Items: Customary Existence as Condition
Summary classification of mastur sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a sale of a covered item as valid or invalid based on whether the item is considered existent in custom, and apply this rule to the examples cited in the text.

Core Idea
A sale of a concealed item is valid only if people customarily treat it as a distinct object.

When you sell something hidden by its natural covering—like wheat in the ear or milk in the udder—fiqh must decide if the sale is valid. The Hanafi school looks to custom: if people normally consider the item existent, the sale is valid; if not, it is invalid. This lesson unpacks the classification from the summary passage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is selling a covered item (mastur) where the covering is innate (khilqi). The ruling is conditional on customary existence.
Distinguish from selling an unseen item (gha'ib) which has a different rule.
2
Distinguish existent vs. non-existent in custom
Custom ('urf) determines whether the item is considered a distinct commodity. If people generally refer to the covered item as a separate entity, it is existent.
Examples: wheat in ear (existent); milk in udder (non-existent).
3
Apply the rule to listed examples
Go through the valid examples (rice, walnuts, etc.) and invalid examples (flour in wheat, oil in olives, etc.) to internalize the pattern.
Pay attention to the boundary cases like pearl in oyster.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on pearl in oyster
Abu Yusuf considered a pearl in an oyster as existent because it is often the intended object; Muhammad disagreed, and the mufta bihi follows Muhammad (invalid).
This shows that the rule is applied with some ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والباقلا الفول والحليج بمعنى المحلوج وهو ما خلص حبه من قطنه. وفي البزازية: لو باع حنطة في سنبلها لزم البائع الدوس والتذرية، وكذا لو أطلق له حنطة في سنبلها فصار حاصل ما نقلناه أنه إذا باع شيئاً مستوراً، فإن كان مستوراً بما هو خلقي فيه أولاً والثاني شراء ما لم يره جائز عندنا. والأول لا يخلو إما أن يكون المبيع موجوداً في العرف أو معدوماً، فإن كان موجوداً جاز كبيع في سنبلها وأرز وجوز ولوز وكرش شاة مذبوحة قبل سلخها ولؤلؤة في بطن دجاجة، وإن كان يقال في العرف أنه معدوم لم يجز كبيع حب قطن فيه ونوى تمر فيه، ولبن في ضرع، ولحم وشحم وألية في شاة وأكارع وجلد فيها، ودقيق في حنطة، وزيت في زيتون وعصير في عنب ومحلوج قطن فيه، ولؤلؤة في صدف على المفتى به وتبن سنبلها
And 'al-baqila' is beans, and 'al-haleej' is ginned cotton, meaning that whose seeds have been removed from its cotton. And in al-Bazaziyyah: If he sells wheat in its ear, the seller must thresh and winnow. Likewise if he gives him wheat in its ear unconditionally. So the summary of what we have transmitted is that when he sells a covered item, if it is covered by something that is innate in it first, and the second is purchasing what he has not seen is valid according to us. And the first is not devoid of either the sold item being existent in custom or non-existent. If it is existent, it is valid, like selling wheat in its ear, rice, walnuts, almonds, the stomach of a slaughtered sheep before its flaying, and a pearl in the belly of a chicken. And if it is said in custom that it is non-existent, it is not valid, like selling cotton seed in it, date pits in dates, milk in the udder, meat and fat and tail in a sheep, trotters and skin in it, flour in wheat, oil in olives and juice in grapes, ginned cotton in it, and a pearl in an oyster shell according to the mufta bihi, and straw in its ear.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of sales where the object is concealed (mastur) by a natural covering. First, it defines terms: al-baqila (beans), al-haleej (ginned cotton). Then from al-Bazaziyyah, it notes that if one sells wheat in the ear, the seller must thresh and winnow. The summary distills the rule into two categories: (1) If the covering is innate (khilqi) to the item, like the shell of a walnut, the sale is valid; (2) If the covering is not innate? Actually the text says 'first and second' but seems to present one rule. The key dichotomy is whether the item is considered existent in custom (mawjud fil-'urf) or non-existent. Examples of existent: wheat in ear, rice, walnuts, almonds, stomach of a slaughtered sheep, pearl in chicken. Examples of non-existent: cotton seed in pod, date pits in dates, milk in udder, meat, fat, tail, trotters, skin in sheep, flour in wheat, oil in olives, juice in grapes, ginned cotton in its pod, pearl in oyster shell (according to the fatwa), and straw in the ear. The classification is based on common usage: if people normally refer to the item as distinct, it is existent; otherwise not.

Key Terms
مستور (mastur): An item covered or concealed by its natural casing, such as wheat in ear, milk in udder.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The validity of selling a naturally covered item depends on whether people customarily treat it as a distinct object.

Delivery Costs: Who Bears What in Sales
Ruling on who bears the cost of measuring, weighing, and counting in sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on who bears the cost of measuring, weighing, and counting in a sale and apply it to different scenarios including jizaf sales.

Core Idea
The seller delivers the goods; the buyer delivers the price—costs follow delivery.

When you buy rice by weight, who pays for the weighing? The Hanafi school has a clear rule: the seller bears all costs of delivering the sold item, including measuring, weighing, and counting. Conversely, the buyer bears the costs of delivering the price. This lesson unpacks that principle and its applications, including special cases like jizaf (sale by estimation) and the role of custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The passage addresses the allocation of costs for measuring, weighing, counting, and related actions in a sale. These costs are part of the obligations of the parties.
The issue is part of the buyer's and seller's duties beyond just transferring ownership.
2
Apply the general principle
The seller must deliver the sold item completely; the buyer must deliver the price. Costs that are part of completing delivery (tatmim al-taslim) fall on the party obliged to deliver. Thus, the seller bears costs of measuring, weighing, counting, cubing, etc., while the buyer bears costs of weighing and paying the price.
This principle is derived from the fact that delivery (taslim) is upon the seller, and payment (taslim al-thaman) is upon the buyer.
3
Analyze special cases
In a jizaf (estimation) sale, the seller's duty ends at making the goods accessible; the buyer cuts or harvests at his own expense. For goods in containers (e.g., clothes in a bag), opening the container is on the seller, taking out on the buyer. For wheat in ear, the seller must thresh and winnow.
The text also mentions that for firewood in the city, carrying is on the seller, and for water from a water carrier, pouring is on the carrier.
4
Consider custom
The text emphasizes that local custom ('urf) is the ultimate reference. The default rules are followed unless custom dictates otherwise. For example, if it is customary for the buyer to bear measuring costs, that prevails.
Custom can override the default allocation as it is a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school.
5
Synthesize the ruling
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that measuring and pouring are on the seller (from al-Khulasah), and the cost of weighing the price is on the buyer. This is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwayah) from the Sahibayn.
The text notes that al-Sadr al-Shahid used to give fatwa on this, and it is the correct position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وأجرة الكيل على البائع) يعني إذا بيع مكايلة وكذا أجرة الوزان والعداد عليه والذراع لأنه من تمام التسليم وتسليم المبيع عليه فكذا ما كان تمامه . قيد بالكيل لأن صب من في الوعاء على المشتري وكذا إخراج الطعام من السفينة وكذا قطع العنب المشتري جزافاً عليه، وكذا كل شيء باعه جزافاً كالثوم والبصل والجزر إذا خلي بينها وبين المشترى، وكذا قطع الثمر إذا خلي بينها وبين المشتري؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وأشار إلى أنه لو اشترى حنطة في سنبلها فعلى البائع تخليصها بالدرس والتذرية ودفعها إلى المشتري وهو المختار. وفي المعراج: والتبن للبائع وإذا اشترى ثياباً في جراب ففتح الجراب على البائع وإخراج الثياب على المشتري. وقيل كما يجب الكيل على البائع فالصب في وعاء المشتري يكون عليه أيضاً. وكذا لو اشترى ماء سقاء من في قربة كان صب الماء على السقاء والمعتبر في هذا العرف؛ كذا في الخانية. وفي المجتبى: لو اشترى وقر حطب في المصر فالحمل على البائع قوله: (وأجرة نقد الثمن ووزنه على المشتري) لما ذكرنا أن الوزن من تمام التسليم وتسليم الثمن على المشتري فكذا ما يكون من تمامه وكذا يجب عليه تسليم الجيد لأن حق البائع تعلق به، وما ذكره المصنف في نقد الثمن هو الصحيح كما في الخلاصة وهو ظاهر الرواية كما في الخانية وبه كان يفتي الصدر الشهيد قال: وبه يفتى إلا إذا قبض البائع الثمن ثم جاء يرده بعيب الزيافة قوله: (وفي البزازية لو باع حنطة في سنبلها الخ الظاهر أن المراد باع الحنطة بعينها وما في المتن في بيعها مع السنبل لا بعينها تأمل قوله كذا في الخلاصة قال الرملي الذي في الخلاصة: لو اشترى حنطة مكايلة فالكيل على البائع وصبها في وعاء المشتري على البائع أيضاً هو المختار اهـ؛ كذا رأيت بخط شيخ الإسلام محمد الغزي رحمه الله تعالى اهـ.
His saying: (And the fee for measuring is on the seller) meaning when sold by measure, and likewise the fee for weighing and counting is on him, and the cubit, because it is part of complete delivery, and the delivery of the sold item is upon him, so likewise what completes it. He restricted to measurement because pouring from the vessel is on the buyer, and similarly taking out food from the ship, and similarly cutting grapes for the buyer by estimation is on him, and likewise everything he sells by estimation like garlic, onions, carrots when he leaves them between him and the buyer, and likewise cutting fruit when he leaves it between him and the buyer; thus in al-Khulasah. And he indicated that if he buys wheat in its ear, the seller must extract it by threshing and winnowing and hand it over to the buyer, and that is the chosen position. And in al-Mi'raj: And the straw belongs to the seller. And if he buys clothes in a bag, opening the bag is on the seller and taking out the clothes is on the buyer. And it was said: just as measuring is obligatory on the seller, so pouring into the buyer's vessel is also on him. Likewise if he buys water from a water carrier in a water skin, pouring the water is on the water carrier. And the consideration in this is custom; thus in al-Khaniyah. And in al-Mujtaba: If he buys a load of firewood in the city, carrying is on the seller. His saying: (And the fee for paying and weighing the price is on the buyer) because we have mentioned that weighing is part of complete delivery, and delivery of the price is on the buyer, so likewise what completes it. And likewise he must deliver good quality because the seller's right is attached to it. And what the author mentioned regarding payment of the price is correct as in al-Khulasah, and it is the apparent transmission as in al-Khaniyah, and al-Sadr al-Shahid used to give fatwa on it. He said: and by it fatwa is given, except if the seller has taken the price and then comes to return it due to a defect of bad coin. His saying: (And in al-Bazaziyyah: if he sells wheat in its ear, etc.) Apparently what is meant is that he sold the wheat itself, and what is in the text about selling it with the ear not itself, reflect. His saying: thus in al-Khulasah. Al-Ramli said: What is in al-Khulasah: If he buys wheat by measure, measuring is on the seller and pouring it into the buyer's vessel is also on the seller; that is the chosen position. Thus I saw in the handwriting of Shaykh al-Islam Muhammad al-Ghazzi, may Allah have mercy on him.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage covers the costs associated with delivering the sold item. The general rule: costs of measuring (kayl), weighing (wazn), counting (idad) and measuring length (dhira') are on the seller because these are part of the delivery (taslim) of the sold item. However, the cost of pouring from the seller's container into the buyer's vessel is on the buyer? Actually the text says 'صب من في الوعاء على المشتري' but then later says pouring is on the seller if it is part of measurement? There is some nuance. The author restricts to measurement: pouring into the buyer's vessel is on the buyer if the seller measured by pouring into buyer's container? Actually it says 'قيد بالكيل لأن صب من في الوعاء على المشتري' – meaning the restriction to measurement (that the seller bears the cost of measuring) is because the pouring from the vessel during measurement? The text seems to differentiate: if the sale is by weight or count, the seller pays; but if the sale is by estimation (jizaf), then cutting or harvesting is on the buyer? Wait: 'وكذا قطع العنب المشتري جزافاً عليه' – cutting grapes for the buyer in a jizaf sale is on the buyer. The rule is: In a jizaf sale, the seller must leave the item to the buyer, and the buyer cuts or harvests at his own expense. If the sale is by measure (mukayalah), the seller must measure and also pour? There is a mention that pouring into buyer's vessel is on the buyer, but later a quote from al-Khulasah says it is on the seller. The commentary by Ramli clarifies that the chosen opinion is that both measuring and pouring are on the seller. Then the passage discusses the cost of weighing and paying the price: that is on the buyer, because the buyer must deliver the price. The seller's right is to receive the price, so the buyer must pay and weigh the money. However, if the seller later wants to return the money due to bad coins (ziyafah), that is a different matter. Finally, a note on the Bazaziyyah quote: it clarifies that when selling wheat in its ear, the seller must thresh and winnow, and the straw belongs to the seller. Also, selling wheat with the ear is different from selling wheat alone. The passage ends with a note from Ramli quoting al-Khulasah that measuring and pouring are on the seller.

Key Terms
مكايلة (mukayalah): Sale by measure (kayl). The seller must measure the goods.
جزافاً (jizafan): Sale by estimation without exact measure or weight. The seller's duty is limited to making the goods accessible.
نقد الثمن (naqd al-thaman): Payment of the price, including counting and weighing of money. The buyer bears its cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The seller, because it is part of delivery.
B) The buyer, because he must deliver the price.
C) Both equally.
D) It depends on custom.

Key Takeaway
The seller bears the cost of delivering the goods (measuring, weighing, counting), while the buyer bears the cost of delivering the price (weighing and paying).

The Ruling on Selling a Pearl in Its Shell: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the sale of a pearl in a shell.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on selling a pearl in a shell, explain the disagreement between Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A pearl in a shell is considered non-existent by custom; thus its sale is invalid (mufta bihi).

When an item is concealed in its natural covering, the ruling depends on whether custom deems it existent. For a pearl in a shell, Abu Yusuf permits the sale with an option to inspect, while Muhammad declares it void. This lesson examines their ikhtilaf and the final relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the specific case
The case is a pearl bought while still inside its natural shell (صدف).
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf holds that the sale is permissible, with the buyer having khiyar al-ru'yah (option to rescind after seeing).
3
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad holds that the sale is not permissible (la yajuz), i.e., batil.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, the contract is initially valid but the buyer can cancel; under Muhammad, the contract is void from inception.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'وعليه الفتوى' (upon it is the fatwa), so the operative ruling is Muhammad's view: the sale is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فباع حبة اللؤلؤة التي في بطنها جاز ولا خيار للمشتري إن كان رآها إلا إذا تغيرت، وإن لم يكن المشتري رأى اللؤلؤة فله الخيار إذا رآها ولو اشترى لؤلؤة في صدف قال أبو يوسف : يجوز البيع وله الخيار إذا رأى. وقال محمد لا يجوز وعليه الفتوى، والباقلا الفول والحليج بمعنى المحلوج وهو ما خلص حبه من قطنه. وفي البزازية : لو باع حنطة في سنبلها لزم البائع الدوس والتذرية، وكذا لو أطلق له حنطة في سنبلها فصار حاصل ما نقلناه أنه إذا باع شيئاً مستوراً، فإن كان مستوراً بما هو خلقي فيه أولاً والثاني شراء ما لم يره جائز عندنا . والأول لا يخلو إما أن يكون المبيع موجوداً في العرف أو معدوماً، فإن كان موجوداً جاز كبيع في سنبلها وأرز وجوز ولوز وكرش شاة مذبوحة قبل سلخها ولؤلؤة في بطن دجاجة، وإن كان يقال في العرف أنه معدوم لم يجز كبيع حب قطن فيه ونوى تمر فيه، ولبن في ضرع، ولحم وشحم وألية في شاة وأكارع وجلد فيها، ودقيق في حنطة، وزيت في حنطة الزيتون وعصير في عنب ومحلوج قطن فيه، ولؤلؤة في صدف على المفتى به وتبن سنبلها
Then a person sold a pearl in its oyster, it is permissible, and the buyer has no option if he had seen it, unless it has changed. And if the buyer had not seen the pearl, then he has the option when he sees it. And if a person bought a pearl in a shell, Abu Yusuf said: The sale is permissible and he has the option when he sees it. Muhammad said: It is not permissible, and the fatwa is upon it. And 'baqila' is 'fool' (beans), and 'haleej' is the same as 'mahlooj', meaning that its seeds have been separated from its cotton. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If a person sold wheat in its ears, it is incumbent upon the seller to thresh and winnow it. Similarly, if he sold him wheat in its ears, so the conclusion of what we have transmitted is that if he sold a concealed thing, then if it is concealed by something that is innate to it, then the first and second [types]: the purchase of what he has not seen is permissible according to us. And the first is not free from either being existent in custom or non-existent. If it is existent, it is permissible, like selling wheat in its ears, rice, walnuts, almonds, and the stomach of a slaughtered sheep before skinning it, and a pearl in the belly of a chicken. And if it is said in custom that it is non-existent, it is not permissible, like selling the seeds of cotton in it, and dates' pits in them, milk in the udder, meat and fat and tail (fat of sheep) in a sheep, and trotters and skin in it, flour in wheat, oil in olive in grain, grape juice in grapes, carded cotton in it, and a pearl in a shell according to the mufta bihi, and straw in its ears.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling items that are concealed within something else, particularly when the concealment is natural (خلقي) like grain in its husk or a pearl in an oyster. The author explains the general rule and then presents a dispute between two early Hanafi imams. The text begins with the case of a pearl in its oyster: the sale is valid, and the buyer's option (خيار الرؤية) depends on whether they saw the pearl before purchase. If they saw it, no option unless the condition changed. If they didn't see it, they have the option upon seeing it. The discussion then moves to a pearl in a shell (الصدف): Abu Yusuf permits the sale with the option, while Imam Muhammad declares it impermissible, and the fatwa is according to Muhammad. The author then defines 'baqila' (beans) and 'haleej' (carded cotton). From al-Bazzaziyyah, if someone sells wheat in its ears, the seller must thresh and winnow it. The general principle is established: when selling a concealed item, two categories exist: (1) the item is concealed by something innate (e.g., wheat in ear, nut in shell), and (2) purchase of an unseen item (which is allowed in Hanafi school). For the first category, the ruling hinges on custom (عرف): if the item is considered existent in custom, the sale is valid (e.g., wheat in ear, rice, walnuts, almonds, sheep's stomach before skinning, pearl in chicken's belly). If it is considered non-existent in custom, the sale is invalid (e.g., cotton seed in cotton, date pit in date, milk in udder, meat/fat in sheep, flour in wheat, oil in olives, juice in grapes, carded cotton, pearl in shell according to the mufta bihi, and straw in its ears). The reliance on custom is a distinctive Hanafi approach, grounding legal rulings in prevailing practice.

Key Terms
مستور (mastūr): A item that is concealed or covered, whether by a natural covering (e.g., shell, husk) or a container.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’yah): The buyer's option to rescind a sale after viewing the item if they had not seen it at the time of contract.
عرف (‘urf): Custom; common practice among people that is accepted as a basis for legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the mufta bihi (relied upon) ruling regarding the sale of a pearl in a shell (الصدف) according to this passage?
A) The sale is valid with the buyer having an option upon seeing the pearl.
B) The sale is invalid (batil) and cannot be executed.
C) The sale is valid only if the buyer has seen the pearl before the contract.
D) The sale is temporarily suspended until the pearl is removed.

Key Takeaway
A sale is invalid if custom treats the item as non-existent at the time of contract, even if it will become apparent later.

Custom as the Legal Basis for Sales of Concealed Items (Mastūr)
General rule and examples for sales of concealed items.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a sale of a concealed item as valid or invalid based on whether custom considers the item existent, using the Hanafi examples.

Core Idea
A sale of a concealed item is valid if custom treats it as existent; it is void if custom treats it as non-existent.

The Hanafi school sets a clear criterion for sales of items hidden in natural coverings: the ruling follows the custom of traders. If custom considers the item as existent despite concealment, the sale is permissible. Otherwise, it is void. This lesson applies this rule to a wide range of examples.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between two types of concealed sales
First: item concealed by something innate (e.g., wheat in ear). Second: purchase of an unseen item (permissible generally). Focus on the first.
2
Apply the custom test
Ask: Does custom (urf) consider this concealed item as existent? If yes, sale valid; if no, sale invalid.
3
Identify examples of valid sales
Review the list: wheat in ear, rice, walnuts, almonds, sheep stomach before skinning, pearl in chicken belly (not shell).
4
Identify examples of invalid sales
Review the list: cotton seeds in cotton, date pits in dates, milk in udder, meat in live sheep, flour in wheat, etc.
5
Recognize the mufta bihi exception
Pearl in shell is ruled invalid despite some considering it existent; this is the fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فباع حبة اللؤلؤة التي في بطنها جاز ولا خيار للمشتري إن كان رآها إلا إذا تغيرت، وإن لم يكن المشتري رأى اللؤلؤة فله الخيار إذا رآها ولو اشترى لؤلؤة في صدف قال أبو يوسف : يجوز البيع وله الخيار إذا رأى. وقال محمد لا يجوز وعليه الفتوى، والباقلا الفول والحليج بمعنى المحلوج وهو ما خلص حبه من قطنه. وفي البزازية : لو باع حنطة في سنبلها لزم البائع الدوس والتذرية، وكذا لو أطلق له حنطة في سنبلها فصار حاصل ما نقلناه أنه إذا باع شيئاً مستوراً، فإن كان مستوراً بما هو خلقي فيه أولاً والثاني شراء ما لم يره جائز عندنا . والأول لا يخلو إما أن يكون المبيع موجوداً في العرف أو معدوماً، فإن كان موجوداً جاز كبيع في سنبلها وأرز وجوز ولوز وكرش شاة مذبوحة قبل سلخها ولؤلؤة في بطن دجاجة، وإن كان يقال في العرف أنه معدوم لم يجز كبيع حب قطن فيه ونوى تمر فيه، ولبن في ضرع، ولحم وشحم وألية في شاة وأكارع وجلد فيها، ودقيق في حنطة، وزيت في حنطة الزيتون وعصير في عنب ومحلوج قطن فيه، ولؤلؤة في صدف على المفتى به وتبن سنبلها
Then a person sold a pearl in its oyster, it is permissible, and the buyer has no option if he had seen it, unless it has changed. And if the buyer had not seen the pearl, then he has the option when he sees it. And if a person bought a pearl in a shell, Abu Yusuf said: The sale is permissible and he has the option when he sees it. Muhammad said: It is not permissible, and the fatwa is upon it. And 'baqila' is 'fool' (beans), and 'haleej' is the same as 'mahlooj', meaning that its seeds have been separated from its cotton. And in al-Bazzaziyyah: If a person sold wheat in its ears, it is incumbent upon the seller to thresh and winnow it. Similarly, if he sold him wheat in its ears, so the conclusion of what we have transmitted is that if he sold a concealed thing, then if it is concealed by something that is innate to it, then the first and second [types]: the purchase of what he has not seen is permissible according to us. And the first is not free from either being existent in custom or non-existent. If it is existent, it is permissible, like selling wheat in its ears, rice, walnuts, almonds, and the stomach of a slaughtered sheep before skinning it, and a pearl in the belly of a chicken. And if it is said in custom that it is non-existent, it is not permissible, like selling the seeds of cotton in it, and dates' pits in them, milk in the udder, meat and fat and tail (fat of sheep) in a sheep, and trotters and skin in it, flour in wheat, oil in olive in grain, grape juice in grapes, carded cotton in it, and a pearl in a shell according to the mufta bihi, and straw in its ears.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on selling items that are concealed within something else, particularly when the concealment is natural (خلقي) like grain in its husk or a pearl in an oyster. The author explains the general rule and then presents a dispute between two early Hanafi imams. The text begins with the case of a pearl in its oyster: the sale is valid, and the buyer's option (خيار الرؤية) depends on whether they saw the pearl before purchase. If they saw it, no option unless the condition changed. If they didn't see it, they have the option upon seeing it. The discussion then moves to a pearl in a shell (الصدف): Abu Yusuf permits the sale with the option, while Imam Muhammad declares it impermissible, and the fatwa is according to Muhammad. The author then defines 'baqila' (beans) and 'haleej' (carded cotton). From al-Bazzaziyyah, if someone sells wheat in its ears, the seller must thresh and winnow it. The general principle is established: when selling a concealed item, two categories exist: (1) the item is concealed by something innate (e.g., wheat in ear, nut in shell), and (2) purchase of an unseen item (which is allowed in Hanafi school). For the first category, the ruling hinges on custom (عرف): if the item is considered existent in custom, the sale is valid (e.g., wheat in ear, rice, walnuts, almonds, sheep's stomach before skinning, pearl in chicken's belly). If it is considered non-existent in custom, the sale is invalid (e.g., cotton seed in cotton, date pit in date, milk in udder, meat/fat in sheep, flour in wheat, oil in olives, juice in grapes, carded cotton, pearl in shell according to the mufta bihi, and straw in its ears). The reliance on custom is a distinctive Hanafi approach, grounding legal rulings in prevailing practice.

Key Terms
مستور (mastūr): A item that is concealed or covered, whether by a natural covering (e.g., shell, husk) or a container.
خيار الرؤية (khiyār al-ru’yah): The buyer's option to rescind a sale after viewing the item if they had not seen it at the time of contract.
عرف (‘urf): Custom; common practice among people that is accepted as a basis for legal rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following sales is considered invalid because custom treats the item as non-existent?
A) Selling wheat in its ears.
B) Selling a pearl in the belly of a chicken.
C) Selling milk in the udder of a cow.
D) Selling almonds in their shells.

Key Takeaway
Custom is the decisive criterion for the validity of sales of concealed items; only items that traders treat as existent can be sold before separation.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
6 min
Cost Allocation in Sale: Delivery vs. Payment
Allocation of costs for measuring, weighing, and paying in a sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on allocation of delivery and payment costs in a sale and apply it to common scenarios.

Core Idea
The seller pays to deliver the goods; the buyer pays to deliver the price.

When you buy or sell goods, who pays for measuring, weighing, and checking the money? The Hanafi school provides a clear default allocation based on the principle that each party bears the cost of completing their own obligation. This lesson unpacks the ruling from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq, covering specific cases and the role of custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Default Rule
The text states: the fee for measuring (kayl), weighing (wazn), counting (‘add), and cubit-measuring (dhirā‘) is on the seller. The fee for paying (naqd) and weighing the price is on the buyer. Understand this as the starting point.
The key principle: 'it is part of complete delivery' (min tamām al-taslīm).
2
Analyze Specific Cases
The passage lists several applications: pouring (sabb) from the seller's container is normally on the buyer; removing goods from a ship or cutting lump-sum (jizāf) produce like grapes or onions is on the buyer; but for wheat in ears, the seller must thresh and winnow. For clothes in a bag, opening the bag is on the seller, taking out clothes on the buyer.
Custom (‘urf) is the final determinant in these matters.
3
Apply the Payment Rule
The buyer must also deliver good quality coins. The fee for checking and weighing the price is on the buyer. This is the relied-upon view (mufta bihi). An exception: if the seller accepted the price and later tries to return it due to a defect (e.g., debased coins), the expense reverts to the seller.
This exception prevents the seller from shifting the cost after acceptance.
4
Recognize the Role of Custom
The text emphasizes that custom (‘urf) governs these allocations. If local practice differs, it may override the default ruling.
Custom is a recognized source of law in Hanafi fiqh for transactional matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأجرة الكيل على البائع) يعني إذا بيع مكايلة وكذا أجرة الوزان والعداد عليه والذراع لأنه من تمام التسليم وتسليم المبيع عليه فكذا ما كان تمامه . قيد بالكيل لأن صب من في الوعاء على المشتري وكذا إخراج الطعام من السفينة وكذا قطع العنب المشتري جزافاً عليه، وكذا كل شيء باعه جزافاً كالثوم والبصل والجزر إذا خلي بينها وبين المشترى، وكذا قطع الثمر إذا خلي بينها وبين المشتري؛ كذا في الخلاصة. وأشار إلى أنه لو اشترى حنطة في سنبلها فعلى البائع تخليصها بالدرس والتذرية ودفعها إلى المشتري وهو المختار. وفي المعراج : والتبن للبائع وإذا اشترى ثياباً في جراب ففتح الجراب على البائع وإخراج الثياب على المشتري. وقيل كما يجب الكيل على البائع فالصب في وعاء المشتري يكون عليه أيضاً. وكذا لو اشترى ماء سقاء من في قربة كان صب الماء على السقاء والمعتبر في هذا العرف؛ كذا في الخانية. وفي المجتبى : لو اشترى وقر حطب في المصر فالحمل على البائع قوله : (وأجرة نقد الثمن ووزنه على المشتري لما ذكرنا أن الوزن من تمام التسليم وتسليم الثمن على المشتري فكذا ما يكون من ،تمامه وكذا يجب عليه تسليم الجيد لأن حق البائع تعلق به، وما ذكره المصنف في نقد الثمن هو الصحيح كما في الخلاصة وهو ظاهر الرواية كما في الخانية وبه كان يفتي الصدر الشهيد قال : وبه يفتى إلا إذا قبض البائع الثمن ثم جاء يرده بعيب الزيافة قوله : (وفي البزازية لو باع حنطة في سنبلها الخ الظاهر أن المراد باع الحنطة بعينها وما في المتن في بيعها مع السنبل لا بعينها تأمل قوله كذا في الخلاصة قال الرملي الذي في الخلاصة: لو اشترى حنطة مكايلة فالكيل على البائع وصبها في وعاء المشتري على البائع أيضاً هو المختار اهـ؛ كذا رأيت بخط شيخ الإسلام محمد الغزي رحمه الله تعالى اهـ.
His saying: (And the fee for measuring is upon the seller) meaning when it is sold by measure, and similarly the fee for the weigher and the counter is upon him, and the fee for the cubit measurer, because it is part of complete delivery, and delivery of the sold item is upon him, so likewise whatever is part of its completion. He restricted it to measuring because pouring from the container is upon the buyer, and similarly removing the food from the ship is upon him, and similarly cutting the grapes sold by lump sum (jizāf) is upon him, and similarly everything sold by lump sum like garlic, onion, and carrots when the buyer is given free access to them, and similarly cutting the fruit when the buyer is given free access to it; thus in al-Khulāṣah. And he indicated that if he bought wheat in its ears, then upon the seller is to free it by threshing and winnowing and give it to the buyer, and that is the chosen opinion. And in al-Mi‘rāj: And the straw is for the seller, and if he bought clothes in a bag, opening the bag is upon the seller, and taking out the clothes is upon the buyer. And it is said: just as measuring is incumbent upon the seller, so pouring into the buyer’s container is also upon him. And similarly, if he bought the water of a water carrier from a water skin, pouring the water is upon the water carrier, and the consideration in this is custom; thus in al-Khāniyyah. And in al-Mujtabā: If he bought a load of firewood in the city, then carrying it is upon the seller. His saying: (And the fee for paying the price and weighing it is upon the buyer because we mentioned that weighing is part of complete delivery, and delivery of the price is upon the buyer, so likewise whatever is part of its completion. And similarly, he must deliver the good quality because the seller’s right is attached to it, and what the author mentioned regarding paying the price is correct as in al-Khulāṣah, and it is the apparent narration as in al-Khāniyyah, and thus the Chief Martyr used to give fatwa. He said: and fatwa is given upon it, except if the seller received the price then came to return it due to a defect of being debased. His saying: (And in al-Bazzāziyyah, if he sold wheat in its ears, etc.) The apparent is that he meant selling the wheat itself, and what is in the text about selling it with the ear without specifying the wheat itself, consider. His saying: thus in al-Khulāṣah, al-Ramlī said: What is in al-Khulāṣah: if he bought wheat by measure, then measuring is upon the seller and pouring it into the buyer’s container is upon the seller as well, that is the chosen opinion, thus I saw in the handwriting of Shaykh al-Islām Muḥammad al-Ghazzī, may God have mercy on him, end.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqā’iq deals with who bears the expenses (ujrah) associated with delivery of the goods and payment of the price in a sale. The author explains that the cost of measuring (kayl), weighing (wazn), counting (‘add), and measuring by cubit (dhirā‘) is upon the seller, because these acts are part of complete delivery (tamām al-taslīm) of the sold item. The seller is obligated to deliver the item fully. However, pouring (sabb) from the seller’s container into the buyer’s container is upon the buyer, as is removing goods from a ship or cutting grapes or fruit in a lump-sum sale when the buyer is given access. For wheat sold in ears, the seller must thresh and winnow it. Straw belongs to the seller if not included. For clothes in a bag, opening the bag is on the seller, taking out clothes on the buyer. Custom (‘urf) is the determinant in these matters. The text then turns to the fee for paying the price (nagd al-thaman) and weighing it: this is upon the buyer, because weighing the price is part of completing delivery of the price, which is the buyer’s duty. The buyer must also deliver good quality coins. The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that the fee for paying the price is on the buyer, except if the seller had already received the price and later tries to return it due to a defect (e.g., debased coins), in which case the expense falls back on the seller. The commentator also cites al-Ramlī’s verification that for wheat bought by measure, both measuring and pouring into the buyer’s container are on the seller. The passage emphasises that these rulings are based on custom and the principles of delivery.

Key Terms
كيل (kayl): The act of measuring dry goods by volume using a standard measure.
جزاف (jizāf): A sale by lump sum or estimation without exact measurement or weighing.
نقد (naqd): The act of paying the price in cash, including checking coins for quality and genuineness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who bears the fee for weighing the price (nagd) in a normal sale?
A) The seller, because delivery of the price is part of complete sale.
B) The buyer, because delivering the price is his duty.
C) It is shared equally between seller and buyer.
D) It depends on local custom exclusively.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, costs of measuring and delivering the goods are on the seller; costs of paying and weighing the price are on the buyer, subject to custom and exceptions.
